Discussion Name: Should the UK Remain in the EU if the only Alternative is a Hard Brexit?

1. The UK should remain in the EU if a hard Brexit is the only alternative option.
1.1. Pro: A hard Brexit would end Freedom of Movement, and all the associated benefits it brings.
1.1.1. Pro: Without a deal or other residency rights, the entitlement of UK nationals to reside elsewhere in the EU could technically disappear overnight.
1.1.1.1. Con: If both the UK and individual EU states value the relationship they currently have with each other, they can form bilateral agreements to preserve the conditions required to ensure ease of travel, work and education.
1.1.1.1.1. Con: For individual EU countries to grant the UK the same full open borders as before, the UK would have to reciprocate by opening their own borders too. This is not politically supportable for the UK, as closing down immigration was a major goal of Brexit - there won't be public desire or the political mandate to start re-opening borders.
1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Public support may exist to open borders with countries more ethnically close to us, like France and Germany, but keep eastern Europeans out.
1.1.1.1.2. Pro: An increasing number of EU countries \(most recently [Portugal](https://eutoday.net/news/politics/2019/portugal-to-provide-special-lanes-for-uk-visitors-after-brexit)\) have made unilateral pledges to maintain visa free travel post-Brexit. The countries most likely to carry on this trend are the countries which benefit a great deal from UK tourism; i.e. the countries UK citizens would be most concerned about unrestricted travel to.
1.1.1.1.3. Pro: The UK can make bilateral arrangements to retain rights as it sees fit. For example, Switzerland and the UK have reached a [bilateral agreement](https://www.thelocal.ch/20181217/switzerland-signs-off-on-post-brexit-trade-agreement-with-uk) that retains trade and residency rights, and the UK and Spain have made [similar bilateral arrangements](https://www.ft.com/content/171ddbb0-1d96-11e9-b2f7-97e4dbd3580d),
1.1.1.1.4. Pro: Spain has guaranteed British citizens can continue [to live there](http://www.cityam.com/274318/brexit-british-expats-spain-allowed-stay-even-if-no-deal) even in the event of a no-deal Brexit.
1.1.1.1.4.1. Con: Spain is only one of the roughly 27 other member states of the EU. Most other EU states will require visas from British people and have to comply with that EU legislation. Free movement is for EU people only and if the UK leaves they will forfeit that right for the majority of member states.
1.1.1.2. Con: Spain, which has by far the largest number of UK citizens, has [bilaterally agreed](https://www.ft.com/content/171ddbb0-1d96-11e9-b2f7-97e4dbd3580d) [citizens rights](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-europe-47412708) with the UK.
1.1.1.3. Pro: A hard Brexit would also harm the ability of Britons to settle in the EU in the future.
1.1.1.4. Pro: British tourists looking to travel Europe would most likely need [visas and security screenings](https://www.express.co.uk/travel/articles/904036/post-brexit-travel-passport-visa-europe-eurozone). This makes travel more costly and more difficult.
1.1.1.4.1. Con: Those visiting the UK from the EU would also have to obtain visas, making it easier to keep an accurate account of who is legally in the UK.
1.1.1.4.1.1. Con: While that may be positive for immigration, it could have a negative impact on the tourism industry in the UK.
1.1.1.4.1.1.1. Pro: [Tourism](https://www.visitbritain.org/visitor-economy-facts) in the UK generates 9% of the country's GDP and nearly 10% of jobs. A decline in tourists due to Brexit would damage the UK economy.
1.1.1.4.1.1.2. Pro: Currently, the UK's [airline routes](http://uk.businessinsider.com/wef-tourism-competitiveness-report-brexit-impact-on-uk-tourism-2017-4) to EU and US destinations are arranged through EU agreements; a hard Brexit could call these into question.
1.1.1.4.1.1.2.1. Pro: As an island nation, transport links are highly important.
1.1.1.4.1.1.2.2. Pro: The aviation sector does not have the emergency fall-back option of World Trade Organisation rules which apply to most sectors. As a result, Brexit could [severely reduce](http://uk.businessinsider.com/wef-tourism-competitiveness-report-brexit-impact-on-uk-tourism-2017-4) the number of routes for holidaymakers, business travellers and visitors to the UK.
1.1.1.4.1.1.3. Pro: The additional work of procuring a visa may deter people from using the UK as a holiday destination.
1.1.1.4.2. Con: British people spending money abroad through tourism is money leaving the British economy. It is better to have them vacation domestically.
1.1.1.4.3. Con: It is likely that the visa scheme will resemble [the U.S. process](https://www.express.co.uk/travel/articles/904036/post-brexit-travel-passport-visa-europe-eurozone) which guarantees 90 days upon entry. While this is more difficult than the status quo, [millions](https://www.express.co.uk/travel/articles/564537/4-million-Brits-travel-US-in-2014-record-breaking-tourism-year-Ireland-overseas-visitors) of tourists still travel from Britain to the U.S. each year.
1.1.2. Con: [Brits don't particularly want to live in the EU](http://www.dailymail.co.uk/news/article-2690347/Revealed-Where-5million-Brits-live-abroad-1-3million-Australia-four-emigrated-San-Marino.html). None of the top 3 countries that Brits emigrate to are in the EU \(Australia, US, and Canada\).
1.1.2.1. Con: [1.3 million people](http://www.un.org/en/development/desa/population/migration/data/estimates2/estimates17.shtml) born in the UK live in other EU countries, according to 2017 estimates from the United Nations \(UN\).
1.1.2.1.1. Con: This is still less than the number of Brits living in Australia alone and reinforces rather than negates the parent claim. Given the EU27 comprises 27 countries, 450 million people and 15% of global GDP, while Australia had a population and GDP about one 20th the size, this illustrates the vast preference of Brits to live in non-EU countries, which preference is greatly increased even further when retirees are discounted.
1.1.2.2. Con: This may partly be because [the most popular holidays destinations are in the EU](https://www.kialo.com/should-the-uk-remain-in-the-eu-if-the-only-alternative-is-a-hard-brexit-8708/8708.0=8708.1+8708.1011+8708.1173), and people emigrating want to take the chance to live and work somewhere new that they couldn't visit easily otherwise.
1.1.2.2.1. Con: The qualities one looks for in a holiday destination are different to those one looks for in a place to settle permanently. That people are familiar with the EU but don't want to live there suggests simply that they don't want to live there. Language and cultural barriers are far more likely reasons for Brits choosing not to live in the EU, with the exception of retirees - who are a special case of immigrant living a lifestyle more similar to that of a holidaymaker.
1.1.2.2.2. Con: Given EU countries represent the closest and cheapest travel destinations, this statistic is hardly surprising.
1.1.2.2.3. Con: America is very popular as a tourist destination \(no. 5\) but more than twice as popular for migration as the most popular EU migration destination \(Spain\).
1.1.2.3. Con: Around 900,000 UK citizens are long-term residents of other EU countries \([ONS, 2010 2011](https://fullfact.org/europe/how-many-uk-citizens-live-other-eu-countries/)\).
1.1.2.3.1. Con: [Over 250k](https://www.ons.gov.uk/economy/investmentspensionsandtrusts/articles/pensionersintheeuanduk/2017-09-05) of these are pensioners, who are effectively on a semi-permanent holiday.  Therefore the number of UK citizens working in the EU is at most just 650k.
1.1.2.3.2. Con: This is a relatively small number considering there are over [1.3 million](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_diaspora) British residents in Australia alone.
1.1.2.3.3. Con: These are not distributed evenly across the EU, and therefore the UK would be better served by dealing with popular destinations on a bilateral basis.
1.1.2.3.3.1. Con: You can’t negotiate that with part of the EU with any real success, especially in the Schengen zone. You may want to read the basis of negotiations in this briefing. [researchbriefings.files.parliament.uk](http://researchbriefings.files.parliament.uk/documents/LLN-2016-0013/LLN-2016-0013.pdf)
1.1.2.4. Con: Freedom of Movement is not the same as Immigration. By definition it is the ability to move about and work anywhere within the EU.
1.1.3. Pro: Many students studying in the UK will have less exposure to employers outside of the UK, as well as lower opportunities for studying abroad.
1.1.3.1. Con: If there are fewer EU people working in the UK, then there may be more opportunities for students studying there to get a job in the UK.
1.1.4. Con: The right to move and work throughout Europe could still be granted through a standard visa process.
1.2. Pro: A hard Brexit would threaten the UK's domestic security with respect to crime, terrorism and the rule of law.
1.2.1. Pro: The European Arrest Warrants, which [rely](https://www.parliament.uk/business/committees/committees-a-z/lords-select/eu-home-affairs-subcommittee/news-parliament-2017/arrest-warrant-government-response/) on the jurisdiction of the Court of Justice of the European Union, have reduced the time taken to extradite criminals [from 1 year to just 48 days](http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-europe-23239493).
1.2.1.1. Con: Refusing to sign an extradition treaty with the UK to effectively preserve the present system would be an example of the EU acting maliciously against the interests of its own citizens. It is right to leave a club as nasty as to endanger lives of its own people rather than compromise with a close ally.
1.2.1.2. Pro: While there are some theoretical alternatives to European Arrest Warrants which could be as efficient, these have [not been](https://www.parliament.uk/business/committees/committees-a-z/lords-select/eu-home-affairs-subcommittee/news-parliament-2017/arrest-warrant-government-response/) successfully implemented despite years of negotiation.
1.2.1.3. Con: This arrangement need not be dependent on the wide-scale political and economic union that the EU entails. Britain could leave the EU in its totality and then make a bilateral agreement with the EU, which closely resembles the current arrangement. It would be mutually beneficial for the EU and UK to make such an agreement.
1.2.2. Pro: In 2016, UK agencies launched [3,000 investigations](http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-39272921) at Europol \(the [EU's law enforcement agency](https://www.europol.europa.eu/about-europol/our-thinking)\) into cross-border organised crime and terrorism.
1.2.2.1. Con: Refusing to allow Europol to work with the UK would be an example of the EU acting maliciously against the interests of its own citizens. It is right to leave a club as nasty as to endanger lives of its own people rather than compromise with a close ally.
1.2.2.2. Con: Europol is not crucial in combating terrorism as Intelligence Agencies [rarely share](http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-39272921) this kind of sensitive information via this avenue.
1.2.3. Pro: A hard Brexit would be particularly dangerous as it might threaten access to some [essential](http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-39272921) systems such as the [Second Generation Schengen Information System](https://ec.europa.eu/home-affairs/what-we-do/policies/borders-and-visas/schengen-information-system_en), which is a key tool in countering terrorism.
1.2.3.1. Con: Losing access would be an example of the EU acting maliciously against the interests of its own citizens.  It is right to leave a club as nasty as to endanger lives of its own people rather than compromise with a close ally.
1.2.3.1.1. Con: Many aspects of EU counter-terrorism is subject to EU law governed by the ECJ. It would simply be illegal under current EU laws to share information covered by data protection with 3rd nations. Brexit was a vote to become a 3rd nation not subject by EU laws, thus we have voted to [exclude](https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/2017/aug/12/britain-falling-behind-eu-terror-fight) ourselves from the sharing of counter-terrorism information.
1.2.3.1.2. Con: Revoking access to the benefits of the EU is important to maintain the EU's own collective integrity. An 'exclusive' club that grants all of its benefits to non-members would quickly lose many of its members. Therefore the EU's revocation of the UK's access is simply pragmatic, rather than ''nasty''.
1.2.3.1.2.1. Pro: The EU's tough stance when dealing with Brexit has led to decreased demand for similar exits from [other countries](https://www.thelocal.dk/20190104/danish-eu-support-at-record-high-poll).
1.2.3.2. Pro: This system is accessible to individual police officers on the street, and allowed them to identify [over 6,000](https://publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201617/ldselect/ldeucom/77/7706.htm) people of interest \(missing, organised crime, etc.\) in the UK between April 2015 and March 2016.
1.2.3.3. Pro: [Counter terrorism](https://publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201617/ldselect/ldeucom/77/7706.htm) alerts, which are more discrete than the regular police ones, can also be entered into the system.
1.2.3.4. Pro: This system holds the details of [over 8,000](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2017/may/13/brexit-may-britain-terrorists-schengen-information-system) suspected members of ISIS, making it invaluable when it comes to tracking returning foreign fighters.
1.2.3.5. Con: The article quoted says Britain would be unlikely to lose access to these systems: "But shutting Britain out of this club would be counter-productive for the rest of Europe, so officers are confident it will be retained after Brexit."
1.3. Con: Being part of the EU harms British culture.
1.3.1. Pro: The EU projects its own symbols of a shared identity, for example an [anthem and a flag](https://www.coe.int/en/web/tbilisi/the-coe/about-coe/flag-anthem). These [cultural symbols](https://books.google.com/books?id=app9DAAAQBAJ&pg=PA128&lpg=PA128&dq=council+of+europe+symbols+impact+on+culture&source=bl&ots=gRCvGO2kdo&sig=arEKdjK954syvTNPUUxw7eJlZd8&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwjtgPfXiYjZAhVDs1kKHaKFA4UQ6AEIUDAJ#v=onepage&q=council%20of%20europe%20symbols%20impact%20on%20culture&f=false) distract from more local and national symbols of culture.
1.3.1.1. Con: The UK contains [five](https://www.gov.uk/government/news/cornish-granted-minority-status-within-the-uk) national identities, each with their [own flag](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_British_flags), and even some counties within England, such as Lancashire, have their [own flag](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flag_of_Lancashire). If someone can consider themselves to be from Lancashire, England, and the United Kingdom, it should not be difficult to also identify as European.
1.3.1.1.1. Con: It is not a question of difficulty, but of reality. 50% of British people felt they were British to the exclusion of being European \([Standard Eurobarometer, p.30](http://ec.europa.eu/commfrontoffice/publicopinion/index.cfm/ResultDoc/download/DocumentKy/86438)\), and therefore feel that European identity and symbols do not represent them.
1.3.1.1.1.1. Con: And yet, in the same study 58% reported considered themselves to be a citizen of the EU.
1.3.1.2. Con: The EU indeed has its own flag and anthem to create a shared feeling of being European. This was deliberate to end wars between European states. The EU has been mostly successful in this, but this doesn't override the national flags or anthems.
1.3.2. Con: "British culture", through history, has been defined by its inclusivity, welcoming nature and acceptance of other cultures. Brexit seeks to alter British culture as opposed to Remain, which enshrines it.
1.3.2.1. Pro: British culture is itself an amalgamation of different cultures, including from many EU countries.
1.3.2.1.1. Pro: Being European is a big part of British culture. British art, food, sports and religions are primarily derived from thousands of years of shared history with other European countries and peoples.
1.3.2.1.2. Pro: British people and their culture are fundamentally European. The [Anglo-Saxons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anglo-Saxons) and even the [Celts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celts) arrived here from Europe. Before the Celts, Britain was [populated by Basques](https://thewildpeak.wordpress.com/2011/12/28/are-the-english-basque/) who arrived from France and Spain. Old English originates from North Sea Germanic, and [Modern English developed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_English) through combining Old English with Norse, French, Latin, and Greek.
1.3.2.1.3. Con: British identity, although having indeed been influenced by various cultures over the course of history, is still a unique country, with a specific culture and identity, that cannot be reduced to an amalgamation of different cultures \(which, in the end, could be considered true of all cultures\). After all, it is obvious from a glance, and even more if we dig deeper, that the UK is very different from France, Germany, Italy, Poland, Hungary, etc.
1.3.2.1.3.1. Con: The UK is in fact a [collection](https://brilliantmaps.com/eng-gb-uk/) of countries \(and a country fragment\) each with its own distinct culture. They borrow from one other, and from their European neighbours.
1.3.2.1.3.2. Con: Italians are different from French \(remember Napoleon?\), Polish are different from Germans \(remember WW2?\). And yet, French, Italians, English, Polish and Germans share common values: human rights, no death row, freedom of speech, public services, democracy \(to mention a few\). The English are different, yes, but not more different than other European nations between them. And at the same time have much more in common compared to other cultures.
1.3.2.2. Con: Britain has historically been ruthless and [discriminatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colonial_mentality#British_India) against the cultures of the people it has colonized.
1.3.2.2.1. Con: Colonial bad behaviour against others' cultures has occurred in the colonies, but that does not establish that the same has occurred in Britain.
1.3.2.3. Pro: Britain has internalized many of the cultural practises of the nations of the Commonwealth. For example, [Indian food](http://news.bbc.co.uk/2/hi/8370054.stm) is now present and readily available throughout Britain.
1.3.3. Con: The greatest effect on 'British culture' would likely occur in [high immigration areas](https://theconversation.com/hard-evidence-how-areas-with-low-immigration-voted-mainly-for-brexit-62138). However, populations in high immigration areas voted overwhelmingly to stay. This suggests that people genuinely affected by changes in British culture are supportive of this change and that British culture has improved.
1.3.3.1. Con: If you look at changes in immigration levels a [different pattern](https://www.economist.com/britain/2016/07/14/explaining-the-brexit-vote) emerges. Recent changes in the proportion of immigrants \(even though to smaller absolute numbers\) correlated strongly with voting leave.
1.3.3.1.1. Pro: This shift may mean that the areas that voted leave were less equiped to adjust to immigration, thus they were acting off real experience.
1.3.3.1.2. Con: This does not necessarily disprove the parents claim that "perception of migrants has more effect than experience", instead it shows that people do not like change.
1.3.3.1.2.1. Pro: It could be suggested that if the rate of migration in a town like Boston slowed drastically, the town would get used to the new migrants and opinions would move towards that of the other high total immigration areas \(leaning towards remain\).
1.3.3.2. Pro: From this it appears that any negative consequences for culture and national unity are not so much experienced by people who have the most experience with immigrants, but could be just a perception of those without much experience with immigrants.
1.3.3.3. Con: Even if this were true, it would not be surprising that people who have not yet been negatively affected by immigration would be apprehensive of those effects reaching them — as them not living in high immigration areas does not mean they cannot know how immigration affects their country.
1.3.4. Con: EU membership confers benefits and privileges that help preserve British culture.
1.3.4.1. Pro: EU programs are essential to [protecting cultural heritage](https://ec.europa.eu/culture/policy/culture-policies/cultural-heritage_en), including archaeological sites, monuments, museums, and artwork, that distinctively represent the culture within its member states. Brexit would mean that Britain would lose access to these programs which promote British culture.
1.3.4.1.1. Pro: The Creative Industries Federation \(an association of members from UK creative industries\) states that 96 per cent of its members supported remaining in the EU and 84 per cent of its members believed [the outcome of the referendum](https://www.newstatesman.com/politics/brexit/2016/07/what-will-brexit-mean-arts-and-culture-uk) was important to the future success of their organisations, as Brexit would result in a loss of EU funding and support for cultural programs.
1.3.4.1.2. Con: Protecting archeological sites is almost useless if the overall culture and identity of the country are being diluted.
1.3.4.1.3. Con: The money that the UK \([who contributes more than it receives](http://news.bbc.co.uk/1/hi/world/europe/8036802.stm)\) gives to the EU could be used directly in their own country. Leaving the EU would thus be more positive than negative.
1.3.4.1.3.1. Con: This claim also does not take into account those benefits the UK derives which cannot easily be measured in terms of money, i.e. the increased quality of goods consumed, the efficiencies for companies of having a wider workforce, the healthcare enjoyed by UK pensioners and others in other countries.
1.3.4.1.3.1.1. Pro: The EU could achieve higher efficiencies for the member states because it allows division of labour and higher specialisation \([see Adam Smith](http://www.victorianweb.org/economics/division.html)\). This is the ideal the EU \(ought to\) work towards.
1.3.4.2. Pro: The UK has been part of the EU for over 40 years and still has a distinct culture. This suggests that EU membership is, at the very least, not a threat to British culture.
1.3.4.2.1. Pro: There is plenty of [evidence](https://warwick.ac.uk/fac/soc/al-archive/leap/uklife/britishculture/) that British culture \(however you define it\) is robust enough to withstand outside influence. There is no evidence that Germans are less German or French are less French because of the EU.
1.3.4.2.2. Pro: Countries even further intregrated into the EU, such as France and Germany, have also been able to maintain their cultural identity.
1.3.4.3. Pro: The EU supports regional culture, and thus regional identity, more than the UK government does.
1.3.4.3.1. Pro: The European Capital of Culture Programme has supported regional culture [twice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Capital_of_Culture) in the UK, once in Glasgow and once in Liverpool.
1.3.4.3.2. Con: Between 2015-2016, the UK benefitted from [€11.3 million](http://www.artscouncil.org.uk/sites/default/files/download-file/Arts_culture_Sector_exit_from_EU_NOV_16.pdf). In 2018, the total [Arts Council England](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arts_Council_England) \(which distributes government arts funds\) budget is £622 million, with the amount spent outside London targeted to rise [from 70% to 75%](https://www.theguardian.com/culture/2015/may/28/arts-council-england-increase-ratio-funding-outside-london-darren-henley). The EU's support for regional culture is minor compared to the UK's.
1.3.4.3.2.1. Con: The EU budget is [€290](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economy_of_the_European_Union) per capita, while the UK government budget is [£9350](https://www.google.com/url?sa=t&rct=j&q=&esrc=s&source=web&cd=2&cad=rja&uact=8&ved=2ahUKEwjU1dLWt7PiAhV5VBUIHdSpCLoQFjABegQIDBAF&url=https%3A%2F%2Fresearchbriefings.parliament.uk%2FResearchBriefing%2FSummary%2FSN04033&usg=AOvVaw3MXly-AM0e2o5uLzdZmliG) per capita. Thus making a direct comparision between €11m and £622m is extremely disingenuous.
1.3.4.4. Pro: Many British cultural leaders [believe](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2017/dec/31/brexit-threat-to-arts) that a hard Brexit will hurt their nation's artistic reputation, as it curtails the ability of most British artists to work freely abroad and international artists to work in the UK. This also hurts the perception of the UK as an iconic cultural hub.
1.3.4.4.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.4.1.1.
1.3.4.4.2. Pro: Travel restrictions, which would result from a hard Brexit, will add large financial and logistical costs to touring companies and so prevent many British theatre companies from touring.
1.3.4.4.3. Con: Travelling won’t suddenly become impossible without the EU, and it doesn’t change anything for non-EU artists or British artists travelling outside of the EU.
1.3.4.4.4. Con: Britain’s iconic cultural hub identity is independent from the EU, as it predates it — wether you take into account London being a capital of culture in the 19th century, or the popular culture boom in the[interwar era](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_the_United_Kingdom#Interwar_era_1918–1939). Believing brexit will hurt the UK as a culture hub is greatly underestimating the country’s appeal and history.
1.3.5. Con: British culture is primarily influenced by America, even as a member of the EU. Many Leave voters take no issue with American influence but protest cultural influence of other regions.
1.3.5.1. Pro: British media is made to be sellable to America as they are the largest and wealthiest English language culture market.
1.3.5.2. Pro: Language barriers make influence from European cultures slow to cross over and even slower to spread, but Americanisation permeates every level of British life.
1.3.5.3. Con: One problem does not negate another, and one could say that at least it’s anglo-saxon culture. The fact is the problem of American influence is a different problem to that of European influence \(especially this notion that the UK doesn’t really have its own culture, and is at best just an amalgam of other cultures — European and otherwise\).
1.3.5.3.1. Con: The Angles, Saxons and Jutes all came from mainland Europe, or from north Germany, Denmark and northern Holland to be more precise. America is itself a huge blend of cultures including German, Italian, Chinese, etc. so suggesting that they are culturally far closer to us is misleading.
1.3.6. Pro: While immigration is not a problem in itself, if it is too large, and too fast, the people coming in [cannot be assimilated](http://www.france24.com/fr/20150501-birmingham-limites-multiculturalisme-anglais-royaume-uni-elections-islam-tensions-communautarisme-small-heath) to the country’s culture. Thus, communities form that live apart from British culture and do not assimilate or identify with it. It harms British culture, and weakens social cohesion indeed.
1.3.6.1. Con: The way UK immigration figures are calculated means that the numbers include a great many people who have no intention of becoming permanent residents of the UK. Students, seasonal workers, etc. form a large part of the "immigrant" population and they all tend to return to their home countries after a short stay. There's no need for such people to be assimilated, and no evidence that their clustering for mutual support while resident here inflicts any damage on British culture.
1.3.6.2. Con: There's no reason why immigrants should be assimilated to the "country's culture". The desire to force them or persuade them to conform to whichever values someone decides to call British and to call them a problem if they don't is racism.
1.3.6.3. Con: The issues raised - too many immigrants in isolated communities leading to lack of integration - are planning issues that could be mitigated bycarefully considered mix-use housing developments in the places where migrant work is required.
1.3.6.4. Con: Culture is organic and evolves constantly, with each generation adding or removing customs and practices. Leaving the EU will do nothing to change this, as migration will still be allowed in both directions, even if in reduced quantities.
1.3.6.4.1. Pro: This means the British population will still be exposed to different cultures, customs and practices which each individual is allowed to choose to adopt or not \(if the law permits\).
1.3.6.4.2. Con: The impact of on-screen world, whether television or internet, dwarfs that of real life. We interact much more with devices than we do with our neighbours, making any cultural impact they may have negligible.
1.3.6.4.3. Con: Culture is indeed organic, however it needs time to assimilate various influences to make it its own. The lack of control over immigration policy weakens the ability to control the rate at which people come into the country, which in turn makes it harder to both assimilate immigrants and to integrate eventual cultural influences from them into \(and not on top of\) British culture.
1.3.6.4.3.1. Con: -> See 1.3.6.2.
1.3.6.4.3.2. Con: Culture has no sensibilities so it needs no time so presumably this refers to bigots. This is about letting bigotry delay or even stop change.
1.3.6.5. Con: A major proportion of immigrants in the UK are not from the EU, but rather [countries somehow tied in with the British Empire](http://www.migrationobservatory.ox.ac.uk/resources/briefings/migrants-in-the-uk-an-overview/) such as Pakistan, India, South Africa, Bangladesh, Nigeria. The control of borders for these immigrants has been resting with British authorities and is not an EU issue.
1.3.6.5.1. Con: There has been a great [increase of immigration from Europe](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/2016/03/22/eu-referendum-the-europe-debate-in-numbers/) in the last five to ten years, whether it be [Western, or Eastern Europe](https://www.migrationwatchuk.org/statistics-net-migration-statistics/#create-graph) \(in particular Bulgaria and Romania\). More accurately, immigration is on a downward trend from non-European countries, whereas immigration from EU-countries has greatly increased. Furthermore, there being strong non-EU immigration doesn’t mean EU-immigration cannot be a problem. Those are two different issues, and should be handled differently.
1.3.6.5.2. Con: A lot of the immigration people were concerned about was illegal immigration, with the current migrant crisis, as the EU has been very lax in controlling its own borders. In this case, once migrants get into the EU, they can pretty much travel anywhere in it without much problems.
1.4. Con: The EU is too dysfunctional for the UK.
1.4.1. Pro: The EU wastes [vast sums of money](http://www.europarl.europa.eu/document/activities/cont/201310/20131025ATT73477/20131025ATT73477EN.pdf) in [decamping](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/av/uk-politics-20096221/eu-parliament-s-journeys-from-strasbourg-to-brussels) from [Brussels to Strasbourg](https://news.sky.com/story/meps-on-the-move-madness-of-strasbourg-shift-10293596) every month. If the EU is incapable of making the trivial decision to end this waste, then it is not capable of making more consequential decisions.
1.4.1.1. Con: This is not an example of the EU being dysfunctional per se. It is also not a trivial decision, nor particularly challenging. The policy may be annoying and a strange distribution of wealth - one that ultimately benefits both towns at the expense of the entire block - but does not demonstrate dysfunction.
1.4.1.1.1. Con: One function, entrusted to the EU, is to handle the vast sums of money it is given effectively. Therefore, wasting it in this way is a very good example of dysfunctionality.
1.4.1.2. Pro: 91% of MEPs [oppose the commuting](https://www.quora.com/Why-does-the-European-parliament-move-from-Brussels-to-Strasbourg-once-a-month), with France the only country benefiting.
1.4.1.3. Con: A cost of 0.06% of the overall Budget of the European Union is relatively trivial.
1.4.1.4. Con: The UK government also inefficiently wastes vast sums of money on matters for which trivial decisions could be taken to end it.
1.4.1.4.1. Pro: The House of Lords is hugely wasteful as an institution. Its reform should be easy for the Government to achieve.
1.4.1.4.1.1. Pro: The House of Lords has 778 members, vastly more than necessary for its function.
1.4.1.4.1.1.1. Pro: The US Senate, whose role is roughly equivalent to the Lords, functions with just 100 members.
1.4.1.4.1.2. Pro: The daily cost to sustain some peers in the Lords is [25 times higher](https://www.parliament.uk/documents/lords-finance-office/2018-19/allowances-expenses-2018-19-month10-january.pdf) than most members of the House of Commons.
1.4.1.4.2. Pro: The UK Government has continued to pour money into HS2, despite clear warnings from experts that it is a waste of money and [a lack of public support](https://www.comresglobal.com/polls/quicksilver-media-hs2-poll-feb-2019/).
1.4.2. Pro: The EU has failed to address many of the [economic problems](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2014/oct/17/europe-in-turmoil-five-years-of-economic-crisis) that have developed since 2008.
1.4.2.1. Con: It is not an agreed area of EU competence to intervene in sovereign member states' economic affairs. Therefore it is not to be expected that the EU would address member states' internal economic problems. This point appears to conflate EU membership with the adoption of the Euro, which clearly the UK has not joined. And the link in question is very old news.
1.4.2.2. Con: Many countries outside the EU, especially the USA, also suffered a deep recession starting in 2008. Blaming the EU for this is wrong.
1.4.3. Pro: The EU is not democratic.
1.4.3.1. Con: Two of the three pillars of UK governance are unelected, i.e. 'The House of Lords' and ‘The Judiciary’.
1.4.3.2. Pro: One of the most powerful EU bodies, the College of Commissioners, is [not democratically elected](http://www.huffingtonpost.co.uk/matthew-ellery/eu-referendum_b_9514608.html).
1.4.3.2.1. Pro: The European commission is unelected and have been active since 2007. We may elect our MEPs but the [main policy makers](https://ec.europa.eu/info/sites/info/files/c_2017_6915_final_en.pdf) are still unelected and while they are accountable to the European parliament who can censure their decisions it is by no means a perfect system as it falls to the foibles of international politics.
1.4.3.2.1.1. Con: Commissioners are selected by democratically elected Governments. Having no faith in our Government choosing Commissioners to represent us within the EU suggests that we have no faith in them governing us with the full sovereignty Brexit will deliver.
1.4.3.2.1.2. Con: The EU is no less democratic than the UK. The British cabinet ministers are unelected; they are selected by the PM.
1.4.3.2.2. Con: One potential Commissioner is [nominated](http://www.europarl.europa.eu/atyourservice/en/displayFtu.html?ftuId=FTU_1.3.8.html) by the ruling \(democratically elected\) national government of each member state.  These suggestions are then confirmed by the democratically elected European Parliament.  So, the public vote for their own national government and, in doing so, place their trust in them to choose a suitable Commissioner, in an equivalent way to that which the UK public places their trust in their elected House of Commons to select a suitable Prime Minister.
1.4.3.2.2.1. Con: No one asks their local/national candidates who their pick for EU Commissioner would be let alone lets it influence their vote. People elect them for local/national matters not EU wide direction.
1.4.3.2.2.2. Con: In the UK with FPTP voting only [twice](https://researchbriefings.parliament.uk/ResearchBriefing/Summary/CBP-7529) in the last 100 years has the ruling party earned \>50% of the vote. From that the party picks the PM, and then the PM decides the Commissioner. This is too many steps from the people to the Commission for popular will to reasonably be carried out.
1.4.3.2.3. Con: The President of the Commission \(Jean-Claude Juncker\) was elected by the [Spitzenkandidat process](http://www.europarl.europa.eu/news/en/headlines/eu-affairs/20150526STO59409/spitzenkandidaten-the-story-of-what-made-last-year-s-ep-elections-different).
1.4.3.3. Con: When passing laws the unelected bureaucrats of the EU work in a [co-decision process](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2016/jun/13/is-the-eu-undemocratic-referendum-reality-check) with elected representatives of the European Parliament.
1.4.3.3.1. Con: In the UK, civil servants and the House of Lords \(both groups of unelected bureaucrats\) work in a co-decision process with British MPs \(the elected representatives\).
1.4.3.4. Pro: The democratic deficit within the EU has left many feeling disenfranchised and led to the rise of [right wing parties.](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2017/sep/29/right-social-democracy-dying-europe-afd-far-right-germany)
1.4.3.4.1. Con: Growth of far-right politics is growing around the world, including in America and Australia. This rise has probably much more the effect of the Global Economic Crisis, where people around the world are dissatisfied with wage freezes, greater job insecurity and their politicians austerity policies reducing spending on infrastructure. [Rise of the American Far Right](https://www.newyorker.com/news/daily-comment/the-far-right-revival-a-thirty-year-war)
1.4.3.4.2. Con: The rise of right wing parties doesn't make the EU undemocratic.
1.4.3.5. Con: The European Commission acts as the EU's Civil Service. Given most Civil Services requires individuals with specialised skills and training, it is unreasonable to expect them to be elected.
1.4.3.6. Con: Democracy does not necessarily mean the same thing in the EU and the UK. The EU needs to strike a consensus between the different democracies of the Member States, some being coalition systems with multiple parties, some with first-past-the-post systems, etc. The EU cannot have the same type of democracy as a state, because it is a supranational organisation, working for the benefit of citizens and countries.
1.4.3.7. Con: Member states must have [strong democratic institutions](https://ec.europa.eu/neighbourhood-enlargement/policy/glossary/terms/accession-criteria_en) in order to join the EU.
1.4.3.7.1. Con: There are four Nazis sitting on the European Parliament, three elected with the Greek [Golden Dawn](http://www.pappaspost.com/refer-greeces-golden-dawn-party-neo-nazi/), and one elected with the German [National Democratic Party](http://www.abc.net.au/news/2017-01-18/national-democratic-party-resembles-nazis-but-no-ban/8189930). Their wages are paid by the EU.
1.4.3.7.1.1. Con: Allowing extreme parties is a characteristic of liberal democracy, not a lack of it. Golden dawn and NDP were elected with enough Greek/German votes to earn their position. Proportional representation requirements for MEP elections are designed to allow minorities a voice, no matter how unpleasant that voice may be. Until convicted of a crime, they have the right to become elected in a democracy, so they shouldn't be seen as a point against the EU's democratic institutions.
1.4.3.7.1.1.1. Con: Allowing Nazis to be elected is a characteristic of a liberal democracy, but Nazis actually managing to get elected is testament of weak and corrupt democratic institutions.
1.4.3.7.2. Con: Poland and Hungary have been making increasingly [illiberal and anti-democratic](http://foreignpolicy.com/2018/02/05/hungary-and-poland-arent-democratic-theyre-authoritarian/) changes in their governance over recent years. It is unclear if the EU has the capacity to maintain democracy among its member states.
1.4.3.7.3. Pro: Following Turkey's dramatic shift toward illiberalism, European Commissioner Johannes Hahn [stated](https://www.reuters.com/article/us-turkey-eu/turkeys-eu-dream-is-over-for-now-top-official-says-idUSKBN17Y0U0) that its bid to join the EU was effectively finished.
1.4.3.7.3.1. Con: This is no longer [the case.](https://www.ft.com/content/09935e16-a230-11e8-85da-eeb7a9ce36e4)
1.4.3.7.3.1.1. Pro: The article states that the EU might attempt to strengthen diplomatic relationships, but does not suggest that this comes anywhere close to the threshold required to join the EU.
1.4.3.7.4. Con: EU's accession criteria doesn't prove or refute the \(un\)democratic nature of the EU itself.
1.4.3.8. Pro: European Parliament elections [suffer](https://www.theatlantic.com/international/archive/2014/05/europes-democratic-deficit-is-getting-worse/371297/) from low turnouts, and increasingly disengaged electorates.
1.4.3.8.1. Con: This does not make the EU a non-democracy; the [United States](http://www.pewresearch.org/fact-tank/2017/05/15/u-s-voter-turnout-trails-most-developed-countries/) suffers from similarly low voter turnouts.
1.4.3.9. Pro: Even the EU [recognizes](http://eur-lex.europa.eu/summary/glossary/democratic_deficit.html?locale=en) that many voters feel it suffers from a democratic deficit.
1.4.3.9.1. Con: The fact that the EU publicly recognizes this is the mark of a responsive governance system.
1.4.3.9.2. Con: The EU has acted to correct this. The democratic basis of the EU has been the European Parliament whose members have been elected by the electorate of the member states. While this is democratic, the electorate places more significance on their votes for their own national government than they do for their MEP, and are subsequently disenchanted that the European Parliament can overrule their national Government. Had the UK not chosen to pursue Brexit this discrepancy would have been [corrected](http://eprints.lse.ac.uk/73195/1/blogs.lse.ac.uk-A%20vote%20to%20leave%20the%20EU%20may%20have%20a%20detrimental%20impact%20on%20Parliament.pdf).
1.4.3.9.3. Con: The public opinion about the democratic deficit in the EU doesn't necessarily reflect reality.
1.4.3.10. Con: Democracy is not a guarantee of functionality.
1.4.3.10.1. Pro: The Chinese Government is relatively functional, despite there being virtually no democracy in China.
1.4.3.10.1.1. Pro: The Chinese government, which has no real democratic obligations or accountability, is far better at investing in long term [infrastructure growth projects](https://www.kpmg.de/docs/Infrastructure_in_China.pdf).
1.4.3.10.1.2. Pro: China was given a 0 score for ''Electoral Process and Pluralism" in the [ECU's Democracy Index](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democracy_Index).
1.4.3.10.1.3. Pro: From [1960-2016](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_\(real\)_per_capita_growth_rate), China had the highest average annual growth rate at 6.61% of any country in the world.
1.4.3.10.2. Pro: Ukraine has relatively strong democratic institutions and yet has also been ranked as the [9th most corrupt](https://fraudsurveys.ey.com/ey-global-fraud-survey-2018/detailed-results/) in a survey of 55 countries.
1.4.3.11. Con: The European Union Parliament is elected using [proportional representation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elections_to_the_European_Parliament), ensuring it is more democratic than the UK's own parliament.
1.4.3.12. Con: The EU's democratic legitimacy is also derived from participation of democratic national governments and leaders in the decision-making process. Some [EU decisions](http://www.votewatch.eu/en/term8-proposal-for-a-council-decision-establishing-provisional-measures-in-the-area-of-international-prote.html#/##vote_list_tabs_content_1) are taken on an international basis and excludes Parliament \(supranational legislature\) from the decision-making process.
1.4.3.13. Pro: Due to its large population and dominating economy, Germany has had [major influence](https://www.reuters.com/article/us-eu-germany-influence-insight/insight-merkels-europe-how-her-men-run-brussels-idUSBRE99N05320131024) over the vast majority of EU policies, much to the [dissatisfaction](https://www.express.co.uk/news/world/796149/Eurozone-EU-dominance-Brussels-France-Germany-Merkel-Macron) of other member states.
1.4.3.13.1. Con: The same could be said about British politics, where labour and conservative political parties have major control and influence, to the dissatisfaction of other parties/independants.
1.4.3.13.2. Pro: Germany has been the principal [force](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2015/jan/31/growth-decide-eurozones-future-greek) behind EU austerity measures throughout the Eurozone, particularly in Greece.
1.4.3.13.2.1. Con: Greece has a huge culture of [Tax Evasion](https://www.dianeosis.org/en/2016/06/tax-evasion-in-greece/) which it has been reluctant to tackle, as well as hiding its [debt](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-europe-16834815) from the EU when it joined. The EU could not afford to continue bailing out Greece to the cost of other nation states including Britain when it made no real attempt at tackling it's own economic problems.
1.4.3.13.3. Con: Large and powerful states/regions typically have power over smaller and weaker ones, irrespective of their place in/out of the EU.
1.4.3.13.3.1. Pro: England and London similarly have a huge influence over UK politics, much to the dissatisfaction of other areas.
1.4.3.13.3.1.1. Pro: HS2 is a project that would primarily benefit London, and is [opposed by the vast majority](https://www.comresglobal.com/polls/quicksilver-media-hs2-poll-feb-2019/) of people living in northern communities
1.4.3.13.3.2. Pro: Under any reasonable democratic system, a larger share of the population should equate to a larger degree of political leverage.
1.4.3.13.4. Con: Influence or soft power might differ among EU member states, but each EU member state has only one vote in the EU Council and European Council. Therefore decisions are taken democratically with a consent of majority member states.
1.4.3.14. Pro: The EU is anti-democratic. It routinely attempts to enforce its will on member states regardless of democratic direction, as it did regarding Ireland's vote against the Lisbon Treaty. If Britain wishes to have political power, rights and freedoms, it must leave the EU. [The Guardian on the Lisbon Vote](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2008/dec/13/eu-ireland-lisbon-treaty)
1.4.3.14.1. Pro: This has happened [three times](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/europpblog/2015/10/19/asking-the-public-twice-why-do-voters-change-their-minds-in-second-referendums-on-eu-treaties/) in EU referendums – in Denmark on the Maastricht Treaty in 1992-3, Ireland on the Nice Treaty in 2001-2, and Ireland again on the Lisbon Treaty in 2008-9.
1.4.3.14.2. Con: For all three repeated EU referendums, the governments [sought reassurances](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/europpblog/2015/10/19/asking-the-public-twice-why-do-voters-change-their-minds-in-second-referendums-on-eu-treaties/) from the EU on the controversial themes of the first campaign. This allowed them to ask the same question again, but address the issues raised by the initial vote.
1.4.3.14.3. Con: Scotland hasn't voted for a Conservative Government in decades and yet are generally controlled by Tories in Westminster. Being in a political union will inevitably involve compromises as well as benefits.
1.4.3.14.4. Con: The situation in Ireland [changed](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/comment/5638510/Why-Ireland-is-primed-to-reverse-its-Lisbon-Treaty-vote.html) after they voted against the Lisbon treaty so they put it to the vote again. It is a strange kind of democracy that cannot allow the electorate to change their mind.
1.4.4. Con: Brexit is the ultimate expression of dysfunction and the EU an example of stability and union.
1.4.5. Con: Since it has been shown that being an EU member is beneficial to the UK and the cost to the individual citizen minimal, then the efficiency, or lack of, of it's process is irrelevant.
1.4.6. Con: In some cases it is the EU that protects British citizens from their own Government's dysfunction.
1.4.6.1. Pro: The EU may in fact protect the citizens of the UK, through legal oversight, from the negative effects of poor policy making or implementation. Vulnerable and marginalised groups [have used](https://academic.oup.com/ejil/article/28/3/763/4616672#100347677) the European Court of Justice to bring numerous claims against the British Government for human rights abuses.
1.4.6.1.1. Pro: The EU has created [many directives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_labour_law#Equality) which encourage equal opportunities for ethnic minorities across Europe.
1.4.6.1.1.1. Pro: The EUs [European Convention on Human Rights](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Convention_on_Human_Rights#Article_14_%E2%80%93_discrimination) is a key legislation for preventing gender/ethnic discrimination, amongst many other similar areas.
1.4.6.1.1.1.1. Con: The ECHR is enforced by the Council of Europe, which is a completely different organisation to the EU.  The [UK will still be signed up to the ECHR](https://www.equalityhumanrights.com/en/our-human-rights-work/what-does-brexit-mean-equality-and-human-rights-uk) when it leaves the EU.
1.4.6.1.1.2. Pro: The Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union provides some [Human Rights which would be lost](https://www.equalityhumanrights.com/en/our-human-rights-work/what-does-brexit-mean-equality-and-human-rights-uk) if we leave the EU as proposed under the [Repeal Bill](https://www.gov.uk/government/publications/the-repeal-bill-white-paper).
1.4.7. Con: Dysfunction is completely subjective. Who's to say what is normal for a union of countries like those in Europe. The fact that they exist and have 28 member states strongly suggests that they are not, "[deviating from the norms of social behavior in a way regarded as bad](http://www.dictionary.com/browse/dysfunctional)."
1.4.7.1. Pro: The UK has [seriously influenced](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/long_reads/brexit-britain-uk-eu-europe-influence-history-second-world-war-a8711586.html) the way EU works. If the EU is dysfunctional, then it is partly UKs fault, and as such the UK ought to help develop functionality within the union rather than fleeing from its own mess.
1.4.7.2. Con: If the structure of the EU leads to harmful externalities for its member states, this should be a relevant factor in the decision for the UK to remain or leave in the event of a hard Brexit.
1.4.8. Con: The UK would need to replace many of the EU's policies and institutions. These replacements will likely end up being more dysfunctional than the existing ones.
1.4.8.1. Pro: The course and outcome of negotiations on Brexit on both sides suggest that the [UK is more dysfunctional than the EU](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/brexit/2018/07/13/brexits-institutional-irony-how-the-eu-has-successfully-outflanked-the-uk/). Hence leaving the EU for this reason is illogical.
1.4.8.1.1. Con: It is not reasonable to gauge the organization of entire administrations based on the conduct of their small negotiation teams.
1.4.8.2. Pro: The Windrush scandal [illustrates](https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/2018/may/25/number-of-windrush-cases-passes-5000-mark) how [incompetent](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-parliaments-43791047) the Home Office is.
1.5. Pro: A hard Brexit will hurt the scientific community in the UK.
1.5.1. Con: Brexit will increase International non-EU collaborations as the interventionist EU policies incentivising collaboration with other EU groups to the detriment of non-EU groups will dissipate.
1.5.1.1. Con: There are a series of [structural reasons](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/brexit/2015/12/05/debunking-the-myths-about-british-science-after-an-eu-exit/) which make international collaborations within the EU effective. It is unlikely that collaborations outside the EU can replicate these conditions.
1.5.1.1.1. Pro: The centralised funding [substantially reduces bureaucracy](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/brexit/2015/12/05/debunking-the-myths-about-british-science-after-an-eu-exit/) when it comes to arranging international collaborations.
1.5.1.1.2. Pro: The shared political interest in promoting EU science means that the [best laboratory](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/brexit/2015/12/05/debunking-the-myths-about-british-science-after-an-eu-exit/) can be chosen to perform a particular task without preferentially selecting national funding for work in the country it is sourced from.
1.5.1.1.3. Pro: Centralised funding means that there is [less risk](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/brexit/2015/12/05/debunking-the-myths-about-british-science-after-an-eu-exit/) in engaging in international collaborations, as there is no chance that your collaborator may lose, or fail to acquire, funding.
1.5.1.2. Con: There is [no indication](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/brexit/2015/12/05/debunking-the-myths-about-british-science-after-an-eu-exit/) that the EU reduces other international collaborations, as it already works with 170 countries around the world.
1.5.1.3. Con: International collaboration is already the core of scientific research. There has been no evidence presented that Brexit can improve that in any way.
1.5.1.4. Con: If Marmite was suddenly taxed heavily, you could expect sales of Vegemite to increase. That doesnt change the fact that people were CHOOSING marmite and are now having their preferences impacted by regulation. This claim does not attack the parent claim. Unless it also suggests that non EU collaborations are inherently more valuable. IE Vegemite is somehow superior to Marmite.
1.5.1.5. Pro: Many projects funded under the EU's Horizon 2020 fund [require collaboration between at least 3 organisations from at least 3 EU \(or associated\) states](http://ec.europa.eu/research/participants/docs/h2020-funding-guide/cross-cutting-issues/international-cooperation_en.htm), prioritizing intra-EU collaboration over the preferences of the researchers themselves.
1.5.1.5.1. Con: The Horizon fund says that "participants from all over the world ... can participate in most of the calls of Horizon 2020". It specifically supports and encourages international collaboration.
1.5.1.5.2. Con: Whilst the UK is a net contributor overall, the [amount it receives in research funding](https://royalsociety.org/topics-policy/projects/uk-research-and-european-union/role-of-EU-in-funding-UK-research/how-much-funding-does-uk-get-in-comparison-with-other-countries/) is [greater than the proportion](https://fullfact.org/europe/eu-research-funding-uk/) of its funding that goes to research from contributions the EU pot
1.5.1.5.3. Con: Even if the UK withdrew £x for research and put it directly in its own pot for research \(as opposed to funding those NHS budget claims\), that funding would be subject to its own criteria \(maybe even to support projects that worked with EU states in order to maintain the research links post-brexit\).
1.5.1.5.4. Con: Horizon 2020 is not the only research grant available.
1.5.1.5.5. Con: Horizon 2020 serves as an example of a research avenue that the UK might not be able to access in the future.
1.5.1.5.5.1. Pro: Some UK researchers report that they have already been '[frozen out](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/science-environment-36835566)' of making grant applications to Horizon 2020, the EU's Research and Innovation programme.
1.5.2. Con: A proportion of current EU-funded non-UK EU scientists who are working in the UK will have been hired over non-EU \(and potentially UK\) scientists for reasons of [administrative ease, visa quotas, funding stipulations etc](https://www.timeshighereducation.com/features/non-eu-scholars-struggling-with-the-uks-visa-policy/2010106.article) rather than on merit alone. Post-Brexit, non-EU individuals will not be structurally disadvantaged relative to EU individuals.  Hiring foreign scientists based solely on merit rather than citizenship will increase the quality of the scientific research carried out in the UK.
1.5.2.1. Con: Money is an issue, however. A significant portion of the funding which is required for those jobs comes from EU sources. The figures vary from sector to sector, but it is roughly [15%](https://fullfact.org/education/how-much-money-do-british-universities-get-eu/) in [general funding](https://www.universitiesuk.ac.uk/policy-and-analysis/reports/Documents/2016/university-funding-explained.pdf), and according to Universities UK, [overall EU research funding generated over 19,000 full-time-equivalent jobs in the UK in 2014/2015](https://www.universitiesuk.ac.uk/policy-and-analysis/reports/Documents/2016/economic-impact-of-eu-research-funding-in-uk-universities.pdf) \(p4\). Thus, leaving the EU might mean prioritising non-EU hires in theory, but the money to hire them would be reduced.
1.5.2.1.1. Con: No funding is guaranteed, and the EU could equally have withdrawn it in the future.  As the UK is a net payer into the EU, this funding is really UK money, anyway.
1.5.2.1.2. Con: Quantity does not mean quality when it comes to scientific research.
1.5.2.2. Con: The administrative cost of bringing in non-EU citizens will not improve as a result of a hard Brexit. However it will be more costly to bring in EU scientists, meaning that overall quality could be hurt.
1.5.3. Pro: [Polling from the Campaign for Science and Engineering in 2015](http://www.epc.ac.uk/wp-content/uploads/2015/12/151214-FINAL-EPC_CaSE_Research-and-EU-Report_Dec-2015.pdf) showed that over 90% of scientists felt that EU membership was a *major* benefit to science and research \(pg. 1\). Many leading scientists have publicly called for a second referendum or to abort Brexit entirely; almost none support hard exit.
1.5.3.1. Pro: [97%](https://www.universityworldnews.com/post.php?story=20181023110732232) of UK scientists believe that a hard Brexit would have negative outcomes for science in the UK.
1.5.3.1.1. Con: The scientists in this survey were all staff from the biomedical research lab, [The Francis Crick Institute](https://www.universityworldnews.com/post.php?story=20181023110732232). As such, this finding may not be representative of all UK scientists who work across a plethora of different fields.
1.5.3.1.2. Pro: Scientists are well-placed to assess the impact of a hard Brexit on the scientific community because they have first-hand experience of it.
1.5.4. Pro: Brexit will reduce the numbers of scientists in the UK.
1.5.4.1. Pro: Ending free movement will restrict the ability of scientists to work across international boundaries and reduce the pool of scientists from which UK institutions can be drawn.
1.5.4.1.1. Con: Most immigration systems, such as [Australia](https://www.dailymail.co.uk/news/article-5899247/Australia-announces-change-points-general-skilled-migration.html), allow skilled workers like scientists to travel quite freely.
1.5.4.1.2. Con: In September 2019, PM Boris Johnson announced that he would create a [fast-track visa system](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/science-environment-49282689) to attract foreign scientists to UK institutions.
1.5.4.1.2.1. Con: Foreign scientists are unlikely to be persuaded to come to the UK just because the fast-track visa system makes it easier to get there because [there remain deeper issues](https://www.personneltoday.com/hr/uk-scientist-visa-post-brexit-boris-johnson/) faced by the scientific community upon arrival.
1.5.4.2. Pro: More than [half of all scientists](https://www.nature.com/news/brexit-vote-drives-uk-academics-to-think-about-leaving-1.21259) reported that Brexit had increased the likelihood of them leaving. This was even higher for non-UK EU citizens, at over three quarters.
1.5.4.2.1. Con: "More likely" does not mean "likely" or indicate how much more likely.
1.5.4.2.2. Con: It is common for people to overestimate the impact that political votes will have on their future actions.
1.5.4.3. Pro: [31,000 academics](https://www.nature.com/news/brexit-by-the-numbers-the-fear-of-brain-drain-1.21142), or about 16% of the university-based workforce, are non-UK EU citizens. A hard Brexit could jeopardise their residency rights.
1.5.4.3.1. Con: -> See 1.5.2.
1.5.4.3.2. Pro: Many academic staff currently do not earn [enough money](https://www.nature.com/news/brexit-by-the-numbers-the-fear-of-brain-drain-1.21142) to qualify for a tier 2 visa for skilled workers.
1.5.4.3.2.1. Con: As a sovereign country the UK can adjust its visa system to meet its needs.
1.5.4.4. Pro: Applications to UK-based scientific research programs by foreign nationals have dramatically [dropped](https://www.bloomberg.com/news/features/2017-08-08/brexit-guts-british-science-and-risks-graphene-innovation)(Every year Geim receives a steady flow of applications from abroad seeking to join the Marie Curie research program in Manchester. In 2017 he has received no applications at all.) since the referendum.
1.5.4.5. Con: -> See 1.5.4.1.2.
1.5.5. Pro: Brexit will reduce EU collaborations.
1.5.5.1. Con: -> See 1.5.1.
1.5.5.2. Con: Some notable collaborations, such as [CERN](http://www.epsnews.eu/2016/07/cern-statement-about-uk-referendum-on-the-eu/), are not contingent upon EU membership.
1.5.5.3. Pro: Outside the European Union, Britain will no longer [host](http://www.sciencemag.org/news/2016/06/researchers-deplore-uk-decision-leave-european-union) the European Medicines Agency, which evaluates medicinal products throughout the EU and has 890 employees. Instead the Agency and those scientists will [relocate](http://www.ema.europa.eu/docs/en_GB/document_library/Press_release/2017/11/WC500238862.pdf) to Amsterdam.
1.5.5.3.1. Con: This will not affect scientific research in the UK, and so only affects an extremely small proportion of the scientific community.
1.5.5.4. Con: Non-EU [scientists](https://www.newscientist.com/article/2091490-vote-brexit-and-british-science-will-still-be-a-powerhouse/) from Israel and Switzerland have been part of the European Research Council. There is no reason Britain's scientists cannot do the same after Brexit.
1.5.5.4.1. Pro: Israel and Switzerland win more [funding](https://www.timeshighereducation.com/championship-form-uk-leads-european-research-council-cash) per capita than UK researchers, and have similar success rates when attempting to obtain grants.
1.5.5.4.1.1. Con: Switzerland \(2.8 percent\) and Israel \(4.4 percent\) spend proportionally [more](https://www.theguardian.com/science/occams-corner/2016/apr/06/eu-brexit-scientific-impact-complicated) of their GDP on R&D than Britain \(1.7 percent\), making it easier for them to obtain research grants from the EU. It is unlikely that the UK will match them in spending anytime soon, given the economic consequences of Brexit.
1.5.5.4.2. Con: These associate members attend Research Council meetings in an [observer](https://www.theguardian.com/science/occams-corner/2016/apr/06/eu-brexit-scientific-impact-complicated) capacity only, and consequently their abilities to shape research policy are limited.
1.5.5.5. Pro: The UK [won](https://www.theguardian.com/science/occams-corner/2016/apr/06/eu-brexit-scientific-impact-complicated) research grants valued at €1.7 billion during the last funding period of the European Research Council. By contrast, Germany won grants valued at €1.1 billion.
1.5.5.5.1. Con: The ERC is not the only source of funding for UK scientific research, and so even if these funds are lost \(which is not necessarily the case as other non-EU countries receive ERC funds\) they could be compensated for through other sources.
1.5.5.5.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.5.5.4.1.
1.5.5.6. Pro: Between 2015 and 2019, the amount of EU science fellows choosing UK universities dropped from [33% to 22%](https://www.sciencemag.org/news/2019/10/split-decisions-how-brexit-has-taken-toll-five-researchers). This trend is likely to continue under a hard Brexit.
1.5.5.7. Pro: -> See 1.5.1.5.5.1.
1.5.6. Pro: Britain receives around 60% more money from the EU for research funding than it contributes to the EU for research \(Royal Society [pg. 12](https://royalsociety.org/~/media/policy/projects/eu-uk-funding/uk-membership-of-eu.pdf)\).
1.5.6.1. Con: It is unlikely that the loss of EU control over the funding of British science will cause any harm. UK universities were good at science before the UK joined the EU, and will likely continue to remain so.
1.5.6.2. Con: Since the UK is a [net contributor](https://researchbriefings.parliament.uk/ResearchBriefing/Summary/CBP-7886) to the EU to the tune of £8.9bn, so any EU money science programs lose can be replaced by the UK government funding them directly.
1.5.7. Con: The UK can have better scientific research outside the EU as evidenced by the fact that many countries outside the EU have better scientific research programmes than many countries inside the EU.
1.5.7.1. Con: Research in EU-member states has developed massively thanks to the presence of [EU-funded projects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marie_Sk%C5%82odowska-Curie_Actions) and its supra-national institutes \(such as [EMBL](https://www.embl.org/)\). Many of these have [premises in UK](https://www.ebi.ac.uk/). Brexit will change this mainly for UK, as researchers that are working in the aforementioned projects will relocate to EU-member states.
1.5.7.2. Con: [Causation/Correlation Fallacy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Correlation_does_not_imply_causation): The fact that other countries have better X \(scientific research programs\) than countries with characteristic Y \(membership within the European Union\) does not mean that success in X \(scientific research programs\) is causally related to Y \(membership within the European Union\) in any way.
1.5.7.3. Pro: Nations such as Australia and the United States currently [recruit](https://www.newscientist.com/article/2091490-vote-brexit-and-british-science-will-still-be-a-powerhouse/) more international researchers than Britain, which shows that membership in a supranational organization is not a prerequisite for success in this regard.
1.5.7.3.1. Con: False Equivalency: Australia and the United States are not Britain. This claim assumes that being removed from the EU will make Britain like the United States or Australia. There is no rational reason to make that assumption.
1.5.8. Con: The UK scientific community will no longer be beholden to burdensome EU regulations.
1.5.8.1. Con: There are relatively few EU regulations on research that the UK would not also implement.
1.5.8.1.1. Pro: Development of new biological weapons, heavily restricted under EU law, is unlikely given that the UK was the proposer of the original [Biological Weapons Convention](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biological_Weapons_Convention).
1.5.8.1.2. Pro: Additional EU law governing animal experimentation was pushed heavily by the UK. UK law was already strict, but [a central aim of the revised legislation was to raise standards of oversight and animal use in other Member States](https://royalsociety.org/~/media/policy/projects/eu-uk-funding/phase-3/EU-regulation-and-policy-in-governing-UK-research.pdf).
1.5.8.1.3. Pro: The UK has been far more stringent in its application of the [Clinical Trials Directive](https://royalsociety.org/~/media/policy/projects/eu-uk-funding/phase-3/EU-regulation-and-policy-in-governing-UK-research.pdf) than other EU members.
1.5.8.2. Pro: At present, EU state aid [regulations](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/technology/2018/11/19/britains-biotech-industry-stifled-strict-eu-aid-rules/) limit the ability of UK funding bodies to support transnational research from universities into start up companies. Leaving the EU will remove this problem.
1.5.8.3. Con: EU regulations ensure research is undertaken in a safe, sustainable, ethical and accountable way.
1.5.8.4. Con: For example, the 'burdensome' EU regulations that prevent pesticides that devastate biodiversity from being used freely across Europe, thereby protecting the environmental resources on which scientific research \(and a lot more\) depends. It's policies like these, that benefit everyone \(except the likes of Monsanto\)[, that the British government seeks to block](https://www.theguardian.com/environment/2017/jun/22/tories-aim-block-full-eu-ban-bee-harming-pesticides)
1.5.9. Con: The impact of a hard Brexit on the scientific community will likely be cushioned by increased domestic spending on UK science.
1.5.9.1. Pro: -> See 1.5.6.2.
1.5.9.2. Pro: The UK government has committed to investing [2.4% of GDP](https://www.ons.gov.uk/economy/governmentpublicsectorandtaxes/researchanddevelopmentexpenditure/bulletins/ukgrossdomesticexpenditureonresearchanddevelopment/2017) in the Research & Development \(R&D\) sector by 2027. This is significantly more than the current investment of 1.7%.
1.5.9.2.1. Con: The current government's spending of just 1.7% of GDP on R&D is already significantly less than the EU recommendation of [2.07%](https://www.ons.gov.uk/economy/governmentpublicsectorandtaxes/researchanddevelopmentexpenditure/bulletins/ukgrossdomesticexpenditureonresearchanddevelopment/2017). Therefore, the UK government has a history of falling short on R&D investment, suggesting that its commitment to 2.4% by 2027 may not be met.
1.5.9.2.2. Con: A lot can politically and economically change before 2027 so this commitment is not guaranteed.
1.5.9.3. Pro: By 2021, the UK government aims to boost investment in R&D by £7 billion, representing the largest ever increase in public funding of R&D by any UK government \([Industrial Strategy, p. 67](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/attachment_data/file/664563/industrial-strategy-white-paper-web-ready-version.pdf)\).
1.5.9.4. Pro: In 2016, [Chancellor Philip Hammond](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-37060430) announced that the UK Treasury would replace EU funds for scientists.
1.6. Con: The UK should be able to control and limit its immigration policy as it sees fit.
1.6.1. Con: The UK can sufficiently control immigration while remaining in the EU.
1.6.1.1. Pro: [EU regulations](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/reality-check/2016/mar/29/eu-dangerous-criminals-allowed-free-entry-uk-vote-leave-claims) already allow countries to reject people with a criminal record.
1.6.1.1.1. Con: [According to the same source](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/reality-check/2016/mar/29/eu-dangerous-criminals-allowed-free-entry-uk-vote-leave-claims), the law states “previous criminal convictions shall not in themselves constitute grounds for taking such measures” \(i.e., for refusing entry to such a person\), adding extra standards for evaluating criminals on a case-by-case basis.
1.6.1.1.1.1. Con: This is a positive thing, as it gives choice and control to UK border staff. Either alternative \(no ability to stop criminals / no ability to accept criminals\) would be worse for the UK than the current situation: getting to decide itself.
1.6.1.1.2. Pro: Convicted criminals can be [excluded](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/reality-check/2016/mar/29/eu-dangerous-criminals-allowed-free-entry-uk-vote-leave-claims) on a case-by-case basis if they present “a genuine, present and sufficiently serious threat affecting one of the fundamental interests of society”.
1.6.1.2. Pro: Member States, like the UK, can restrict entry and residence of EU citizens on grounds of public policy or public security \([art 27 and further CD](https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/?uri=CELEX:32004L0038)\). This is understood as 'genuine, present and sufficiently serious threat affecting one of the fundamental interests of society'. Although to be interpreted restrictively, based on the personal conduct of the individual, it is to be read in light of the national needs of the Member State. \(see cases 30/77[Bouchereau](http://curia.europa.eu/juris/showPdf.jsf;jsessionid=9ea7d2dc30dd789656d3536448358ccef87255e02c57.e34KaxiLc3qMb40Rch0SaxyOa3j0?text=&docid=89572&pageIndex=0&doclang=EN&mode=lst&dir=&occ=first&part=1&cid=233337) and 41/74 [Van Duyn](https://eur-lex.europa.eu/legal-content/EN/TXT/?uri=CELEX%3A61974CJ0041)\)
1.6.1.3. Con: The number of Romanians and Bulgarians living in the UK [has increased by over 80% since 2014](https://www.express.co.uk/news/uk/964654/Romanians-UK-immigration-levels-2018-EU-Blair-Poles-immigrants) to over 400,000 as a result of EU open border immigration laws. This is unprecedented, was not anticipated \(even Nigel farage underestimated the numbers that would come\) and would not have happened had the UK had full control of immigration.
1.6.1.3.1. Con: It was consecutive UK governments who decided not to restrict residency rights without proof of sustainable work of EU immigrants. The EU is not responsible for the UK lack of regulations. Immigrants who work and pay taxes however contribute hugely to the UK economy.
1.6.1.4. Pro: The UK [already](http://ec.europa.eu/social/main.jsp?catId=460) has the [ability](http://ukandeu.ac.uk/fact-figures/what-is-freedom-of-movement/) to control immigration. It has chosen not to. The fact that there is no enforcement of these rules in the UK is not the fault of of the EU.
1.6.1.4.1. Pro: Other EU nations like Austria currently [restrict EU migrants](https://www.gov.uk/guidance/living-in-austria#visas-and-residency) using existing EU laws. Theresa May merely chose not to do so when she was Home Secretary. Brexit was never necessary to restrict migration from the EU.
1.6.1.4.1.1. Con: Austria's laws only restrict EU migrants that aren't working/studying/able to support themselves. The vast majority of EU migrants to the UK are employed, and so these laws would do next to nothing to restrict migration from the EU. Therefore Brexit is absolutely necessary to restrict EU migration.
1.6.1.4.1.1.1. Con: There is no consensus between Leave voters as to what controls on immigration should be put in place. Some insist that immigration was never the issue, some want only to restrict the unemployed being a burden on the country, others go much further insisting that we have "No room". If migrants are working then they are contributing to British society and paying towards new schools, housing, the NHS, etc.
1.6.1.4.2. Pro: The UK government has failed to implement the measures allowed by the EU, when it could have. Brexit will enable it to continue to fail to do so. [Farage Admits UK could have controlled EU immigration.](https://twitter.com/Femi_Sorry/status/938456838940643332)
1.6.1.4.3. Pro: The UK previously chose to let in higher numbers of EU migrants than they had to, as the result of their own immigration policy.
1.6.1.4.3.1. Pro: The UK was [one of only three](https://theconversation.com/the-huge-political-cost-of-blairs-decision-to-allow-eastern-european-migrants-unfettered-access-to-britain-66077) EU member states, alongside Sweden and Ireland, to open its labour market to the new 2004 Eastern European EU citizens \(EU10, including the A8\) immediately.
1.6.1.4.3.1.1. Pro: [EU rules allowed](http://www.ucl.ac.uk/~uctpb21/reports/HomeOffice25_03.pdf) existing member states 2+3+2 years of their own restrictions, and to suspend free movement of workers if they experienced or anticipated serious disturbances to their labour market.
1.6.1.4.3.2. Pro: [Tony Blair made a 2004 deal](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/uknews/1458492/Revealed-Blair-made-deal-with-Romanian-PM-to-let-in-migrants.html) with Romanian prime minister Adrian Nastase [accepting more migrants](https://www.express.co.uk/news/uk/964654/Romanians-UK-immigration-levels-2018-EU-Blair-Poles-immigrants) in order to manipulate Asylum application figures to make good on earlier promises, rather than trying to resolve the problem properly.
1.6.1.4.3.2.1. Con: Blair's deal was short lived, and it was reversed by the UK government in [2006](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2006/oct/25/immigrationandpublicservices.eu). As a consequence of Romania then joining the EU and EU migration law, the UK government lost the power to restrict migration from Romania from [2014](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/world-europe-25565302), tying the hands of future UK governments who were then unable to change it despite the [democratic desire to do so](http://migrationobservatory.ox.ac.uk/resources/briefings/uk-public-opinion-toward-immigration-overall-attitudes-and-level-of-concern/). This illustrates the fundamentally undemocratic way the EU project operates.
1.6.1.4.3.3. Pro: Opening borders to eastern european migrants was in keeping with Labours 1997–2010 immigration policies, which had always focused on [expanding immigration and removing restrictions](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/1369183X.2015.1045711?journalCode=cjms20), to encourage the economic benefits of increased migration.
1.6.1.4.3.3.1. Pro: Under Blair’s Labour government, Britain’s economic immigration policy went from a highly restrictive approach to [one of the most expansive in Europe](https://theconversation.com/how-new-labour-made-britain-into-a-migration-state-85472). It resulted in Immigration five times higher than under the previous government.
1.6.1.4.3.4. Pro: EU rules allow the UK to require migrants to become [self-sufficient within 3 months](http://www.eearegulations.co.uk/CitizensRightsDirective/ByPage/Article_07) of arriving \(excluding students and families\), but the [UK does not](https://www.huffingtonpost.co.uk/richard-bird/immigration-blame-the-uk-_b_13120104.html) register migrants as they arrive and as such has no way of knowing how long they have been in the UK.
1.6.1.4.3.4.1. Pro: There are no efforts to track or control this movement. The [Home Office has admitted](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/worldnews/europe/eu/10361971/Britain-admits-it-has-no-figures-on-EU-welfare-tourist-numbers.html) it does not even know how many EU citizens are in Britain and eligible for welfare.
1.6.1.4.4. Pro: The UK, Ireland and Denmark [have opt-outs](http://eulawanalysis.blogspot.com/2015/05/can-uk-opt-out-of-mandatory-eu-refugee.html?m=1) from parts of EU justice and home affairs \(JHA\) policy. They could use those opt-outs to avoid the European Agenda on Migrations mandatory burden-sharing, if they wanted to.
1.6.1.4.5. Con: The only instance in which the UK can deny entry to an EU citizen is on security grounds, such as when the person who tries to enter is a criminal. Other than that, the UK has absolutely no control over the people that seek entry to the UK. And as for the people who want to stay in the UK, but they don't satisfy the conditions pointed out in [Article 7 of the Free Movement Directive](http://www.eearegulations.co.uk/CitizensRightsDirective/ByPage/Article_07), they can still enter if they have a family member residing in the UK that satisfies these conditions.
1.6.1.5. Pro: EU citizens only have the right to reside in the UK for longer than 3 months if they work, or if they are self-sufficient \(including dependents\), students, or accompanying family members - [Article 7 of the Citizenship Directive](http://www.eearegulations.co.uk/CitizensRightsDirective/ByPage/Article_07).
1.6.1.5.1. Con: While the claim is true that as a nation we could assert controls on EU migrants who are not financially independent, we're reliant on EU law to do this and we would still be restricted in our ability to control the flow of EU workers.
1.6.1.5.2. Con: EU spouses of UK citizens who earn less than £18,600 are still able to live in the UK and burden the social system, whereas [non-EU spouses are not](http://migrationobservatory.ox.ac.uk/resources/reports/the-minimum-income-requirement-for-non-eea-family-members-in-the-uk-2/).
