1.6.1.5.2.1. Con: EU-originating benefit tourism was not one of the major factors in the Brexit referendum, and so is largely incidental to this debate.
1.6.1.5.2.1.1. Con: Benefit tourism is important to the electorate, and therefore plays an important part in the publics decision to Leave/Remain. [76%](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/uknews/immigration/10570559/The-public-crave-a-crackdown-on-welfare-tourism.-But-are-they-deluded.html) said they would support a two year benefits ban for immigrants \(2014\).
1.6.1.5.3. Pro: -> See 1.6.1.4.3.4.
1.6.1.5.4. Pro: The UK [could limit 'benefit tourism'](https://brexit853.wordpress.com/2016/09/27/powers-that-the-uk-has-failed-to-use-to-control-eu-freedom-of-movement-directive/) from EU countries if it desired to do so, by using [Article 7 of the Citizenship Directive](http://www.eearegulations.co.uk/CitizensRightsDirective/ByPage/Article_07).
1.6.1.5.4.1. Con: -> See 1.6.1.5.2.1.
1.6.1.5.4.2. Pro: -> See 1.6.1.4.3.4.
1.6.1.5.4.3. Con: There are many other reasons to exclude someone from coming to your country other than that they will be a burden on your state. Two reasons in particular would be if they were not a good cultural fit, or they posed a national security risk.
1.6.1.5.4.3.1. Con: -> See 1.6.1.1.
1.6.1.5.4.3.2. Con: -> See 1.6.1.2.
1.6.1.5.5. Pro: The UK does not need to leave the EU in order to deport migrants after 3 months unless they can demonstrate that they are employed/self-sustaining. EU rules already permit member countries to do this, but successive British governments have opted not to implement or enforce this rule. The 3 month permitted period facilitates short term travel e.g. tourism. Migrants are not eligible for any state benefit during this time so that such visitors are in no way a cost to the UK.
1.6.1.5.6. Con: It is not enough for the UK to maintain partial control. These rules significantly infringe on the UK's ability to regulate immigration as it sees fit, which de-prioritizes the national interest.
1.6.1.5.6.1. Pro: An uncontrolled influx of cheap labour suppresses wages and benefits business owners at the expense of the working classes.
1.6.1.5.6.1.1. Con: There is a national minimum wage employers have to abide by, failure to do this for EU citizens would be breaking the law. Obviously this is harder to account for being paid for 'cash in hand' but this is something to target those businesses and traders over, not those willing to work.
1.6.1.5.6.1.2. Pro: Studies find a correlation between an influx of migrants and lower native wage levels.
1.6.1.5.6.1.2.1. Pro: The Bank of England [found](https://fullfact.org/immigration/does-immigration-reduce-wages/) that a 10% increase in the proportion of foreign born workers in lower paid service jobs was associated with a near 2% fall in average pay for those jobs, when focusing on particular regions.
1.6.1.5.6.1.2.1.1. Con: The study says: "This is a relatively small effect" comparing it to "global changes in technology and trade, or national differences in education, attitudes to gender, and union activity".
1.6.1.5.6.1.2.1.2. Con: It's possible that companies would go out of business or [reduce](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-47083214) the [scale](https://www.gov.scot/publications/sectoral-impact-analysis-brexit-readiness-assessment/pages/5/) of their business without seasonal migrant workers and thus that many people \(including natives\) would be left without an income at all.
1.6.1.5.6.1.2.1.2.1. Pro: The population as a whole will also be without the product of those businesses, much of which will be locally produced food.
1.6.1.5.6.1.2.1.2.1.1. Pro: This will increase carbon emissions as produce is transported further.
1.6.1.5.6.1.2.2. Con: A meta study on the subject found concensus that whilst this effect is statistically signifigant, it is much [smaller](https://papers.tinbergen.nl/04134.pdf) than what is expected.
1.6.1.5.6.1.2.3. Con: A study [Borjas \(2003, p1335\)](https://www.jstor.org/stable/25053941?seq=1#page_scan_tab_contents) found that "the measured impact of immigration on the wage of native workers fluctuates widely from study to study, but seems to cluster around zero".
1.6.1.5.6.1.2.4. Con: The impacts of immigration on the labour market [critically depend](https://migrationobservatory.ox.ac.uk/resources/briefings/the-labour-market-effects-of-immigration/) on the skills of migrants, the skills of existing workers, and the characteristics of the host economy. They also differ between the short and long run when the economy and labour demand can adjust to the increase in labour supply.
1.6.1.5.6.1.3. Pro: Studies show access to migrant labour benefits business.
1.6.1.5.6.1.3.1. Pro: The [IMF](https://blogs.imf.org/2016/10/24/migrants-bring-economic-benefits-for-advanced-economies/) argues that migration makes sense economically, regardless of the political considerations.
1.6.1.5.6.1.4. Con: The impact of migration on the wages of the UK-born in the semi-skilled and unskilled sector since 2004 has been about [1 percent](https://www.ft.com/content/0deacb52-178b-11e6-9d98-00386a18e39d), over a period of eight years. This is not only an insignificantly small decrease over a large period of time, it is certainly not something worth trading off the benefits of migration for.
1.6.1.5.6.1.4.1. Con: It is easy to say that wage repression is insignificant when it is not yourself on the breadline. The studies referred to in the parent shows that immigration reduces wages at the very bottom where small amounts matter and takes no account of inflation: 1% actual decrease is quite a significant loss in real terms against rising costs and prices.
1.6.1.5.6.2. Pro: An uncontrolled flux of migrant workers makes it difficult for Government to plan for and train citizens in critical industries to prevent skills shortages.
1.6.1.5.6.2.1. Con: The UK has [skills shortages](http://www.theirishworld.com/skills-shortages-eu-workers-shun-brexit-britain/) and [requires](https://www.bigissue.com/latest/plugging-the-skills-gap-how-brexit-will-impact-construction-and-health-care/) [migrant workers](https://www.thetimes.co.uk/article/fewer-eu-migrants-increases-skills-shortages-pstf226g8) to fill [key roles](https://www.gov.scot/publications/scottish-government-response-migration-advisory-committee-2018-19-call-evidence-shortage-occupation-list/pages/4/) now.
1.6.1.6. Pro: [The majority](http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/election-2017-40015269) of immigration to the UK comes from non-EU countries, which the UK can already control.
1.6.1.6.1. Con: The EU accounts for less than 10% of the world's population, therefore it is inappropriate for it to account for over 40% of UK migrants.
1.6.1.6.1.1. Con: It is only natural for neighboring countries to account for more migrants, especially when it's easier.
1.6.1.6.1.2. Con: This is a very simplistic statistic that doesn't address real world issues and circumstances. It's far less likely for people from African nations to even be able to afford to even come to Britain whilst people from South America are far more likely to migrate to the USA due to geography.
1.6.1.6.1.3. Con: People have a natural tendency to migrate locally, both because they tend to share common cultural and linguistic roots and because of geographical logistics.
1.6.1.6.1.4. Con: There is no global standard governing the appropriateness or otherwise of the nationality mix of any nation's migrant population.
1.6.1.6.1.5. Con: The EU accounts for a much greater share of the world's skilled labour force and has many workers with particular specialised skills, which the UK requires.
1.6.1.6.2. Pro: UK Immigration statistics [categorize](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2017/apr/04/overseas-students-migrants-universities-brexit) overseas students as migrants, but as many of these students will subsequently leave the UK, this overly inflates migrant numbers.
1.6.1.6.2.1. Con: The [ONS found that](https://www.ons.gov.uk/peoplepopulationandcommunity/populationandmigration/internationalmigration/articles/whatshappeningwithinternationalstudentmigration/2017-08-24) many people do not simply immigrate for study and leave afterwards; their lives are more complex – some people arrive on a work visa and legitimately change to a study visa and vice versa.
1.6.1.6.3. Pro: Only 2.3m of the UK's current 5.5m foreign born workers are from the EU [\(source\)](https://www.solution-loans.co.uk/blog/why-the-uk-should-remain-in-the-eu/) and in the period 1993 to 2017 only 49% of the increase was due to EU born nationals. Leaving the EU will only partially address any perceived immigration issue.
1.6.1.7. Con: -> See 1.6.1.5.6.
1.6.2. Pro: Consistent [polling](http://www.bsa.natcen.ac.uk/latest-report/british-social-attitudes-31/immigration/introduction.aspx) has shown that a sizable majority believe the UK's immigration levels are too high.
1.6.2.1. Pro: The BSA poll is well respected and has been [running since 1983](http://www.bsa.natcen.ac.uk/about/about.aspx).
1.6.2.1.1. Con: How respected a poll is does not take into account that people's perception of immigration is not a solid basis to enact immigration policies.
1.6.2.2. Con: Polling the electorate on their beliefs about the levels of immigration is not a valid method of establishing the correct levels or possible effects of immigration, because individual perceptions can be influenced by the media and scaremongering.
1.6.2.2.1. Pro: Actually, it's true that British people feel they're hosting [more immigrants than those who they really host](https://www.theguardian.com/news/datablog/2014/oct/29/todays-key-fact-you-are-probably-wrong-about-almost-everything).
1.6.2.2.2. Con: It should be noted however, that the opinions of those that compete for services with migrants are the ones whose lives are most affected by immigration. We should be careful not to neglect the feelings of some people based, for example, on your opinion of the newspaper they read.
1.6.2.2.2.1. Con: Societal fears should not be a factor in policy making. Misperceptions should be mitigated through correct, responsible information to those sectors of the society by the institutions.
1.6.2.3. Con: Public perception and concern over immigration levels have little resemblance to the actual levels of immigration.[LSE Blog - Perceptions and Reality](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/politicsandpolicy/perceptions-and-reality-ten-things-we-should-know-about-attitudes-to-immigration-in-the-uk/)
1.6.2.4. Con: Polls with public results alter perceptions and public opinion by making the majority opinion observable to people who may be influenced when reacting to that same issue.
1.6.2.5. Con: Comparing attitudes before and after the referendum from within the same groups of individuals suggests that both Leavers and Remainers have [softened in their attitudes towards immigration](https://migrationobservatory.ox.ac.uk/resources/briefings/uk-public-opinion-toward-immigration-overall-attitudes-and-level-of-concern/).
1.6.2.5.1. Pro: This has been quantified at 19% softening over 4 years. Inconsistent question wording makes historical comparisons difficult, but good Ipsos MORI data since 2013 suggests that there has been a recent softening of attitudes with just 45% agreeing that there are too many immigrants in 2017 compared to 64% just 4 years earlier. [Figure 2](https://migrationobservatory.ox.ac.uk/resources/briefings/uk-public-opinion-toward-immigration-overall-attitudes-and-level-of-concern/#kp3)
1.6.2.6. Con: Immigration policy should not be based on what some people in the country think the immigration levels should be, but rather what evidence indicates would be best.
1.6.3. Pro: The possibility that Turkey, an EU candidate country that borders war-torn Syria and has a large population, may join the EU is a major [concern](https://www.nytimes.com/2016/06/14/world/europe/britain-brexit-turkey-eu.html) to many British Eurosceptics.
1.6.3.1. Con: If Turkey, or any other country, wants to join the EU, they must first [integrate EU policy spanning over 35 areas](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-eu-referendum-35832035). These include human rights policies and freedom of expression. In 10 years Turkey has only managed to align with the EU on two policy areas: science and research. This not only proves that Turkey is not currently anywhere close to joining the EU but also shows that if a country such as Turkey were to join, it would be in a state where its values and interests align with the UK's.
1.6.3.2. Con: Polls in Turkey have shown mixed support for joining the EU and the support has [decreased](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accession_of_Turkey_to_the_European_Union#Population) over the past 10 years.
1.6.3.3. Con: The UK have a veto on whether Turkey ever joined the EU.
1.6.3.4. Con: The EU is [unlikely](https://www.reuters.com/article/us-germany-turkey-eu-factbox/factbox-turkeys-collapsing-eu-membership-bid-idUSKCN1BF1TH) to admit Turkey under its current political [situation](http://www.bbc.com/news/world-europe-34503388) with or without the UK. Moreover, the UK \(while historically in favor of Turkey's accession\) would [probably](http://www.dw.com/en/turkey-eu-relations-which-countries-are-for-or-against-turkish-accession/a-40381533) be against it if it remains and would have [veto](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/penny-mordaunt-andrew-marr-uk-veto-tory-minister-accused-of-flat-out-lying-over-turkey-joining-the-a7041956.html) power over its accession.
1.6.3.5. Con: The reasons why Eurosceptics object to Turkish EU membership are largely unjustified and based on racial fears. Even if the UK should be allowed to regulate its own immigration policy, these would be illegitimate reasons to exclude Turkey.
1.6.3.5.1. Con: Turkish President Erdogan's [purge on media](https://www.aljazeera.com/programmes/listeningpost/2018/07/erdogan-media-turks-care-180701092502264.html) and the [2017 referendum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkish_constitutional_referendum,_2017) granting him further control over government institutions are not compatible with EU democratic standards and anti-corruption efforts.
1.6.4. Con: The EU is not a third party imposing an immigration policy on the UK. As a member of the EU, the UK was involved in the drafting of, and signed up to, the [Maastricht Treaty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maastricht_Treaty) which introduced the notion of EU citizenship, and the right of any citizen to reside anywhere within the Union. The UK was exercising its right to decide its immigration policy by introducing free movement \(along with its fellow EU-members\).
1.6.5. Con: The EU is [blamed](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/boris-johnson-says-uncontrolled-immigration-from-eu-is-driving-down-wages-and-putting-pressure-on-a6948346.html) for uncontrolled immigration, yet the EU does have laws that do this that the UK has failed to enact.
1.6.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.6.1.
1.6.6. Con: The UK has full control over the flow of non-EU migrants, which accounts for over [80% of the UK's recent immigrant intake](https://www.ons.gov.uk/peoplepopulationandcommunity/populationandmigration/internationalmigration/bulletins/migrationstatisticsquarterlyreport/february2019#non-eu-net-migration-has-increased-while-eu-net-migration-has-decreased). A failure to control controllable immigration gives a strong indication that EU immigration would not be significantly reduced by Brexit.
1.6.6.1. Pro: -> See 1.6.1.6.
1.6.7. Pro: This would allow the UK to implement a [merit-based](http://www.euronews.com/2017/09/06/watch-live-pm-theresa-may-faces-mps-questions-for-first-time-since-summer-break) immigration policy which, due to strict policing and mandatory quotas, would be impossible under EU regulations.
1.6.7.1. Con: Successive UK Governments have not applied all the measures that are available to police immigration from either without or within the EU.  Under resourced Customs and Immigration departments UK wide are the [problem](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/2016/05/18/border-force-funding-cut-by-quarter-per-person-arriving-to-uk/), not EU membership as such.
1.6.7.1.1. Pro: Immigration departments in Britain do not have any figures as to how many legal EU and non-EU migrants are currently living in Britain [Link](https://www.express.co.uk/news/uk/845163/Immigration-figures-wrong-again-Home-Office-scramble-to-get-numbers-right). The Windrush scandal happened because of poor immigration records and Theresa May's "Hostile Environment" policies.
1.6.8. Con: By leaving the EU, the UK has to sign free trade deals with other nations, many of whom may require increased freedom of movement of people, which could potentially offset any decreased immigration from the EU.
1.6.8.1. Pro: India has [stated](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-latest-australia-india-tell-uk-relax-immigration-rules-free-trade-deal-eu-visa-restrictions-a7540036.html) that it cannot separate free movement of people from the free flow of goods, services and investments, indicating that any free trade deal will likely require increased immigration from India.
1.6.8.1.1. Con: This statement was from "a senior Indian official", rather than an official red line. It should be seen as a posturing/bargaining point, rather than a certainty.
1.6.8.2. Pro: Australia has [said](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-latest-australia-india-tell-uk-relax-immigration-rules-free-trade-deal-eu-visa-restrictions-a7540036.html) that it wants to liberalise immigration visa policies for Australians wanting to come to the UK.
1.6.8.3. Con: This becomes a matter that can then be controlled by the UK government and renegotiated as necessary, and so is a desirable outcome for those who voted to regain control of the UK's borders.
1.6.8.4. Con: It is highly unlikely any liberalisation of visas will be as expansive as the EU's free movement of people policies.
1.6.8.5. Con: The UK controlling their own immigration policy doesn't necessarily mean it will limit immigration. Perhaps the UK will set such immigration policies as to avoid costs of low immigration or to obtain a free trade agreement with India or Australia.
1.6.8.5.1. Con: Much of the initial impetus of Brexit was around immigration being too high, impacting housing and infrastructure like schools and the NHS. Suggesting that we could have the same or more immigration as a result of Brexit damages the whole "will of the people" argument.
1.6.9. Pro: An open immigration policy with the EU puts the UK's economy at risk.
1.6.9.1. Pro: The UK needs the power to restrict entry into the country in order to maintain its ability to provide for the needs of its citizens in the future, otherwise an unemployment crisis in the rest of the EU could burden the UK with an influx of people in need.
1.6.9.1.1. Con: -> See 1.6.1.5.
1.6.9.1.2. Con: According to \[1\], EU migrants contributed on average £2,300 more than the average contribution to UK public finances. UK-born adults contribute on average £70 less than the average and non-EU migrants contribute on average £800 less than the average. On average, EU migrants therefore are less of a burden to the system than UK-born. In fact they counter the burden that UK-born and non-EU migrants pose on the country thus restricting their access to the UK would be detrimental. [\[1\]](https://www.oxfordeconomics.com/recent-releases/8747673d-3b26-439b-9693-0e250df6dbba)
1.6.9.2. Con: The level of unemployment of the British state was on a [downwards](https://www.ons.gov.uk/employmentandlabourmarket/peoplenotinwork/unemployment/timeseries/mgsx/lms) trend even when taking in a significant influx of immigrants prior to the initial vote of in or out.
1.6.9.3. Con: Market forces are what control immigration in the EU, making the countries with high economic worth more appealing to the best talent that the EU has to offer. For the UK to compete for these key roles, frictionless employment is essential.
1.6.9.3.1. Con: Once we have free trade deals with countries such as India, which will include free movement of workers, employers will be free to recruit workers from whichever nation can supply them the cheapest, whereas if the UK remains in the EU, it will be caught by [new EU regulations](https://www.independent.co.uk/voices/eu-brexit-uk-labour-laws-migrant-workers-a8375836.html) which prevent immigrant labour from undercutting the local workforce.
1.6.9.4. Con: The free movement of people between the UK and the rest of the EU is actually economically beneficial for the UK.
1.6.9.4.1. Pro: The USA is commonly called the most economically powerful country in the world, and has the [highest number of migrants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_sovereign_states_and_dependent_territories_by_immigrant_population) \(46 million\).
1.6.9.4.1.1. Con: Correlation does not necessarily mean causation - immigration might not be a factor causing success, but a reaction to success.
1.6.9.4.1.1.1. Pro: Immigrants naturally flock away from less successful countries towards more successful countries.
1.6.9.4.1.2. Pro: A survey of leading economists shows a consensus behind the view that high-skilled immigration makes the average American better off. A survey of the same economists also shows strong support behind the notion that low-skilled [immigration makes the average American better off](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_States#Economic).
1.6.9.4.1.2.1. Con: The predictions of "economists" in general are not credible. Specific economists with demonstrable track records of predictive success may be more credible.
1.6.9.4.1.2.1.1. Pro: The predictions and views of surveyed economists are so often [misleading or plain wrong.](https://www.theguardian.com/money/2017/sep/02/economic-forecasting-flawed-science-data)
1.6.9.4.1.2.1.2. Pro: [Economics is not a science](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/finance/comment/10390981/Time-to-stop-this-pretence-economics-is-not-science.html) or -at least- economists are not scientists.
1.6.9.4.1.2.1.3. Pro: Economists have been found guilty of [groupthink, guided by political ends and using error-prone gravity modelling](https://www.theguardian.com/business/economics-blog/2017/jan/08/economic-forecasts-hardwired-get-things-wrong).
1.6.9.4.1.3. Pro: America, the promised land, [was built](https://www.huffingtonpost.com/entry/immigrants-make-america-great-because-immigrants-made_us_5928a40ce4b08861ed0cc96d) on the backs of immigrants. It's melting pot of migrants is a huge part of it's conception, history, and success.
1.6.9.4.1.3.1. Pro: Because the United States is a settler colonial society, the vast majority of Americans, with the exception of Native Americans, [can trace their ancestry to immigrants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immigration_to_the_United_States) from other nations.
1.6.9.4.1.4. Pro: The other countries with next highest immigration levels are also all economically strong countries:  Germany \(12m\), Russia \(11m\), Saudi Arabia \(10m\), UK \(8m\).
1.6.9.4.1.4.1. Con: The economies of Russia and Saudi Arabia are massively reliant on oil and natural gas, and so in addition to this being something unattributable to migration, the lack of diversification makes the economies prone to economic shock and underlying strength may be illusory.
1.6.9.4.1.4.2. Pro: Germany, UK, and Russia are in the [top 5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Financial_and_social_rankings_of_sovereign_states_in_Europe) economies in Europe, by nominal and PPP GDP.
1.6.9.4.1.4.2.1. Con: Russia is [46th](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Financial_and_social_rankings_of_sovereign_states_in_Europe) out of 50 European countries by measure of GDP growth rate, and [31st](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Financial_and_social_rankings_of_sovereign_states_in_Europe) out of 50 by GDP per capita.
1.6.9.4.1.4.3. Pro: The Saudi economy is the [largest](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economy_of_Saudi_Arabia) in the Arab world.
1.6.9.4.1.5. Con: The USA does not have free movement of people with the EU, indicating free movement with  the EU is in no way a prerequisite for a country to be economically  powerful.
1.6.9.4.2. Con: The free movement of people may have been economically beneficial for the UK in the past, however the law of diminishing returns suggests that the economic benefits are being increasingly eroded by the associated societal costs, as negative externalities caused by population pressures mount. This is what has happened in the UK.
1.6.9.4.2.1. Pro: Immigration has been the [largest driver](https://fullfact.org/health/increasing-population-households/) of the increasing population, according to the Office for National Statistics.
1.6.9.4.2.1.1. Con: Given that the average birthrate in the UK is [1.7 children per](https://www.onaverage.co.uk/other-averages/average-number-of-children) couple, the UK needs immigration to reach a stable population replacement rate.
1.6.9.4.2.2. Pro: In 2010, a cross-party group including MPs, poverty experts, economic historians, and the former Archbishop of Canterbury issued a [“Declaration on Population”](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2016/feb/09/is-britain-full-home-truths-about-population-panic). It warned that a UK population of 70 million – which will arrive about 2030, based on current trends – “would be seriously damaging to the future harmony of our society.”
1.6.9.4.2.3. Con: The [UK's ageing population](https://www.parliament.uk/business/publications/research/key-issues-parliament-2015/social-change/ageing-population/) makes the free movement of people advantageous to the long-term strategic requirements of the UK population by providing working age migrants to do the necessary jobs, care for the elderly and contribute to public service funding.
1.6.9.4.2.3.1. Pro: [EU migrants](http://bruegel.org/2016/06/what-is-the-age-profile-of-uk-immigrants/) are younger and more likely to be in work compared to the average member of the population, and so pay more tax and rely on state services less.
1.6.9.4.2.4. Con: The "negative externalities", such as housing and transport issues, are planning failures. The failure of government to properly allocate taxes to manage infrastructure investment requirements has little to do with immigration.
1.6.9.4.3. Con: Whether or not it is beneficial in an economic sense does not affect whether or not the UK should have the right to control it.
1.6.9.4.4. Pro: Migrants make a positive net contribution to the economy.
1.6.9.4.4.1. Pro: Data analysis of 30 years of data from 15 countries in Western Europe shows that [migrants positively affect the economy within a year of arrival](http://advances.sciencemag.org/content/4/6/eaaq0883), continuing to have significant impact for 2+ years.
1.6.9.4.4.2. Pro: In America, immigrants are [net fiscal contributors](http://www8.nationalacademies.org/onpinews/newsitem.aspx?RecordID=23550) on the federal level.
1.6.9.4.4.3. Pro: Researchers generally agree that immigrants from the European Economic Area \(EEA\) and recent immigrants are, as groups, [more likely to have a positive net fiscal impact](https://fullfact.org/immigration/how-immigrants-affect-public-finances/).
1.6.9.4.4.3.1. Pro: A [2014 UCL study](http://www.cream-migration.org/files/FiscalEJ.pdf) estimated that during the years 1995-2011 the total fiscal impact of EEA migrants in the UK was about +£0.3 billion per year, but the total fiscal impact of non-EEA migrants for this period was estimated at -£6.9 billion per year. The UK-born population impact was -£34.8 billion per year.
1.6.9.4.4.4. Con: A [report](https://www.ippr.org/files/images/media/files/publication/2011/05/Paying%20Their%20Way_1352.pdf?noredirect=1) from the Institute for Public Policy Research \(IPPR\) in 2005 found that expenditure attributed to immigrants grew by more than the revenue from them, resulting in a negative impact of -£0.4 billion in 2003-4.
1.6.9.4.4.4.1. Con: During this time period the report found that the UK born population also had a negative fiscal impact, and their [impact was higher](https://fullfact.org/immigration/how-immigrants-affect-public-finances/) than the immigrant impact.
1.6.9.4.4.5. Con: [MigrationWatch UK studies](https://www.migrationwatchuk.org/briefing-paper/7) show that depending on how you account for dependent children or the elderly, migrants give a negative net contribution.
1.6.9.4.4.5.1. Pro: If costs from children born to one immigrant and one UK-born parent are [shared between both categories](https://fullfact.org/immigration/how-immigrants-affect-public-finances/), MigrationWatch estimates show that the net fiscal impact of immigrants was negative \(-£1 billion for 1999-2000 and -£5 billion for 2003-2004\).
1.6.9.4.4.5.1.1. Con: These UK born children should count as fully UK born, not half immigrant, because they are UK citizens.
1.6.9.4.4.5.1.2. Pro: Alternative estimates allocating all children of mixed couples to the immigrant group show the fiscal cost of migrants to be [much higher](https://fullfact.org/immigration/how-immigrants-affect-public-finances/) \(around -£3.8 billion in 1999-2000\).
1.6.9.4.4.5.2. Pro: A Migration Watch UK [paper](https://www.migrationwatchuk.org/briefing-paper/381) looking at the 2014/15 period found that if you look at the Working Age population only, the UK-born population was the only group that \(out of EEA, Non-EEA, UK Born\) [did make a positive fiscal contribution](https://fullfact.org/immigration/how-immigrants-affect-public-finances/).
1.6.9.4.4.5.2.1. Con: It is fairer to look at the whole age range than a narrow one, as increases in dependents/the elderly has a significant effect, and all effects of migration should be considered.
1.6.9.4.4.5.3. Con: Migration Watch is an [advocacy group](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/MigrationWatch_UK) that argues for increased restrictions on immigration to the United Kingdom. It is likely to select and report data in a biased fashion.
1.6.9.4.4.6. Pro: HMRC released a [report](https://www.gov.uk/government/statistics/tax-contribution-of-recently-arrived-eea-nationals-for-2013-to-2014) in May 2016, which said that recently arrived EEA nationals –those who arrived within the previous 4 years – received £0.56 billion in tax credits and Child Benefit in the 2013/14 fiscal year but paid £3.11 billion in income taxes and national insurance contributions.
1.6.9.4.4.6.1. Con: This report ignored taxes like VAT and Council Tax, benefits like Housing Benefit and JSA, and use of a range of public services, and so is not a good indicator of financial cost/benefit.
1.6.9.4.4.7. Pro: In 2013 the Office for Budget Responsibility \(OBR\) predicted that in the long term \(50 years\) [higher net immigration would reduce pressure on government debt](http://obr.uk/docs/dlm_uploads/2013-FSR_OBR_web.pdf).
1.6.9.4.4.8. Pro: The [OECD found that](http://www.oecd-ilibrary.org/social-issues-migration-health/international-migration-outlook-2013_migr_outlook-2013-en) migration in the UK contributed +0.46% of GDP.
1.6.9.4.4.8.1. Pro: The UK benefits more than average from migration, receiving +0.11% higher GDP due to migration than the average \(+0.35% of GDP\).
1.6.9.4.4.9. Con: -> See 1.6.1.5.6.1.2.
1.6.9.4.4.10. Pro: -> See 1.6.1.5.6.1.2.1.2.
1.6.9.4.4.11. Pro: -> See 1.6.1.5.6.1.3.
1.6.9.4.5. Pro: Engineered austerity, not immigration, is [behind](https://www.newyorker.com/news/daily-comment/a-scathing-report-of-britains-treatment-of-the-poor) a lack of resources.
1.6.9.4.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.6.9.4.4.
1.6.9.4.6. Pro: Our ageing population will not be able to support itself on the tax revenue of the next generation, without attracting an immigrant population of working-age people.
1.6.9.4.7. Pro: The end of the free movement of people will reduce the supply of labour, damaging the British economy.
1.6.9.4.7.1. Pro: Skilled foreign workers actually [grow](http://uk.businessinsider.com/immigrants-create-jobs-for-local-workers-2015-6) British businesses, making them more profitable and leading to further job creation.
1.6.9.4.7.2. Pro: Leaving the EU means we could lose over [4,500 NHS nurses](https://www.mirror.co.uk/news/politics/nhs-brexit-crisis-could-see-12925831), among many other skilled workers that come from the EU.
1.6.9.4.7.2.1. Con: The expected reduction in the numbers of NHS staff required from the rest of the EU will ensure that the UK government will be forced to reverse decades of decline in the numbers of NHS staff trained within the UK. This will in the long term make the NHS more self-sufficient in staffing, will improve salaries for NHS staff and will improve employment in the country overall.
1.6.9.4.7.2.1.1. Con: It is untrue that they will be forced to spend more money, when there is a constraint on resources owing to Brexit. It seems more likely that they'll force the NHS to adjust to the lack of money.
1.6.9.4.7.3. Pro: -> See 1.6.9.4.4.
1.6.9.4.7.4. Con: The UK is one of the [least mechanised](https://blogs.spectator.co.uk/2018/08/britains-economy-is-not-suffering-as-much-as-the-doom-mongers-insist/) country in the developed world due to the low cost of immigrant labour. This depresses wages and harms manufacturing output. Lack of immigrant labour would lead to increased mechanisation and the funding of training for employees who would in turn earn more money - thus helping with the gap in wages and cost of living.
1.6.9.4.7.5. Pro: -> See 1.6.9.4.2.3.1.
1.6.9.4.7.6. Pro: UK unemployment is at an [all time low](http://uk.businessinsider.com/uk-unemployment-rate-productivity-wages-2017-2018-1), meaning the country is almost at [full employment](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/f/fullemployment.asp), and therefore the economy will suffer if the labour pool is reduced.
1.6.9.4.7.7. Pro: In low-skilled sectors \(e.g. hospitality, food processing and construction\) employers have difficulty finding British employees and rely on predominantly Eastern European migrant labour \([Rolfe, p.51](https://www.niesr.ac.uk/sites/default/files/publications/Free%20movement%20-%20Final%20report.pdf)\).
1.6.9.4.7.8. Con: Reducing immigration is a political choice that is not necessitated by a hard Brexit.
1.6.9.4.7.8.1. Pro: The point of Brexit is that it will be up to the people, as represented through their MPs, who will decide what sort of immigration system they want; together with the freedom to change it whenever they want into the future.
1.6.9.4.7.8.1.1. Con: The UK Government has chosen to implement its own interpretation of Brexit. There has been no consultation with the British electorate to debate what kind of immigration system the public wants or what socio-economic costs there would be for doing so.
1.6.9.4.7.8.1.1.1. Con: The Government sought a mandate from the electorate based on its manifesto at the last general election, and received it.
1.6.9.4.7.8.1.1.1.1. Con: Theresa May insisted that a vote for the Conservatives was a vote for confidence in her delivering Brexit. She lost numerous seats in the last General Election.
1.6.9.4.7.8.2. Con: -> See 1.6.2.
1.6.9.4.7.8.3. Pro: -> See 1.6.7.
1.6.9.4.7.8.4. Con: Both sides chose to focus on values, not the details of Britain's EU membership. Due to the Leave campaign's focus on immigration policy, [immigration became the defining issue of the referendum](https://www.ipsos.com/ipsos-mori/en-uk/immigration-now-top-issue-voters-eu-referendum). This means many people likely made their decision based on the Leave campaign's promises to cut immigration, rather than considering the specifics of EU policy and what leaving actually entailed.
1.6.9.4.7.8.4.1. Con: [Ashcroft's poll](https://lordashcroftpolls.com/2016/06/how-the-united-kingdom-voted-and-why/) taken on referendum polling day suggests the single most popular reason for the leave vote was the issue of sovereignty \(49%\). Since [polls taken on election day yield a more representative sample](https://www.ft.com/content/0e38ae9e-4a16-11e7-a3f4-c742b9791d43), it is more likely that sovereignty was the defining issue.
1.6.9.4.7.8.4.1.1. Con: [Parliamentary sovereignty](https://www.parliament.uk/about/how/role/sovereignty/) is a principle of the UK constitution. It makes Parliament the supreme legal authority in the UK, which can create or end any law. Generally, the courts cannot overrule its legislation and no Parliament can pass laws that future Parliaments cannot change. Parliamentary sovereignty is the most important part of the UK constitution. Yet the hard brexiteers want to deprive Parliament of a [meaningful vote](https://amp.businessinsider.com/explained-dominic-grieve-meaningful-vote-brexit-amendment-2018-6), thus subverting their sovereignty.
1.6.9.4.7.8.4.1.2. Con: It is hard to separate votes for immigration and votes for sovereignty. Leave politicians insisted that taking back our Sovereignty was a means of restricting immigration by allowing us to "control our borders".
1.6.9.4.7.8.4.2. Con: Regardless of the defining issue for leave or remain voters \(for whom it was[the risk of leaving to economy, jobs etc.](https://lordashcroftpolls.com/2016/06/how-the-united-kingdom-voted-and-why/)\), it does not logically follow that EU policy and what leaving actually entailed were not given full consideration by those voters.
1.6.9.4.7.8.5. Pro: -> See 1.6.8.5.
1.6.9.4.7.9. Con: Although it is generally small, there is a specific downward effect on the [wages of unskilled native workers](https://www.bankofengland.co.uk/working-paper/2015/the-impact-of-immigration-on-occupational-wages-evidence-from-britain). As they are a vulnerable group, their economic welfare ought to be prioritised.
1.6.9.4.7.9.1. Con: Maybe, instead of paralysing the British economy with a Hard Brexit, the government should invest in high-skills education for those native workers who aren't skilled.
1.6.9.4.7.9.2. Pro: -> See 1.6.1.5.6.1.2.
1.6.9.4.7.9.3. Con: Brexit is [unlikely to help](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-wages-theresa-may-gdp-deal-eu-latest-analysis-a8655746.html) the most economically disadvantaged.
1.6.9.4.7.9.4. Con: The economic welfare of the poorest is better served by the increase in tax income that immigration [provides](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/eu-workers-uk-tax-treasury-brexit-migrants-british-citizens-a8542506.html) overall than by trying to protect them from the small reduction in wages that immigration sometimes causes.

1.6.10. Con: The Brexit campaign resulted in a significant increase of expressions of xenophobia and racism, which may make it harder for the UK to attract foreign workers.
1.6.10.1. Pro: The government has a policy of creating a [hostile environment](https://publications.parliament.uk/pa/cm201719/cmselect/cmhaff/421/42106.htm) for illegal immigrants which will be extended to include [EU citizens](https://www.the3million.org.uk/hostile-environment).
1.6.10.1.1. Con: EU citizens who choose to break our laws and come here illegally should find a hostile environment.
1.6.10.2. Con: Making it more difficult to attract foreign skilled workers will encourage the public and private sectors to invest more in training UK residents.
1.6.10.3. Con: An exodus of skilled workers from foreign countries can lead to a brain drain in those countries, negatively affecting their development.
1.6.10.4. Pro: A [UN special report](https://ohchr.org/en/NewsEvents/Pages/DisplayNews.aspx?NewsID=23074&LangID=E) said that Brexit has contributed to an environment of increased racial discrimination and intolerance, and has made [racial and ethnic minorities more vulnerable](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2018/may/11/uk-has-seen-brexit-related-growth-in-racism-says-un-representative) to racial discrimination and intolerance.
1.6.10.5. Pro: Police figures obtained through Freedom of Information \(FOI\) requests show [incidents surged by 23 per cent](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/home-news/racist-hate-crimes-surge-to-record-high-after-brexit-vote-new-figures-reveal-a7829551.html) – from 40,741 to 49,921 – in the 11 months after the EU referendum, compared with the same period the previous year. This is the highest spike in religious and racial hate crimes ever recorded.
1.6.10.6. Pro: The UK's Home Office confirmed victims’ reports of a [dramatic increase in incidents](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/crime/hate-crimes-eu-referendum-spike-brexit-terror-attacks-police-home-office-europeans-xenophobia-a8004716.html) motivated by attackers’ hostility towards their race, nationality, religion or other factors.
1.6.10.6.1. Con: A home office spokesperson said that the increase was thought to also be due to ongoing [improvements in crime recording](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/crime/hate-crimes-eu-referendum-spike-brexit-terror-attacks-police-home-office-europeans-xenophobia-a8004716.html) by the police.
1.6.10.7. Con: This is a temporary effect, not a long lasting change in UK racism.
1.6.10.7.1. Pro: The increase was due to the widespread public debate. Debate will significantly lessen once we leave \(or remain\), and therefore tension within the British public will lessen.
1.6.10.7.2. Con: There has been a [large](https://www.standard.co.uk/news/crime/revealed-antimuslim-hate-crimes-in-london-soared-by-40-in-a-year-a3775751.html) [rise](https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/2018/jul/20/record-number-anti-muslim-attacks-reported-uk-2017) in hate crimes against muslims \(26% rise in 2017\) and people from other non-EU backgrounds. After brexit these groups are not going to decrease in size \(if anything we will see a rise in commonwealth migrants\) even though the expectation among brexiteers is that migration will fall. This disillusionment quite possibly will lead to further increases in bigotry and racism
1.6.10.8. Con: -> See 1.6.2.5.
1.6.10.9. Pro: Her Majesty’s Inspectorate of Constabulary and Fire & Rescue Service have warned of a “[real possibility](https://www.theguardian.com/uk-news/2018/jul/19/brexit-will-trigger-rise-in-hate-crimes-warns-police-watchdog)” that Britain’s exit from the European Union next year will trigger a spike in hate crimes.
1.7. Pro: A hard Brexit will negatively affect the National Health Service.
1.7.1. Con: [£20 billion has been pledged to NHS](http://www.itv.com/news/2018-06-16/nhs-to-receive-20bn-boost-by-2024-partly-funded-by-brexit-dividend-says-may/) by 2024, funded by money saved from the EU membership.
1.7.1.1. Con: FullFact suggest that [none of the £20 billion Brexit dividend will actually come from Brexit savings](https://fullfact.org/europe/does-brexit-dividend-exist/).
1.7.1.2. Pro: The Brexit dividend will pay the [equivalent of £384 million a week](https://www.express.co.uk/news/uk/975322/brexit-news-nhs-funding-600-million-week-victory-leave-european-union), more than the [£350 million promised by Vote Leave](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/health/brexit-latest-nhs-england-350-million-per-week-pledge-eu-simon-stevens-brussels-a8043191.html).
1.7.1.2.1. Con: The EU membership cost to Britain is £13.1bn a year, or [approximately](https://fullfact.org/europe/our-eu-membership-fee-55-million/) £251m a week. £4.5bn of that fee covers money paid back to Britain for farming subsidies, science research, regeneration grants, etc. Researchers suggest that the loss of trade through a No-Deal Brexit will [cost far more](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/business/news/brexit-no-trade-deal-with-eu-cost-58-billion-annually-customs-union-tariffs-a8251581.html) than the EU membership fee itself, a total of £58bn a year.
1.7.1.2.2. Con: The figures of £350/384 million are intentionally misleading as they do not include the UK's rebate, [this amount is never sent to the EU](https://fullfact.org/europe/our-eu-membership-fee-55-million/), so should not be considered for any dividend.
1.7.1.3. Con: A likely period of economic harm after Brexit may be enough to do permanent damage to the already struggling NHS as well as other public services as Government tax revenues would be hit.
1.7.1.3.1. Pro: A hard Brexit will significantly damage the UK economy.
1.7.1.3.1.1. Pro: Britain may have to pay a Brexit divorce bill.
1.7.1.3.1.1.1. Pro: The UK's [Office for Budget Responsibility \(OBR\) suggests](https://assets.publishing.service.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/attachment_data/file/689581/CCS207_CCS0318157642-1_OBR_EFO_March_2018_Accessible.pdf#page=23) that Britian will be [paying the divorce bill until 2064](https://fullfact.org/europe/brexit-divorce-bill-2064/), with the majority paid by 2021, and the total net estimate at £37.1 billion. \(March 2018\)
1.7.1.3.1.1.1.1. Con: It's the same with a bad internet provider. Sometimes it is better to pay the contract cancellation fee than to use a bad service.
1.7.1.3.1.1.1.2. Con: In the budget for financial year 2016–17, [proposed total government spending was £772 billion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_spending_in_the_United_Kingdom). This should be our perspective. £37.1 Billion sounds like a lot of money, but in 3 years time, it's not that much.
1.7.1.3.1.1.2. Con: In the event of a no deal brexit, there will be [no divorce bill](http://uk.businessinsider.com/government-not-obliged-to-pay-52-billion-brexit-divorce-bill-2017-3) for the UK to pay, as the [agreement](https://www.gov.uk/government/publications/joint-report-on-progress-during-phase-1-of-negotiations-under-article-50-teu-on-the-uks-orderly-withdrawal-from-the-eu&ved=2ahUKEwiDt-Wv8-rbAhUrBsAKHcamDHAQFjAFegQIAhAB&usg=AOvVaw2lySUz0sIGRclxf59BZreS) reached thus far has been "under the caveat that nothing is agreed until everything is agreed."
1.7.1.3.1.1.3. Pro: In December 2017, the British prime minister [put forward £39 billion](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-divorce-bill-financial-settlement-39-billion-theresa-may-juncker-agreement-northern-ireland-a8099076.html), though recent [auditors say](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/eu-divorce-bill-brexit-theresa-may-estimate-eu-exchange-rate-pac-a8312776.html) "relatively small changes” to government assumptions – on economic performance, ongoing payments into EU schemes or the exchange rate – would throw out the calculation.
1.7.1.3.1.1.4. Con: The [British prime minister said](https://hansard.parliament.uk/commons/2017-12-11/debates/965E3010-41F6-4353-A2CC-2F5A6C31495F/BrexitNegotiations) "We will continue to pay our net contributions under the current EU budget plan" but added that that was "conditional upon a number of principles we have negotiated over how we will ultimately arrive at a fair valuation of these commitments" \(December 2017\).
1.7.1.3.1.1.5. Pro: Article 70 of the [Vienna Treaty](https://treaties.un.org/doc/publication/unts/volume%201155/volume-1155-i-18232-english.pdf) says a party leaving a treaty has an obligation to existing agreements made before leaving.
1.7.1.3.1.1.5.1. Pro: The [Vienna Treaty text says](https://publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201617/ldselect/ldeucom/125/12511.htm)  "termination of a treaty" "does not affect any right, obligation or legal situation of the parties created through the execution of the treaty prior to its termination.”
1.7.1.3.1.1.5.1.1. Pro: If the Vienna Treaty Applies, Brexit is bound by international treaty to pay it's Divorce Bill.
1.7.1.3.1.1.5.2. Pro: A House of Lords report decided "the meaning of Article 50 TEU, should, as a matter of EU law, be determined in the light of rules laid down in the Vienna Convention"
- ["Brexit and the EU budget" March 2017](https://publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201617/ldselect/ldeucom/125/12511.htm).
1.7.1.3.1.1.5.3. Con: A House of Lords report "concludes that Article 50 TEU is 'lex specialis', or an exception, expressly mentioned in the first part of Article 70 VCLT. In other words, because the TEU contains a specific provision on withdrawal, Article 70 is irrelevant to the UK’s financial obligations arising out of its EU membership." 
- ["Brexit and the EU budget" March 2017](https://publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201617/ldselect/ldeucom/125/12507.htm#_idTextAnchor040).
1.7.1.3.1.1.5.3.1. Con: [Independent legal analysis has suggested](https://www.ejiltalk.org/the-brexit-bill-and-the-law-of-treaties/) this is incorrect. Article 50 TEU only specifies a specific procedure \(the timeline and notification requirements\), and does not explicitly specify anything about existing obligations, and so does nothing to overrule the specified existing obligations under Article 70 VCLT.
1.7.1.3.1.1.5.4. Con: Those agreements were made with two major caveats. Firstly, the possibility of leaving the EU with a two year notice period. Second, the expectation of continuing benefits. Art 70 of the Vienna Treaty is therefore \(arguably\) satisfied without any payment after March 2019 in the event of no deal.
1.7.1.3.1.1.6. Con: If there is no agreement in place at the point of leaving there would be no legal [obligation](http://www.bbc.com/news/uk-politics-39294904) for the UK to make any payment as part of a financial settlement, saving them a large amount of money.
1.7.1.3.1.1.6.1. Pro: The UK can leave the EU without a deal and negotiations for a new arrangement can continue. As a deal will last for many years, if not forever, it is critical to obtain a fair deal and not enter into a bad deal .
1.7.1.3.1.1.6.2. Con: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.1.1.2.
1.7.1.3.1.1.6.3. Con: A part of the financial settlement is finalising EU investment in UK based projects. If the settlement was abandoned, then these projects would have to be finished or, even worse, restarted at the UK taxpayers expense.
1.7.1.3.1.1.6.3.1. Pro: The European Structural and Investment Fund has[€14.2bn of unspent planned UK expenditure with a further €7.3bn of unplanned expenditure to be spent.](http://researchbriefings.files.parliament.uk/documents/CBP-7847/CBP-7847.pdf)
1.7.1.3.1.1.6.4. Con: Not paying for agreed commitments would destroy goodwill and trust towards the UK, which will diminish our ability to negotiate any beneficial new trade deals, whether with the EU or others.
1.7.1.3.1.1.6.4.1. Con: If the UK were to leave without a deal it would leave a hole in the EU budget. This would be a better incentive for a trade deal though the amount might be renegotiated depending on the trade deal on offer.
1.7.1.3.1.2. Pro: Trade deals take [time](https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2016/07/how-long-do-trade-deals-take-after-brexit/) to negotiate. So for a period of some years the UK will not have negotiated trade deals which will impact the economy. At which point with the UK desperate for trade deals in the beginning to limit the impact of WTO tariffs, it will be likely the UK will be negotiating from a position of weakness and will negotiate one sided deals detrimental to the UK.
1.7.1.3.1.2.1. Pro: In the event of a hard Brexit, Britain will overnight be excluded from EU arrangements with “third countries”, or non-EU countries, entering the equivalent of a legal void in key parts of its external commercial relations. This exclusion will mean that the UK will have to [renegotiate](https://www.ft.com/content/f1435a8e-372b-11e7-bce4-9023f8c0fd2e) 759 treaties.
1.7.1.3.1.2.1.1. Con: The UK will still be able to trade under WTO rules with countries whilst setting up \(hopefully more beneficial\) trade agreements with various nations.
1.7.1.3.1.2.1.1.1. Con: The WTO has [stated](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/business/news/no-deal-brexit-uk-trading-wto-under-wto-rules-not-realistic-roberto-azevedo-a8505946.html) that it is not realistic to start trading on WTO terms immediately after Brexit.
1.7.1.3.1.2.1.1.1.1. Con: Britain can negotiate its relationship with the EU [whilst still being a member](https://www.matrixlaw.co.uk/resource/possible-united-kingdom-negotiate-trade-agreement-eu-ftas-third-countries-new-legal-status-within-wto-part-eu/).
1.7.1.3.1.2.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Britain could negotiate their status, if they had the slightest clue of what their negotiating stance should be. Sadly they do not.
1.7.1.3.1.2.1.2. Pro: 295 of these treaties are [trade treaties](https://www.ft.com/content/f1435a8e-372b-11e7-bce4-9023f8c0fd2e), leaving in limbo countless businesses who will not know what rules govern trading relations between the UK and other countries.
1.7.1.3.1.2.1.3. Pro: Each agreement has to be reviewed, the country approached, the decision makers found, meetings arranged, trips made, negotiations started and completed. This will take up considerable time and resources of the British government, where some treaties may not be negotiated in time for Brexit.
1.7.1.3.1.2.1.3.1. Pro: Many countries want to know the outcome of EU-UK talks [before](https://www.ft.com/content/f1435a8e-372b-11e7-bce4-9023f8c0fd2e) making their own commitments to the UK, ensuring that their won't in fact be treaties signed with some non-EU nations by the time a hard Brexit occurs.
1.7.1.3.1.2.1.4. Pro: Multi-lateral agreements based on consensus also need to be renegotiated, where Britain must [re-approach](https://www.ft.com/content/f1435a8e-372b-11e7-bce4-9023f8c0fd2e) 132 separate parties.
1.7.1.3.1.2.1.5. Con: The UK is trying to [secure](https://www.ft.com/content/f1435a8e-372b-11e7-bce4-9023f8c0fd2e) transition agreements that will minimise disruption to trade after Brexit, allowing breathing room for old deals to be renegotiated and new ones to be made.
1.7.1.3.1.2.1.5.1. Pro: The UK has secured a [one year transitional period](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/amp/uk-politics-43456502)  \(Brexit day to 31 December 2020\).
1.7.1.3.1.2.1.5.1.1. Con: The EU27 may extend the transition period and [require Britain to refrain](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-eu-trade-deals-liam-fox-two-years-veto-michel-barnier-negotiating-guidelines-a8160996.html) from implementing any independent trade deals for two years after Brexit.
1.7.1.3.1.2.1.5.2. Con: There will not be any [automatic](https://euobserver.com/uk-referendum/140571) roll-over of EU trade deals for the UK during the post-Brexit transition period, unless the EU negotiates them individually with non-EU states. It is unlikely the EU will taken on this burden, or be able to complete the negotiations for all the treaties in the limited time remaining.
1.7.1.3.1.3. Pro: A Brexit reversal would have a beneficial impact on the UK's economy. This is because it would boost investor [certainty](http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/2017/10/17/reversing-brexit-second-referendum-would-have-positive-impact/) in the UK market.
1.7.1.3.1.4. Pro: A recent government analysis indicates that the UK economy will contract by [as much as 9%](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/feb/26/economy-could-shrink-by-9-percent-under-no-deal-brexit-government-says) after no-deal Brexit.
1.7.1.3.1.5. Con: The exceptionally strong performance of the UK economy since the Brexit vote indicates that a hard Brexit will significantly benefit the UK economy.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1. Con: Since the announcement of Brexit the value of the pound has [dropped](https://www.poundsterlinglive.com/bank-of-england-spot/historical-spot-exchange-rates/gbp/GBP-to-EUR#charts).
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1. Con: The weaker pound will make British exports more competitive.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1. Con: However, with hard Brexit there will be a lack of involvement in the free market. This would mean decreased exports to EU countries due to less benefits and thus increased costs.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1. Pro: With no new trade agreement with the EU, the rules of the World Trade Organisation would apply. Tariffs would be imposed on goods that the UK sends to the EU, which would negatively affect many British companies who rely on this trade.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: British farmers would face [30-40](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-talks-collapse-negotiations-eu-uk-theresa-may-david-davis-no-deal-wto-a7955471.html) per cent charges for exporting to the EU. This would be a huge blow to agriculture in Britain.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Part of the reason UK farmers are so reliant on EU markets is because the [protectionist](https://global.handelsblatt.com/politics/protectionism-tariffs-us-european-union-913499) [nature](https://brexitcentral.com/remainer-lord-hannay-wrong-eu-tariffs-african-imports/) of the [EU bloc](https://iea.org.uk/the-eus-thousands-of-senseless-tariffs-punish-the-poor/) means that reciprocal tariffs or strict quotas are in place with many other countries.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Mark Manger and Atom Vayalinkal \(of the London School of Economics\), who have compared tariff data for Australia, Canada, the EU, Japan, the UK and the US, [reach a different conclusion](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/brexit/2017/10/24/the-eu-isnt-protectionist-its-one-of-the-most-open-economies-in-the-world/). On manufactured goods the EU has the second-lowest tariffs after Japan. On agricultural products Japan has the highest tariffs, followed by the US.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Looking at average tariffs as Manger and Vayalinkal have done can disguise the true picture, as tariffs are only one of the ways in which protectionist entities like the EU and US restrict trade, and specific tariffs still cause harm, as explained by [Rolf Langhammer](https://global.handelsblatt.com/politics/protectionism-tariffs-us-european-union-913499) and [Kevin Dowd](https://iea.org.uk/the-eus-thousands-of-senseless-tariffs-punish-the-poor/).
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.1.1.2. Con: The European Union is one of the most open economies in the world and is committed to free trade.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.1.1.2.1. Pro: European Union Trade Commissioner Cecilia Malmstrom has unveiled a blueprint for a [free-trade deal with the U.S](http://fortune.com/2019/02/22/europe-trade-deal-usa/?#), saying that “While we are talking, we should not impose any tariffs on each other”.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.1.1.2.2. Pro: The average applied tariff for goods imported into the EU is very low. More than [70% of imports enter the EU at zero or reduced tariffs](http://ec.europa.eu/trade/policy/eu-position-in-world-trade/index_en.htm).
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.1.1.2.3. Pro: The EU has [over 35 free trade agreements with 62 partner countries](http://trade.ec.europa.eu/doclib/docs/2018/october/tradoc_157468.pdf), and the pace of new FTAs in increasing.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.2. Con: Entering into WTO would provide us with the same access to Europe as other non-European countries and any negotiations with regards specific tariffs would be scheduled later as we'd continue on current values. Going WTO is a scaremonger story and the reality is we import more than too Europe, WTO negotiations would be in our favour.[ukandeu.ac.uk](http://ukandeu.ac.uk/explainers/no-deal-the-wto-option/)
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.3. Pro: Continued EU membership [guarantees](http://foodresearch.org.uk/publications/feeding-britain-food-security-after-brexit/) the long term security of food.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.4. Pro: UK trades internationally under EU WTO schedules and quotas, a Hard-Brexit will require immediate implementation of a [UK WTO Schedule and Quota](https://www.wto.org/english/news_e/news18_e/mark_24jul18_e.htm). This does not exist and could take a long time to agree.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.5. Con: In the absence of any agreement with the EU, imports from the EU will raise £12.9bn for the UK Treasury in duties, whilst UK exporters will face £5.2bn in total in tariffs on their exports to the EU. [blogs.lse.ac.uk](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/brexit/2017/01/27/post-brexit-trade-can-thrive-under-wto-rules/)
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.6. Pro: Car industry analysis suggests that [EU tariffs on cars](https://www.smmt.co.uk/industry-topics/brexit/) alone could add £1.8 billion to the cost of exporting UK cars to the EU, decreasing UK competitiveness in the European market.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.6.1. Con: The UK [manufactures enough](https://www.smmt.co.uk/industry-topics/brexit/key-exports-data/) cars domestically that British citizens will not have a problem.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.6.1.1. Con: Only a [tiny fraction](https://www.theaa.com/car-buying/cars-made-in-britain) of these are made by British-owned companies. The bulk are made by companies which are [pulling out because of Brexit](https://www.nytimes.com/2019/02/18/business/brexit-honda.html).
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.6.1.1.1. Con: Cars are [bad](https://www.kialo.com/should-private-cars-be-forbidden-in-large-cities-9351) anyway. Good riddance.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.6.2. Con: Cars manufactured in the UK by EU companies will suffer from tariffs and delays on parts, which could make it more expensive and logistically more difficult than to manufacture the cars on the continent and then take just a single tariff and delay to sell to the UK. This could result in the shutdown of plants in the UK owned by EU companies and a consequential loss in jobs.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.7. Con: Reciprocal tariffs imposed on goods the EU sends to the UK would protect some British industries, contributing to economic growth.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.7.1. Con: The UK is a net importer with the EU. [Imports exceeded exports by £7.6 billion](https://www.uktradeinfo.com/Statistics/OverseasTradeStatistics/Pages/EU_and_Non-EU_Data.aspx) in April 2018.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.8. Con: As Britain comprises [16%](http://ec.europa.eu/eurostat/web/products-eurostat-news/-/DDN-20170410-1) of EU GDP, reciprocal tariffs would hurt EU businesses as well, incentivising the agreement of sector-specific trade deals.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.9. Pro: 75% of British fish is exported. Leaving the EU would put Britain in a position similar to Norway, which is subject to tariffs on exported fish, and has described this as a '[serious obstacle to trade](http://www.thisismoney.co.uk/money/news/article-4043758/EU-trade-key-success-UK-fish-industry-75-home-caught-fish-exported-majority-fish-eaten-Britons-imported.html)'.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.9.1. Con: Such a high level of exported fish indicates how in demand British fish is in the EU. The imposition of tariffs will hurt EU consumers first and foremost.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.9.1.1. Con: Trade is not zero-sum: it is possible for tariffs to hurt both the exporter and the importer, and therefore both the UK and the EU.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.9.1.1.1. Con: This would illustrate that the EU as a political organisation is willing to hurt its own citizens in order to protect its own narrow interests, which is a good argument in itself for the UK leaving such a spiteful club.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.2. Con: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.7.1.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.3. Con: Currency depreciation also makes UK produced products [more expensive](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2016/oct/21/why-pound-sterling-collapse-not-good-uk-economy-robert-skidelsky) as many of them are deeply integrated in foreign supply chains and need foreign imported inputs to produce. When sterling depreciates and import prices rise, the effect on export prices renders them less competitive.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.2. Pro: Should a hard brexit actually pass, several experts [predict](https://www.cnbc.com/2019/01/16/brexit-dont-buy-british-pound-sterling-investment-experts-say.html) the pound may come under further pressure.
1.7.1.3.1.5.1.3. Pro: Increased import prices that come with currency depreciation [harm](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/business/news/uk-inflation-latest-updates-news-pound-sterling-brexit-vote-prices-city-london-a7941921.html) the British consumer as the UK economy [relies on imports](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/business/news/uk-trade-deficit-latest-record-high-pound-sterling-slump-exports-goods-imports-brexit-a7992051.html).
1.7.1.3.1.5.2. Pro: UK wage growth is at the [fastest level since 2008](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2019/jan/22/uk-pay-growth-employment-weekly-earnings), far outstripping inflation, confounding the pre-referendum pessimistic forecasts.
1.7.1.3.1.5.3. Pro: The UK employment rate has hit a [record high of 75.8%](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-46958560), confounding the pre-referendum pessimistic forecasts.
1.7.1.3.1.5.4. Pro: The [UK economy grew at 1.3%](https://www.ons.gov.uk/economy/grossdomesticproductgdp/timeseries/ihyr/pn2) in 2018 \(comparing Q4 2018 to Q4 2017\), faster than that of Germany [\(0.6%\)](https://tradingeconomics.com/germany/gdp-growth) or France [\(0.9%\)](https://tradingeconomics.com/france/gdp-growth), confounding the pre-referendum pessimistic forecasts.
1.7.1.3.1.5.4.1. Con: Brexit hasn't happened yet.
1.7.1.3.1.5.4.1.1. Con: Brexit is a process. Economic predictions were for catastrophe as soon as a pro-Brexit vote happened. These were clearly [not just wrong](https://fullfact.org/economy/brexit-and-economic-apocalypse/), but in many cases, e.g. employment and public finances, the opposite of what has happened.
1.7.1.3.1.5.4.1.1.1. Con: Let's not conflate the process of forming any leaving deal with the implementation itself which is not a process.
1.7.1.3.1.5.4.1.2. Con: Businesses cite uncertainty as the biggest economic inhibitor. The post-referendum period has seen the most uncertainty from a policy perspective over a hard brexit. The fact that the economy is doing so well despite this uncertainty indicates a hard brexit holds nothing to fear.
1.7.1.3.1.5.4.1.2.1. Con: Not all [commentators](https://www.pwc.co.uk/services/economics-policy/insights/uk-economic-outlook.html) are convinced the economy is doing as well as claimed.
1.7.1.3.1.5.4.1.2.1.1. Pro: The [CBI](http://www.cbi.org.uk/news/cbi-sets-out-business-impact-of-a-no-deal-brexit-in-every-region-and-nation-across-the-uk/) doesn't want no deal.
1.7.1.3.1.5.5. Pro: The UK unemployment is [below 4% for the first time since 1975](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-47622415), 3 years after the vote to leave the EU, confounding the pre-referendum pessimistic forecasts.
1.7.1.3.1.5.6. Con: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.5.4.1.
1.7.1.3.1.5.7. Pro: The value of UK exports has [increased markedly by over 20%](https://tradingeconomics.com/united-kingdom/exports) in just 2.5 years since the Brexit vote, whereas the value of exports was flat for the preceding four years.
1.7.1.3.1.5.8. Pro: UK government 10 year borrowing costs are at [historically low rates](https://datahub.io/core/bond-yields-uk-10y), currently just 1.01% and down 0.3 points on a year ago, indicating that the market is extremely confident in UK government finances and is unconcerned by any risk associated with a hard Brexit.
1.7.1.3.1.5.8.1. Con: Some [commentators](https://www.theguardian.com/business/live/2018/dec/20/markets-ftse-100-fed-rate-hike-donald-trump-bank-of-england-business-live?CMP=Share_AndroidApp_Copy_to_clipboard) think that low interest rates reflect the Bank of England's concerns regarding the economy.
1.7.1.3.1.5.8.2. Con: The [last time](https://www.ft.com/content/ef568030-a7a8-11e4-be63-00144feab7de) the UK’s 10-year gilt yield fell to a historic low was in the midst of the eurozone debt crisis in 2012.
1.7.1.3.1.5.9. Con: Not [everyone](https://www.google.com/amp/s/amp.ft.com/content/cf51e840-7147-11e7-93ff-99f383b09ff9) agrees that the UK's economic performance has been exceptional.
1.7.1.3.1.5.10. Con: Economic effects are not always immediate. The likely loss of single market and passporting rights has caused investment in the UK economy to drop by around [£300B](https://www.thelondoneconomic.com/news/jaw-dropping-300-billion-negative-swing-in-foreign-investment-flows-since-brexit/29/04/) a year already, even before leaving the EU. This will not be felt immediately, but will have harmful long term consequences on productivity and growth.
1.7.1.3.1.5.11. Con: There is now a [long and expanding list](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/business/news/brexit-companies-leaving-uk-list-job-cuts-eu-no-deal-customs-union-a8792296.html) of major employers leaving the UK due to brexit. These include, but are not limited to: Barclays, Dyson, Honda, HSBC, Lloyds of London, Moneygram, P&O, Nissan, Sony, Panasonic, Airbus, JPMorgan, Toyota. To claim this will lead to economic benefits is not credible.
1.7.1.3.1.5.12. Con: [$1 trillion](https://www.cnbc.com/2019/01/07/brexit-uk-financial-services-sector-moves-1-trillion-in-assets-to-eu.html) of financial assets are leaving Britain because of Brexit. This will significantly harm the Exchequer's ability to tax the financial industry, which makes up a large proportion of the UK tax base.
1.7.1.3.1.6. Pro: A hard Brexit will have an [adverse](https://www.which.co.uk/news/2019/04/what-will-brexit-mean-for-house-prices/) [impact](https://amp-theguardian-com.cdn.ampproject.org/v/s/amp.theguardian.com/business/2018/nov/28/bank-of-england-says-no-deal-brexit-would-be-worse-than-2008-crisis?usqp=mq331AQCCAE%3D&amp_js_v=0.1#referrer=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.google.com&amp_tf=From%20%251%24s&ampshare=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.theguardian.com%2Fbusiness%2F2018%2Fnov%2F28%2Fbank-of-england-says-no-deal-brexit-would-be-worse-than-2008-crisis) on our housing market, and interest rates could rise. This could leave people in negative equity with higher mortgage payments, that some may not be able to afford and potentially people having their homes repossessed.
1.7.1.3.1.6.1. Con: There are consistent complaints that prices are too high for young people to get on the property ladder, and for them this would be seen as a positive effect on the housing market.
1.7.1.3.1.6.1.1. Pro: Brexit is a brilliant idea if you're a [disaster capitalist](https://www.theguardian.com/books/2018/nov/09/mystic-mogg-jacob-rees-mogg-willam-predicts-brexit-plans).
1.7.1.3.1.6.1.2. Con: Most people would not describe the financial ruin of potentially hundreds of thousands of households as a positive effect on the housing market, even if it meant they could milk the despair of their fellow man for quick profit.
1.7.1.3.1.7. Pro: The EU directs [considerable investment](http://blog.policy.manchester.ac.uk/brexit/2017/10/uk-regional-development-in-the-context-of-brexit/) towards poorer regions of the UK that the national government tends to ignore.
1.7.1.3.1.7.1. Pro: The British government [spends significantly more money](https://www.ippr.org/news-and-media/press-releases/new-transport-figures-reveal-north-to-receive-indefensible-2-555-less-per-person-than-london) on transport infrastructure per head in London than anywhere else in the UK.
1.7.1.3.1.7.1.1. Con: This is because the London area has the oldest rail network, and is subject to the most strain with the largest population increase and thus increase in usage, of any location in the UK. Plus the fragmented nature of the network pushes up costs - London is the [hub](https://www.ft.com/content/d82848ca-f7ba-11e7-88f7-5465a6ce1a00) where a lot of this fragmentation meets.
1.7.1.3.1.7.1.1.1. Con: London does not have the UK's oldest train network. The rail networks around Liverpool, Manchester and most of Scotland are significantly older.
1.7.1.3.1.7.1.1.2. Pro: London's rail network is fragmented because it is run by 38 separate private contractors.
1.7.1.3.1.7.1.1.3. Con: Geographically, London is in no way the nexus point for the British railway. It is only the centre of this matrix by choice, not divine, immutable intervention.
1.7.1.3.1.7.2. Pro: The [European Investment Bank](http://www.eib.org/) is the largest multilateral investment bank in the world \(bigger than the World Bank\), and lent [36 billion euros](http://researchbriefings.parliament.uk/ResearchBriefing/Summary/CBP-7847) between 2011 and 2016 to British institutions, with less developed areas \(particularly in West Wales and the South West of England\) receiving more per person than other areas.
1.7.1.3.1.8. Con: The UK could create a more competitive environment for attracting companies.
1.7.1.3.1.8.1. Con: Many companies [are leaving or thinking about leaving](https://www.thenational.ae/business/economy/half-of-big-european-companies-cut-uk-investment-over-brexit-survey-shows-1.743895) the UK.
1.7.1.3.1.8.1.1. Pro: Even the pro-Brexit 'traitor' bashing [Daily Mail](https://twitter.com/AngryScotland/status/888820533512609793?s=19) is quietly considering moving to its Irish offices in Dublin to continue enjoying the benefits of EU membership.
1.7.1.3.1.8.2. Pro: The EU mandates that the UK not have less than a [15% VAT](http://www.blandbaker.co.uk/sites/www.blandbaker.co.uk/files/blandbakerpwp_apu_oct16_vatafterbrexit30.9.16.pdf). Once free from these regulations the UK could lower this and gain a competitive advantage to encourage growth and investment.
1.7.1.3.1.8.2.1. Con: As UK VAT currently stands at 20%, there was room for the Government to lower the tax without exiting the EU.
1.7.1.3.1.8.3. Pro: It has been [proposed](http://www.nationalreview.com/article/446407/brexit-uk-deregulation-parliament-should-seize-opportunity) that the UK could form a commission to cut back on EU regulations post Brexit by almost a quarter. This would save the UK between £33 billion \($43 billion\) and £140 billion \($182 billion\) annually and bring more industry and investment into the UK.
1.7.1.3.1.8.3.1. Con: EU regulations protect the health and safety of its citizens. Severely cutting them back would be a risky measure.
1.7.1.3.1.8.3.2. Con: EU officials have [stated](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-eu-trade-deal-workers-rights-tax-haven-michel-barnier-environmental-legislation-a7851761.html) they will not sign a trade deal with the UK if they begin a deregulatory "race to the bottom".
1.7.1.3.1.8.3.3. Con: Deregulation doesn't automatically mean less stress on public finances or higher economic performance.
1.7.1.3.1.8.3.3.1. Pro: [Deregulation](https://smartasset.com/taxes/do-sin-taxes-affect-cigarette-and-alcohol-consumption) of drugs, tabacco and alcohol would lead to higher consumption of these substances and higher frequency of negative health related issues. Hence higher stress for NHS and public health budgets.
1.7.1.3.1.8.3.3.2. Pro: Unregulated capitalism leads to creation of monopolies, which in turn decreases competition.
1.7.1.3.1.8.4. Con: The UK is already a competitive market for companies under the status quo. Trade barriers will only [harm](https://www.theguardian.com/small-business-network/2016/jun/07/brexit-make-uk-businesses-less-competitive) this.
1.7.1.3.1.9. Pro: The EU is responsible for the funding/loans behind many of the UK's largest infrastructure projects.
1.7.1.3.1.9.1. Pro: The macroeconomic effects and uncertainty caused by Brexit are likely to [slow down](https://www.squirepattonboggs.com/~/media/files/insights/publications/2016/06/eu-referendum-brexit-and-the-impact-on-uk-infrastructure/eureferendumukinfrastructurealert.pdf) infrastructure spending.
1.7.1.3.1.9.1.1. Pro: Werner Hoyer, president of the European Investment Bank, said it was [inevitable](https://www.ft.com/content/aaaaa9cc-b1b2-11e7-aa26-bb002965bce8) that lending to the UK would fall from recent levels of about £7bn a year once it left the EU.
1.7.1.3.1.9.1.2. Pro: The uncertainty around whether the new regulatory landscape of the UK will be in line with EU regulations is already [causing delays](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2017/may/15/uk-infrastructure-projects-could-be-hit-by-european-investment-bank-concerns) in investment, putting jobs at risk.
1.7.1.3.1.9.2. Pro: The UK is one of the largest shareholders \(16%\) in the European Investment Bank \(EIB\), granting it [considerable leverage](https://fullfact.org/economy/uks-stake-european-investment-bank/) to be able to direct the Bank's funding towards its own projects. By leaving the EU, and thereby leaving the Bank, the UK loses access to this pool of money.
1.7.1.3.1.9.2.1. Pro: The UK has already experienced [a significant reduction](https://www.parliament.uk/business/committees/committees-a-z/lords-select/eu-financial-affairs-subcommittee/news-parliament-2017/brexit-eib-report-published/) in funding from the EIB as a result of Brexit preparations.
1.7.1.3.1.9.2.2. Pro: The European Investment Bank loaned £1 billion towards the cost of [Crossrail](http://www.crossrail.co.uk/news/articles/tfl-agrees-1bn-loan-for-crossrail-from-european-investment-bank).
1.7.1.3.1.9.3. Con: The UK is [seeking to maintain its membership](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-eib-european-investment-bank-davis-davis-eu-negotiations-a7932671.html) of the European Investment Bank \(EIB\) after Brexit. If that happens, there will be a minimal effect, if any, on procurement for infrastructure spending.
1.7.1.3.1.9.3.1. Con: Maintaining access to the EIB would require a deal with EU, a prospect that would be costly to the UK and at the moment seems unlikely to even occur.
1.7.1.3.1.9.3.2. Con: In order for the UK to remain a member of the EIB, all the constitution of the Bank would have to be [amended](https://fullfact.org/economy/uks-stake-european-investment-bank/) and all remaining member states would have to agree to this change. This is by no means a given.
1.7.1.3.1.9.4. Con: With regards to funding, the UK is a [net contributor](https://fullfact.org/europe/our-eu-membership-fee-55-million/) to the EU. This means that following Brexit the UK could fund all the currently EU funded projects in the UK and still have money left over. With regards to loans, if the UK wishes to borrow money it can do so. It is not necessary to borrow exclusively from the EU. The UK does not do so now and would not need to in the future.
1.7.1.3.1.9.4.1. Con: The problem with this argument is that it does not take into account the efficiencies generated by doing some things at the EU level. It will probably cost the UK more to do them on their own.  [youtube.com](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=i8SyXnDMfio)
1.7.1.3.1.10. Pro: -> See 1.6.9.4.7.
1.7.1.3.1.11. Pro: A hard Brexit would result in short-term regulatory chaos.
1.7.1.3.1.11.1. Pro: When we leave the EU, we will become a 'third country' in terms of data protection. This will cause business no end of effort and expense when transferring data to the EEA and beyond, unless there is an 'adequacy decision' issued. [EU: the UK will be a 'third country' for personal data transfers from the point of Brexit](https://www.out-law.com/en/articles/2018/january/eu-the-uk-will-be-a-third-country-for-personal-data-transfers-from-the-point-of-brexit1/)
1.7.1.3.1.11.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.1.4.1.1.2.
1.7.1.3.1.11.3. Pro: [Inadequate customs contingency](https://www.ft.com/content/5ae67bea-ca0e-11e7-aa33-c63fdc9b8c6c) planning could lead to gridlock in Kent and resulting in delayed deliveries to businesses in the UK.
1.7.1.3.1.11.3.1. Pro: Sainsbury's, a major supermarket chain, is worried about [food rotting](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/home-news/sainsburys-ceo-mike-coupe-brexit-food-eu-goods-uk-border-a7927606.html) at the border.
1.7.1.3.1.11.4. Pro: Leaving Euratom, the EU's atomic energy community, could cause [energy supplies to be disrupted](http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/business-39774062) due to the lack of equivalent national regulatory body, and the inability to source vital components from Europe.
1.7.1.3.1.11.5. Pro: Regulatory misalignment between the UK and EU could result in a [prohibition on data sharing](https://www.chathamhouse.org/expert/comment/brexit-could-put-data-sharing-jeopardy) between the two zones, causing serious problems for businesses.
1.7.1.3.1.11.6. Pro: [Insurance contracts](http://www.lmalloyds.com/LMA/News/Blog/brexit_cliffedge.aspx), vital to companies' ability to manage risk, could face serious difficulty transferring between UK and EU jurisdictions.
1.7.1.3.1.11.7.
