Pro: A hard Brexit will result in a [legal and regulatory no-man's land](http://uk.businessinsider.com/the-political-economic-and-legal-chaos-risked-by-a-no-deal-brexit-uk-changing-europe-2017-7) with nobody entirely confident of what laws and regulations still apply. Disputes will therefore take far longer to resolve, bringing up costs and throwing into disarray trade relations between businesses.
1.7.1.3.1.11.7.1. Pro: Many international contracts will [lack](https://www.ft.com/content/a9204df0-da84-11e7-a039-c64b1c09b482) the details to deal with a new customs border between the UK and the EU. Potentially reviewing thousands of contracts will take a lot of time, but re-negotiating them will take significantly longer, increasing costs and creating a large degree of uncertainty.
1.7.1.3.1.11.8. Con: The UK is planning on [staying within the remit](https://www.ft.com/content/64d30780-10b5-11e7-b030-768954394623) of some of the regulatory agencies of the EU after Brexit, minimising the so-called regulatory upheaval.
1.7.1.3.1.11.8.1. Con: Without a trade deal or open borders, staying in any regulatory remit will mean very little.
1.7.1.3.1.11.9. Con: Brexit is a golden opportunity to get [rid](http://www.telegraph.co.uk/opinion/2017/03/28/brexit-golden-chance-throw-eu-regulations-bonfire/) of cumbersome EU regulations that the UK has no need for.
1.7.1.3.1.11.9.1. Con: As the most [regulation-happy](https://www.theregister.co.uk/2019/04/08/social_media_explanation/) member State of the EU, the [UK](https://www.independent.co.uk/voices/nanny-state-index-state-interference-smoking-drinking-junk-food-a7727571.html) is more likely to diverge from the EU as a [nanny](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/amp/uk-politics-45356189) State.
1.7.1.3.1.11.9.2. Pro: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.8.3.
1.7.1.3.1.11.9.3. Con: Supporters of deregulation [won't identify](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-david-davis-tax-haven-cut-regulations-after-leaving-eu-based-on-nothing-european-commission-a8218481.html) or are [not upfront about](https://inews.co.uk/news/politics/backlash-liam-foxs-total-secrecy-uk-us-trade-talks/) which regulations need to be scrapped and how they are currently cumbersome.
1.7.1.3.1.11.9.4. Con: It is not certain that deregulation will save money. In fact, the government's own [2014 report](https://www.gov.uk/government/uploads/system/uploads/attachment_data/file/227069/2901084_SingleMarket_acc.pdf) into the functioning of the single market states "... integration has brought to \[...\] the UK, in most if not all observers’ opinions, appreciable economic benefits". \[...\] Is that trade-off, between cost and benefit, between economics and politics, of overall benefit to the UK? ... Most observers, and indeed most of the evidence received for this report, answer positively." \(p.6\).
1.7.1.3.1.11.9.5. Con: There is no certainty that a more deregulated society is a direction that would win support from the general public, especially when it comes to environmental and labour regulations. A future government hoping to be elected on such a manifesto might not get elected, and therefore, such deregulation may never get implemented. In short, these economical advantages are highly hypothetical and speculative, not just on an economical level, but also on the political level.
1.7.1.3.1.11.10. Pro: The UK will have to create national substitutes of the regulatory institutions that the UK benefits from as members of the EU. The UK will lose out on the economies of scale that the EU has and have costs in setting them up and running them.
1.7.1.3.1.12. Con: The UK would save [billions of pounds](https://fullfact.org/europe/our-eu-membership-fee-55-million/) in annual EU contributions.
1.7.1.3.1.12.1. Con: The net gain from leaving has to be compared to the loss to the economy from leaving the free trade area. The impact of leaving the free trade zone is [estimated](https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2018-01-26/carney-says-u-k-economy-is-feeling-brexit-effect-in-short-term) to be greater than the savings in contributions to the EU  budget.
1.7.1.3.1.12.2. Pro: The UK pays [more money](https://fullfact.org/europe/our-eu-membership-fee-55-million/) into the EU budget than it gets back in EU spending in the UK. In 2016, the UK payed £13.1 billion and the EU in return spent £4.5 billion on the UK.
1.7.1.3.1.13. Con: Many regions in the UK are economically unaffected by EU membership in the first place. Therefore, a hard Brexit is unlikely to economically damage sizeable parts of the UK economy.
1.7.1.3.1.13.1. Pro: Poorer regions where inequality was high and quality of life has declined [usually voted for Brexit](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-36616028), despite the comparatively small impact of the EU in these regions.
1.7.1.3.1.13.1.1. Con: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.7.
1.7.1.3.1.13.1.2. Pro: The EU's guidelines leave the responsibility of dealing with regional inequality in countries down to national governments. It is therefore wrong to blame regional inequality on the EU.
1.7.1.3.1.13.1.2.1. Pro: The EU's Principle of Subsidiarity leaves decisions that are more efficiently taken at a national/regional level down to the respective nations or regions. \([Article 5\(3\) of the Treaty on European Union](http://www.europarl.europa.eu/factsheets/en/sheet/7/the-principle-of-subsidiarity)\)
1.7.1.3.1.13.1.2.1.1. Pro: In the UK, all [17 operations](https://ec.europa.eu/regional_policy/en/information/publications/factsheets/2014/cohesion-policy-and-the-united-kingdom) within the EU's Cohesion Policy \(as part of EU regional management\) are managed directly by the UK itself.
1.7.1.3.1.13.1.3. Pro: London, the area that is most heavily intregated and reliant on European legislation, voted [overwhelmingly to Remain](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-eu-referendum-36612916).
1.7.1.3.1.13.1.4. Pro: Leave voters were disportionately clustered in areas where [immigration ​was low](http://theconversation.com/hard-evidence-how-areas-with-low-immigration-voted-mainly-for-brexit-62138).
1.7.1.3.1.13.1.5. Pro: Stronger than expected support for Leave in Sunderland has been accredited to [rising unemployment, inequality and feeling of being ignored by the Westminster establishment](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ReRQ8DryTWQ).
1.7.1.3.1.14. Pro: The UK's trading relationship with the EU will become less profitable.
1.7.1.3.1.14.1. Pro: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.
1.7.1.3.1.14.2. Pro: Businesses could lose their [passporting](https://www.independent.ie/business/brexit/brexit-means-brexit-everywhere-uk-banks-will-lose-eu-passporting-rights-barnier-36336220.html) rights, which allow them to sell their services across the EU without having to obtain licences in each individual country.
1.7.1.3.1.14.2.1. Pro: Over [5,400](https://www.independent.ie/business/brexit/brexit-means-brexit-everywhere-uk-banks-will-lose-eu-passporting-rights-barnier-36336220.html) British firms rely on passporting rights to bring in £9 billion in revenue every year to Britain.
1.7.1.3.1.14.2.2. Pro: Loss of passporting rights would mean the [immediate end](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-talks-collapse-negotiations-eu-uk-theresa-may-david-davis-no-deal-wto-a7955471.html) to any business UK banks had in the EU.
1.7.1.3.1.14.2.2.1. Pro: As a result, since the Brexit vote, many banks and financial firms have begun establishing EU bases to take their operations [out](http://www.theweek.co.uk/brexit/81116/brexit-and-the-british-economy) of the UK.
1.7.1.3.1.14.2.2.2. Con: Losing passporting rights only [involves a restructuring of operations](http://www.cityam.com/249639/moodys-says-uk-banks-dont-need-eu-passporting-rights) in order to continue offering financial services in Europe.
1.7.1.3.1.14.2.2.3. Con: Zurich, Switzerland, is the [second most competitive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Global_Financial_Centres_Index) banking centre in Europe after London despite not having passporting rights.
1.7.1.3.1.14.2.2.3.1. Con: Switzerland benefits from a more open [freedom of movement agreement](https://www.express.co.uk/news/politics/839032/brexit-news-latest-switzerland-germany-economy-finance-banking-european-union) with the EU than the UK will have after Brexit.
1.7.1.3.1.14.2.2.3.2. Con: Switzerland has made many [ethical trade offs](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2009/feb/05/tax-gap-avoidance-switzerland) to ensure their competitive edge in the banking sector.
1.7.1.3.1.14.2.3. Pro: Services includes much of the UK's computing industry, such as apps sold online.
1.7.1.3.1.14.3. Pro: Britain will default to trading under the worst possible terms with the EU in the immediate aftermath of Brexit.
1.7.1.3.1.14.3.1. Pro: Britain could become a 'third country', meaning trading with the world under the worst possible tariff rules. Even after a period spent establishing its position [under WTO rules](http://ukandeu.ac.uk/explainers/no-deal-the-wto-option/), the UK will almost certainly still suffer.
1.7.1.3.1.14.4. Pro: UK companies that participate in security-sensitive EU industry collaborations would suffer.
1.7.1.3.1.14.4.1. Pro: Britan's satellite industry might very well lose out on the [Galileo](https://www.economist.com/news/britain/21741593-britain-struggles-stay-europes-most-important-space-project-britains-space-industry) project.
1.7.1.3.1.14.4.2. Pro: Airbus has already [announced](https://www.economist.com/news/britain/21741593-britain-struggles-stay-europes-most-important-space-project-britains-space-industry) that it will move part of it's satellite making operations from Britain to France.
1.7.1.3.1.15. Con: Economic forecasts are [inherently unreliable](https://www.theguardian.com/money/2017/sep/02/economic-forecasting-flawed-science-data) due to the infinite range of variables involved. Given that, it is impossible to accurately predict the economic consequences of Brexit, or whether the UK economy would be better off in or out of the EU.
1.7.1.3.1.15.1. Pro: The [Bank of England](https://www.express.co.uk/news/uk/715480/Carney-Brexit-Bank-England-admits-Britain-economic-outlook-POSITIVE-EU) and [David Cameron](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=CDS027L8YMo&app=desktop) made very pessimistic predictions during the referendum about the impact of a leave vote and then later admitted these were overstated.
1.7.1.3.1.15.2. Con: The weather is also governed by a near infinite range of variables and while forecasts are often wrong, they provide a more accurate picture than random guesses.
1.7.1.3.1.15.3. Con: Economic forecasts are not predictions. They are a tool that supports evidence based policy making, such as the [decision](https://www.politicshome.com/news/uk/economy/economic-growth/news/77895/bank-england-cuts-interest-rates-and-boosts-quantitative) of the Bank of England after the referendum vote to cut interest rates and boost quantitive easing, to ward off the worst effects of the fall of the pound.
1.7.1.3.1.16. Con: The UK is too focused on ties to Europe. Diversified international trade deals are more lucrative.
1.7.1.3.1.16.1. Pro: Whilst the EU is a primary trading block, [compared](http://ec.europa.eu/eurostat/statistics-explained/index.php?title=File:Intra_EU_exports_of_goods_compared_with_Extra_EU_exports_of_goods_by_Member_State,_2016_\(%25\).png) to other major EU economies the UK is more tilted to trade outside the EU, giving it more resilience and more opportunities within other trade for good pacts.
1.7.1.3.1.16.2. Pro: Trade in non-physical goods rose \(80%\) from 2006-2016. Whereas physical goods had a 40% increase. So physical proximity will be increasingly [less important](https://www.wto.org/english/res_e/statis_e/wts2017_e/WTO_Chapter_02_e.pdf) to overall trade. It's as easy to do financial services for someone on the other side of the planet as it is with a person in the country next to you.
1.7.1.3.1.16.2.1. Con: Trade in many non-physical goods oftens relies on trade agreements and single market agreements, which is why the [EU is the UK's largest service trading partner](https://www.ons.gov.uk/businessindustryandtrade/internationaltrade/bulletins/exportsandimportsstatisticsbycountryforuktradeinservices/julytoseptember2018). A Hard Brexit would cripple EU-UK service trade.
1.7.1.3.1.16.3. Pro: The 'anglosphere' of countries with English as a primary business language puts the UK at an advantage in new global trade deals with US/Canada/Australia/India/Nigeria.
1.7.1.3.1.16.3.1. Con: Countries within the EU teach English as a second language as it is considered the number one language for business in the world.
1.7.1.3.1.16.4. Con: Even if the US and Britain are able to come to an agreement quickly, Britain is [unable](https://www.cnbc.com/2017/08/22/the-uk-is-preparing-11-post-brexit-trade-deals-and-it-might-all-be-for-nothing.html), per EU rules, to sign new deals until the Brexit process is completed.
1.7.1.3.1.16.4.1. Con: Although Britain cannot officially sign independent trade deals while still a member of the EU, Britain has [informal arrangements](https://www.thesun.co.uk/news/5315524/britain-lines-up-post-brexit-trade-deals/) with 36 countries to implement free trade agreements once Britain officially exits.
1.7.1.3.1.16.4.2. Pro: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.2.1.5.1.1.
1.7.1.3.1.16.4.3. Con: It is unlikely any trade deal with another country will be signed immediately. Negotiations for any trade deal will take time, so the UK should start now rather than starting the process after it leaves the EU.
1.7.1.3.1.16.5. Con: Before the referendum Britain [relied](http://money.cnn.com/2017/07/23/news/economy/us-uk-trade-talks-start/index.html) on experienced EU negotiators to secure lucrative trade deals. Many of Britain's new trade officials are novices, and thus at a disadvantage negotiating new agreements.
1.7.1.3.1.16.5.1. Con: The Department for International Trade has [hired high-calibre trade negotiators](http://www.telegraph.co.uk/business/2017/06/16/uk-hires-top-new-zealand-trade-negotiator-head-post-brexit-deals/) to fill the experience gap.
1.7.1.3.1.16.5.2. Pro: Not only is the British negotiating team not sufficiently qualified, they are also [understaffed](https://www.ft.com/content/f1435a8e-372b-11e7-bce4-9023f8c0fd2e): Whitehall's Trade Policy Group employ's 300 people, but the UK Trade Policy Observatory, an independent think-tank, says they need at least 700 people.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6. Pro: On balance, the EU is a [barrier](https://www.trtworld.com/opinion/how-the-european-union-s-protectionism-is-hurting-developing-economies-26771) to global free trade. A hard Brexit will remove this barrier, allowing the UK to pursue profitable free trade agreements with other countries.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1. Pro: The EU prevents member states from striking their own free trade deals with third countries, so a deal opening borders with India is only possible if the UK leaves.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1. Pro: While within the EU, the UK [can not negotiate](http://brexitcentral.com/uk-trade-agreements-before-brexit/) its own trade deals without consensus of all the member states. This makes it far harder to strike deals with important emerging economies like China and India for example.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.1. Con: The ability to do our own trade deals will only bring in an extra [0.6% GDP](https://www.buzzfeed.com/albertonardelli/the-governments-own-brexit-analysis-says-the-uk-will-be). Far less than the losses from Brexit, especially Hard Brexit.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.2. Con: Trade deals tend to involve the liberalisation of immigration controls. Trade deals with [India](https://www.politico.eu/article/india-no-rush-to-do-bilateral-trade-deal-with-post-brexit-britain-commonwealth-eu-customs-union-external-tariffs/) would almost certainly increase immigration from those countries, to the displeasure of many Brexit voters.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.6.8.1.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.2.2. Pro: There are many different levels of trade deal. The strongest / most expansive Trade deals \(those with the [most advanced stage of economic integration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trade_agreement#By_level_of_integration)\) include eliminating labour barriers \([Common markets, Economic Unions, Economic and Monetary Unions, and Complete economic integrations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_integration#Stages)\).
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.2.3. Con: A very common type of trade deal is a Free Trade Area, which typically does not include "[eliminating barriers for exchange of labour](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economic_integration#Stages)".
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3. Con: The EU is also looking at [partnerships](https://www.politico.eu/article/eu-india-trade-germany-pushes-for-post-brexit-deal/) with India.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.1. Con: Yet such a trade deal is [possible](https://www.politico.eu/article/eu-india-trade-germany-pushes-for-post-brexit-deal/) exactly because the UK is about to leave the EU. The two demands made by India - visa liberalisation and high tariffs on foreign wines and spirits - were heavily resisted by the UK during negotiations in 2013.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2. Pro: The EU has been [working on a free trade agreement with India since 2007](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/India%E2%80%93European_Union_relations#Free_trade_negotiations), which has not been successful after 7 rounds of negotiations. The UK is a weaker economic force - if the EU can't do it, the UK alone can't either.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2.1. Pro: Without the negotiating leverage of the combined EU trade block, the UK will be unlikely to secure new trade deals on as favorable terms as it would have as a member of the EU.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2.1.1. Con: The UK wants free trade, as evidenced by the fact that it was a [key plank](https://digital.library.lse.ac.uk/objects/lse:sav235yoh/read/single#page/3/mode/1up) of the winning Leave campaign. All the protectionist conditions that the EU places on its trading partners are inherently unfavourable in the context of free trade.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2.1.1.1. Con: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.1.1.2.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2.1.2. Pro: A [number of countries](https://www.politico.eu/article/eu-trade-partners-object-to-brexit-transition-roll-over/) which have trade deals with the EU wish to renegotiate terms of any post-Brexit deal with the UK to be more in their favour, and thus worse for the UK.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2.1.2.1. Con: Trade is not a zero sum game. Something that is in favour of a trading partner is not necessarily worse for the UK.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2.1.2.1.1. Pro: The EU charges a 16% tariff on oranges from south Africa to protect Big Spanish Orange. Oranges would be [cheaper](https://iea.org.uk/the-eus-thousands-of-senseless-tariffs-punish-the-poor/) if we left the EU and abolished this tariff, and we'd buy more south African oranges.  This is in the interests of both the UK and our trading partner south Africa.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2.1.3. Pro: As the UK economy is already relatively global and deregulated, Britain has [limited leverage](http://www.independent.co.uk/voices/brexit-trade-deal-thatcher-impact-european-union-single-market-deregulation-a8160026.html) when it comes to negotiating free trade deals.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2.1.3.1. Con: The  narrative portrayed in the cited support article of Britain wanting to "dictate terms" and "have leverage" in trade negotiations is inherently false. Britain wants to pursue a free trade agenda. Protectionist attempts to dictate terms and use leverage are things that protectionist blocs like the EU do. Brexit is an opportunity to trade with the world unfettered by such chains.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2.1.3.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2.1.1.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2.1.3.1.2. Con: Wanting unfettered access to all world markets is all well and good, getting such access is entirely another thing, and [not](https://www.japantimes.co.jp/news/2017/12/20/business/post-brexit-britain-may-struggle-top-japan-eu-free-trade-deal/) [very](http://uk.businessinsider.com/brexit-trump-global-trade-war-uk-trade-deal-almost-impossible-2018-3) [likely](http://uk.businessinsider.com/ambassador-charles-ries-why-a-uk-us-trade-deal-will-be-difficult-2018-1).
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.3. Pro: The EU and India recently [adopted](http://www.consilium.europa.eu/en/press/press-releases/2017/10/06/india-summit-statements/) three joint declarations relating to counterterrorism, climate change, and sustainable development at the 14th EU-India summit.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.4. Con: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2.1.
1.7.1.3.1.16.6.2. Con: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.1.1.2.
1.7.1.3.1.16.7. Con: If we carve up the world in terms wealth, most of the 35 most wealthy are in Europe. The rest are really far away e.g. US. Canada, Australia, China, India and Japan.
1.7.1.3.1.16.8. Con: One of the main reasons to trade with Europe \(rather than other countries\) is distance. Distance equates to fuel and time. Increase either and cost increases, while fuel also increases our carbon footprint.
1.7.1.3.1.16.9. Pro: Commonwealth trade officials are currently pursuing [plans](https://www.theguardian.com/global-development/2017/mar/10/drive-to-replace-eu-trade-links-with-closer-ties-to-commonwealth-economies) for closer economic integration with the UK in the wake of Brexit.
1.7.1.3.1.16.9.1. Con: Good trade agreements are based on trust and goodwill. Theresa May has already damaged that through her "Hostile Environment" policies which led to the Windrush Scandal. "It is a matter of denial of the dignity of citizenship, which really cannot be restored," - Jamaican Prime Minister Andrew Holness. [uk.reuters.com](https://uk.reuters.com/article/uk-britain-chogm-windrush/trouble-in-the-family-uks-migrant-embarrassment-undermines-commonwealth-reunion-idUKKBN1HQ1ZM)
1.7.1.3.1.16.10. Pro: The EU is not the place where most economic growth is occurring. The EU’s share of world GDP is forecast to [decline](http://www.betteroffout.net/the-case/10-eu-myths-about-withdrawl/) to 22% in 2025, down from 37% in 1973.
1.7.1.3.1.16.10.1. Con: This argument confuses growth with total volume in economic activity. Many areas of high relative growth still represent small markets. Even with slower growth relative to the rest of the world, the EU will remain the [UK's largest export market](https://visual.ons.gov.uk/uk-trade-partners/).
1.7.1.3.1.16.10.2. Con: This argument neglects the barriers to trade imposed by geography. Even with falling transport costs, these act to increase the cost of UK goods in more distant markets such as China, Japan, and North and South America.
1.7.1.3.1.16.10.3. Con: This claim misleadingly suggests that EU trade is shrinking, by confusing GDP and growth with share of total world trade.  The EU has a smaller total share of world trade, because countries like China, India and South Korea have grown very quickly and now take much larger shares of total world trade than they used to. As more middle income countries grow, everyone is sharing a much bigger pie, meaning smaller percentages don't automatically mean less pie.
1.7.1.3.1.16.11. Pro: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.
1.7.1.3.1.16.12. Pro: The UK could pursue membership in [NAFTA](https://www.reuters.com/article/us-britain-eu-nafta/britain-could-join-nafta-if-brexit-trade-deal-fails-telegraph-newspaper-idUSKBN1CF18K).
1.7.1.3.1.16.12.1. Con: Joining NAFTA would require the UK to conform to the regulatory framework of the deal for goods and services, a framework that is [significantly different](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/brexit/2017/06/29/could-the-uk-join-nafta-there-are-considerable-downsides/) to the one that the UK currently has as a member of the EU. The highly complex legislative changes would make trade difficult with the EU, and would undermine stability in British markets.
1.7.1.3.1.16.12.1.1. Pro: To benefit from preferential market access into NAFTA, UK exporters would, in certain cases, have to [significantly restructure](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/brexit/2017/06/29/could-the-uk-join-nafta-there-are-considerable-downsides/) their production, supply and distribution chains to comply with the North American trading zone’s product standards and rules of origin. Industries such as the automotive one would have to produce two versions of the same product to export to both EU and NAFTA markets.
1.7.1.3.1.16.12.2. Con: Joining NAFTA would [expose](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/brexit/2017/06/29/could-the-uk-join-nafta-there-are-considerable-downsides/) a range of UK manufacturing and agriculture sectors to highly competitive US and Mexican exports, undermining domestic industry.
1.7.1.3.1.16.12.3. Con: The Trump administration is currently [renegotiating](https://www.nytimes.com/2017/09/28/business/how-the-trump-administration-is-doing-renegotiating-nafta.html) NAFTA. This means that not only will the UK have to wait for those negotiations to complete, it currently cannot know if the deal will actually be advantageous for it to join.
1.7.1.3.1.16.12.4. Pro: Joining NAFTA will increase trade with the US, Mexico, and Canada, whose combined GDP reached GPB 17.2 trillion in 2017. This [outweighs](http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/2017/10/10/britain-could-join-nafta-trade-alliance-us-canada-mexico-post/) the EU, at GPB 15.7 trillion.
1.7.1.3.1.16.12.5. Con: The effect of distance on trade is a well studied issue in economics. Whilst we could boost trade with other countries they will be further away, increasing the costs, so it is unlikely we will ever find ourselves trading as much with NAFTA or other distant trade organisations, as we will with countries in Europe. If we do it will be [more expensive, and more environmentally damaging](https://www.nottingham.ac.uk/credit/documents/papers/11-02.pdf) than if we could trade with the EU.
1.7.1.3.1.16.13. Con: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.2.1.
1.7.1.3.1.16.14. Con: The EU remains Britain's [largest trade partner](https://visual.ons.gov.uk/uk-trade-partners/), accounting for 48% of goods exports from the UK in 2017, justifying Britain's focus on the EU.
1.7.1.3.1.17. Con: Damage to the UK economy was advised by both sides during the campaign, with different degrees of the damage, but the majority decided that other benefits will outweigh short economic disadvantages.
1.7.1.3.1.17.1. Con: Warnings of economic damage were almost always dismissed as 'Project Fear' by Leave politicians. Many people weren't aware of any short term damage to the economy and some Leavers now predict up to 30 years of 'short term' economic pain. [Link](https://twitter.com/OFOCBrexit/status/1009884621306302464?s=19)
1.7.2. Pro: Theresa May has [refused](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/theresa-may-nhs-us-trade-deal-talks-privatisation-healthcare-trump-a8198871.html) to rule out when asked in Parliament if the NHS would be part of any trade deal discussions with America.
1.7.2.1. Pro: US law allows American authorities to [access data held overseas in order to tackle crime and terrorism](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/theresa-may-nhs-us-trade-deal-talks-privatisation-healthcare-trump-a8198871.html), potentially including British patients if they are given greater access to the NHS.
1.7.2.2. Pro: This raises risks of [higher costs of drugs for British patients](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/2018/05/15/trump-threatens-use-us-trade-talks-force-nhs-pay-drugs/) as the US has pushed for higher drug prices in Brexit trade negotiations as part of their ''put American patients first'' scheme.
1.7.3. Con: If the UK will regain control over immigration, the demand side of the equation in the health service can be stopped in its increase. Migration is linked to [82% of population growth between 2001 and 2016](https://www.dailymail.co.uk/news/article-6088861/Migration-82-UKs-population-growth.html) - immigrants and their children. 1991 to 2017 [estimated net not-British immigration into the UK at 6,076,000](https://www.migrationwatchuk.org/statistics-net-migration-statistics).
1.7.3.1. Con: -> See 1.6.1.
1.7.3.2. Con: -> See 1.6.6.
1.7.3.3. Con: A tiny fraction of the UK's immigrants from the EU were from the [over 65 or under 15 age ranges](https://migrationobservatory.ox.ac.uk/resources/briefings/migrants-in-the-uk-an-overview/), the groups that create most of the demand for healthcare. Increased demand for healthcare has been driven almost exclusively by the ageing of the native UK population.
1.7.3.4. Con: The [proportion of dependents was much higher](https://migrationobservatory.ox.ac.uk/resources/briefings/migrants-in-the-uk-an-overview/) in other non-EU groups. If this was a serious concern, then it would be far more effective for the UK to simply limit movement from these regions.
1.7.4. Con: A clean Brexit would enable the UK to move away from applying competition law to the NHS, something which is currently more difficult due to EU regulations \([pg. 4](https://www.kingsfund.org.uk/sites/default/files/field/field_publication_file/procurement-competition-rules-kings-fund-mar-2015.pdf)\).
1.7.4.1. Pro: While the UK itself has competition law that applies to the NHS this [cannot be reformed](https://www.nuffieldtrust.org.uk/research/brexit-relationship-eu-shape-nhs#procurement-and-competition) as it is echoed by European regulations. This could be changed after Brexit.
1.7.4.2. Pro: Competition law [has prevented](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2013/nov/15/competition-killing-nhs-bournemouth-poole) the NHS from enacting mergers that could improve care and reduce costs.
1.7.4.3. Pro: Competition law has allowed private health providers to [challenge](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2013/nov/15/competition-killing-nhs-bournemouth-poole) the NHS for failure to refer services, creating expensive legal fees. This risks clinicians within the NHS experiencing a "[chilling effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chilling_effect)" whereby they are discouraged from referring patients to public providers, over private ones, even when it is the best clinical decision.
1.7.4.4. Con: EU competition law only applies to 'undertakings'. When acting in a way connected with 'the exercise of the powers of a public authority', or providing services based on solidarity, an entity is not an undertaking. The NHS therefore does not have to qualify as an 'undertaking' for the purpose of EU competition law. \([Case CJEU: Wouters v Algemene Raad van de Nederlandsche Orde van Advocaten, , 2002, paragraph 57](http://curia.europa.eu/juris/document/document.jsf;jsessionid=9ea7d2dc30ddb41c0a5e81dc4ed98c5ed39e5d840d7a.e34KaxiLc3qMb40Rch0SaxyNchb0?text=&docid=46722&pageIndex=0&doclang=EN&mode=lst&dir=&occ=first&part=1&cid=239325)\)
1.7.4.4.1. Pro: Scotland and Wales essentially avoid competition law by not treating health services as commercial agreements, but rather as something provided as a public service. This is consistent with the EU regulations and could be followed throughout the UK without Brexit \(Collins, B. 2015 [pg. 5](https://www.kingsfund.org.uk/sites/default/files/field/field_publication_file/procurement-competition-rules-kings-fund-mar-2015.pdf)\)
1.7.5. Con: A hard Brexit could improve the quality of service provision and medical staff.
1.7.5.1. Pro: The EU working time directive, which restricts the amount of work a week to 48 hours, has had significant benefits for healthcare staff but it is also inflexible. This inflexibility has a [negative effect](https://www.rcseng.ac.uk/about-the-rcs/government-relations-and-consultation/working-time-directive-taskforce/) on the training of doctors.
1.7.5.1.1. Con: The minor changes required to make the working time directive more flexible for training are already being [considered by EU bodies](https://www.gov.uk/government/news/more-flexibility-for-nhs-doctors-under-european-working-time-directive) in coordination with UK authorities. Such reforms that are beneficial to the UK can take place within the EU.
1.7.5.2. Pro: After Brexit, the General Medical Council and the Nursing and Midwifery Council could require [competency tests](https://www.nuffieldtrust.org.uk/research/brexit-relationship-eu-shape-nhs#regulation-of-life-sciences-and-health-care) for practitioners who trained in the EU. This is relevant as there is considerable variation in the way doctors are trained across Europe
1.7.5.2.1. Pro: Oncologists in the UK are trained in both radiation and chemotherapy, whereas in some parts of Europe they may focus on one \(House of Commons [pg. 25](https://publications.parliament.uk/pa/cm201617/cmselect/cmhealth/640/640.pdf)\)
1.7.5.3. Con: After Brexit UK doctors, and by extension their patients, will be unable to access the [European Reference Networks](https://ec.europa.eu/health/ern/overview_en). These networks bring together experts from around Europe to assist in caring for patients with rare diseases. This loss will reduce the quality of care these people receive.
1.7.5.4. Con: The [Special EU Programmes Body](https://www.seupb.eu/iva-overview) funds specific initiatives in Northern Ireland that tackle the problems caused by having a border. [These include](https://www.nuffieldtrust.org.uk/research/brexit-relationship-eu-shape-nhs#the-regulation-of-workers) initiatives aimed at supporting children with mental health needs and community care. The vast majority of this funding comes from the EU.
1.7.6. Pro: Brexit will interfere with the supply of goods for the NHS.
1.7.6.1. Pro: Continued EU membership [guarantees](https://news.sky.com/story/pharmacists-preparing-to-stockpile-medicines-and-medical-supplies-for-no-deal-brexit-11441089) the long term security of medicines.
1.7.6.2. Pro: Many goods supplied to the NHS have complex supply chains. [For example](https://www.nuffieldtrust.org.uk/research/brexit-relationship-eu-shape-nhs#customs-and-trade), the main supplier of NHS needles produces them in Plymouth but distributes them from Belgium. A hard Brexit will interrupt this movement of products and increase their cost.
1.7.6.3. Pro: Some medical goods, such as radioisotopes and a number of biological agents, have a shelf-life that's [measured in hours](https://www.nuffieldtrust.org.uk/research/brexit-relationship-eu-shape-nhs#medical-radioisotopes). Any delay at customs could be detrimental.
1.7.6.3.1. Con: The UK government would unquestionably put in place arrangements to prevent customs delay on inward movement of such goods.
1.7.6.4. Pro: The depreciation of the Pound against the Euro has already cost the NHS [considerably](https://www.newstatesman.com/politics/health/2018/01/nhs-already-crisis-brexit-could-finish-it) as at least half of the products used come from overseas. It is [likely](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/business/news/pound-sterling-latest-updates-price-value-brexit-currency-dollar-amundi-fund-manager-a8217381.html) that a hard Brexit would cause a further depreciation of the value of the Pound.
1.7.6.4.1. Pro: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.5.1.
1.7.7. Pro: A large number of British pensioners are able to live in Europe and receive healthcare from European systems because of Britain's membership of the EU. If the UK were to leave, some of these pensioners would have to return, costing the NHS \(on net\) approximately [half a billion pounds](https://www.nuffieldtrust.org.uk/news-item/nhs-could-face-bill-of-over-half-a-billion-pounds-from-brexit).
1.7.7.1. Con: The estimate of increased costs is highly derived and unclear if it is true or not. The report only considers the cost to the NHS of pensioners returning to the UK, getting older and using more services. It does not consider the economic benefit of those pensioners spending their money locally which will bolster UK tax revenue, rather than that of Spain or France.
1.7.7.2. Con: That the NHS is so much less efficient than the health services of other EU countries is a reason for NHS reform, not a reason not to leave the EU.
1.7.7.3. Con: In the grand scheme of things, half a billion pounds is not very much \(it would fund the NHS for around 1.5 days, based on a yearly budget of [£116 billion](https://www.nhs.uk/NHSEngland/thenhs/about/Pages/overview.aspx)\).
1.7.8. Con: Currently new medicines used in the UK need to be approved by the European Medical Authority. After Brexit the equivalent UK body could be [more flexible](https://www.nuffieldtrust.org.uk/research/brexit-relationship-eu-shape-nhs#finding-a-system-that-works-for-medicines) and fast-track the approval of medicines, which had already been approved by other regulators \(including the European and American Authorities\).
1.7.8.1. Con: A pharmaceutical company looking to market their drug will have the choice between two markets: a one-country UK, or a 27-country European Union. Pharmaceutical companies are a business, and the most effective strategy would be to sell to as many countries \(and therefore people\) as possible -- this means the UK will be second-choice \(or later\) in the pecking order for medicine approval, meaning it won't "fast-track" drugs, but instead the converse.
1.7.8.2. Con: -> See 1.5.8.1.3.
1.7.8.3. Con: That is an extremely dangerous practice. Fast tracking medicines without proper approval systems will lead to mistakes being made. The EU has a lengthy approval process to protect peoples health from profit hungry pharmaceutical companies.
1.7.9. Pro: When the UK tightened its immigration policy for non-EU citizens this had a [negative effect](https://fullfact.org/immigration/immigration-and-nhs-staff/) on recruitment of hospital staff. It is likely a restriction on EU immigrants would have the same effect.
1.7.9.1. Con: The UK has [just increased immigration quotas](https://www.theguardian.com/society/2018/jun/14/sajid-javid-confirms-cap-on-foreign-doctors-and-nurses-will-be-lifted) for doctors and nurses from non-EU countries showing that it is not necessary to have completely unfettered free movement in order to effect adequate NHS recruitment from other countries.
1.7.9.1.1. Con: -> See 1.6.1.4.1.
1.7.9.2. Pro: -> See 1.6.9.4.7.2.
1.7.9.3. Pro: From the 2016/17 year to the 2017/2018 year, there has been an [87% reduction](https://www.health.org.uk/news-and-comment/news/uk-public-support-recruitment-of-eu-nurses-post-brexit) in nursing registrants from EU countries since the referendum.
1.7.9.3.1. Con: There have recently been [strict new English proficiency tests](https://www.theguardian.com/society/2017/jun/24/english-speaking-ovserseas-nurses-fail-nhs-too-tough-language-test) that are preventing many people being eligible to apply for nursing positions, and so it is not clear whether the referendum has had a significant impact on nursing applications.
1.7.9.3.2. Con: Numbers of non-EU international nurse recruits has [increased markedly](https://www.health.org.uk/chart/chart-large-drop-in-the-number-of-new-nurses-coming-from-the-eu-to-work-in-the-uk) since the 2016 referendum, and are at their highest levels since 2007/08. In 2017/18 overall international registrants were higher than in 2009/10 or 2010/11, suggesting fears of a collapse in international recruits are overblown.
1.7.9.3.2.1. Con: The source indicates that the inflow of nurses from non-EU countries has not increased enough to [compensate](https://www.health.org.uk/chart/chart-large-drop-in-the-number-of-new-nurses-coming-from-the-eu-to-work-in-the-uk) for the drop from EU nursing applicants.
1.7.9.4. Con: The immigration policy won't be applying to just the eu. The whole point is we have one policy applied to the whole world encouraging those with skills to come here. Hospital staff constitute those with skills and thus there will be allowances for those coming here to fill those jobs. Infact instead of non eu citizens who could work as hospital staff being blocked because numbers of internal non skilled eu immigrants were too high and thus external skilled quotas had to be cut.
1.7.9.5. Con: Looking solely at the number of recruits and not the quality of recruits gives a misleading picture. Recruiting large numbers of staff with poor technical and/or language skills is not a good long term strategy for the NHS.
1.7.9.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.7.9.3.1.
1.7.9.6. Con: EU immigrants make up [only 5% of NHS staff](https://fullfact.org/immigration/immigration-and-nhs-staff/), therefore the effect will not be so pronounced.
1.7.9.6.1. Con: The impact of losing 5% of NHS staff will be more pronounced as the system is already under the strain of staff shortages. [theguardian.com](https://www.theguardian.com/society/2017/dec/13/nhs-faces-acute-and-growing-staffing-shortage-warns-agency)
1.7.9.6.2. Con: The "Hostile environment" towards immigrants created by Theresa May and compounded by Brexit will not only make NHS recruitment much more difficult from the EU, but for medical staff from outside the EU too.
1.7.9.6.2.1. Con: The 'Hostile Environment' refers ONLY to illegal immigrants not to legal ones.["We’re going to give illegal migrants a really hostile reception".](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/uknews/immigration/9291483/Theresa-May-interview-Were-going-to-give-illegal-migrants-a-really-hostile-reception.html)
1.7.9.6.2.1.1. Con: That was the stated goal, but it worked out that lots of legal immigrants were [targeted](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-43782241) too.
1.7.9.6.3. Con: The NHS is one of the largest employers in the world employing just over a million "full time equivalent" posts - "only" 5% is roughly 50,000 FTEs - more than we're already short by for nurses e.g. [40,000 nurse FTEs.](https://fullfact.org/health/are-we-short-40000-nurses/)
1.7.9.7. Pro: About [10% of doctors](http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/election-2017-40015269) come from the EU, leaving this workforce is particularly vulnerable to reductions in immigration.
1.7.9.7.1. Con: -> See 1.7.9.1.
1.7.9.7.2. Pro: Almost a third of newly-registered nurses in the UK had [trained in the EEA](https://www.nuffieldtrust.org.uk/news-item/nhs-could-face-bill-of-over-half-a-billion-pounds-from-brexit). \(2016\)
1.7.10. Pro: The Caribbean island of Anguilla is a British Overseas Territory. It [relies heavily](http://westindiacommittee.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/06/The-White-Paper-on-Anguilla-and-Brexit.pdf) on access to the neighbouring island of St Maarten, which is a French territory and therefore in the EU, especially for medical care. The islanders' access to healthcare will be devastated in the event of a Hard Brexit, as there are currently no backup plans.
1.8. Con: The supremacy of EU law impinges on Britain's ability to make laws to govern itself; by exiting the EU, Britain can make rules on any area it chooses.
1.8.1. Con: EU legislation around areas such as employment rights offers a framework of minimum standards to protect workers. Each EU member can improve on those standards through its own legislation. We already do so on matters such as [holiday pay](https://fullfact.org/europe/eu-and-paid-holidays/#).
1.8.2. Con: The minimal amount of sovereignty Britain may win back from Europe is useless if it inhibits future governments from maintaining British prosperity. A Hard Brexit, with its economic and political consequences, calls this into question.
1.8.3. Pro: The future trajectory of the EU as a political union is incompatible with the UK retaining its sovereignty.
1.8.3.1. Pro: The European Union is [planning on replacing](https://ec.europa.eu/taxation_customs/taxation/decision-making-eu-tax-policy_en) the veto system of taxation for a qualified majority system. This movement of powers up is incompatible with the UK who are attempting to devolve powers such as taxation downwards, see differing income tax between Scotland and England for an example.
1.8.3.1.1. Con: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.13.1.2.1.
1.8.4. Pro: The EU is a [neoliberal](https://www.wsj.com/articles/the-european-unions-neoliberal-dilemma-1507147154) institution. As part of it, Britain is unable to pursue [socialist economic policies](https://www.newstatesman.com/politics/brexit/2017/07/lexit-eu-neoliberal-project-so-lets-do-something-different-when-we-leave-it), even if it was democratically mandated.
1.8.4.1. Con: EU rules do not push privatisation. The EU is [constitutionally required](https://fabians.org.uk/negotiating-the-red-lines/) to be neutral as regards public ownership or privatisation, and doesn’t prevent nationalisation of already privatised companies or the setting up of new ones.
1.8.4.1.1. Con: The EU [restricts interventionist government](http://ec.europa.eu/competition/state_aid/overview/index_en.html).
1.8.4.2. Pro: The [ban on tariffs](https://europa.eu/youreurope/business/sell-abroad/import-export/index_en.htm) means countries cannot pursue protectionist economic policies to ensure domestic industries survive.
1.8.4.2.1. Con: The single market removes tariffs for its members, which is possible because it creates a level playing field with regulations \(e.g. on labour, standards and the environment\). There still are protective tariffs for exports originating from outside the EU, where rules are different, to prevent those countries gaining a competitive advantage over the EU countries \(cheap labour creates cheap products\).
1.8.4.2.2. Con: The UK does not like protective measures. It [rejected](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/tata-steel-uk-government-accused-of-failing-to-protect-british-workers-by-blocking-eu-plans-to-allow-a6962446.html) the option to put tariffs on steel to protect the domestic steel industry, even when the EU proposed it.
1.8.4.3. Pro: The EU pursued [austerity policies](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2015/jul/09/greece-debt-crisis-athens-accepts-harsh-austerity-as-bailout-deal-nears) in Greece, [against](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2015/jun/19/greek-people-austerity-eu-greece-economic-crisis-democracy) the Greek people's wishes. This was made possible [by the ECB](https://www.politico.eu/article/greece-agrees-to-new-bailout-deal/), an EU institution.
1.8.4.3.1. Con: When any institution makes a loan they need to ensure such a loan can/will be repaid. It would be highly irresponsible for any organisation not to do so. The Greeks had to raise taxes to cover their spending, or reduce their spending.
1.8.4.3.2. Con: The additional loan given to the Greeks was the European Central Bank 's own money, thus they got to choose the terms. If the Greeks had refused then they would have simply defaulted, been forced into bankrupty, and then be forced into even worse austerity
1.8.4.3.3. Con: The ECB is part of the same system the national banks of [every EU member state, including the UK](https://www.citizensinformation.ie/en/government_in_ireland/european_government/eu_institutions/overview_of_eu_institutions.html)(The ECB is part of the European System of Central Banks, which also includes the national central banks of all EU member states, whether they have adopted the euro or not.). Therefore member state governments can influence the ECB, and must be at least complicit with it's actions.
1.8.4.4. Con: EU employment law provides some of the [strongest safeguards](https://www.theguardian.com/careers/2016/may/24/what-would-leaving-eu-mean--employment-rights) for workers in the world.
1.8.4.5. Con: The past six UK prime ministers \([Thatcher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neoliberalism#United_Kingdom), [Major](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neoliberalism#United_Kingdom), [Blair](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2003/aug/06/society.labour), [Brown](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2014/dec/04/gordon-browns-legacy-rampant-neoliberalism), [Cameron](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/politicsandpolicy/david-cameron-and-the-neoliberal-undercurrents-of-state-austerity/), and [May](http://www.independent.co.uk/voices/theresa-may-tory-manifesto-2017-david-cameron-right-wing-social-care-a7743146.html)\) have run government's with neoliberal policies. The UK does not want to pursue socialists economic policies, so it's ability to do so has never actually come under consideration.
1.8.4.6. Con: The EU is only a sum of the wills of its member states. If the UK citizenry wants to make the EU have more left-wing or protectionist policies, they can vote that way such that their domestic government and EU representatives will advocate for them.
1.8.4.6.1. Con: Whilst it would be possible for a future UK Socialist government and Socialist MEPs to influence the EU, this alone would not be enough to make a significant difference.
1.8.4.6.1.1. Pro: The majority of the EU countries have centre-right or right leaning parties in government, and extreme nationalist and populist parties like AfD are gaining footholds all over Europe.
1.8.4.6.1.1.1. Con: Political trends are fluid. Sometimes there's an authoritarian trend, sometimes there's a democratic trend. The parent assumes that just because the atmosphere is such and such today it will stay just like that frozen in time.
1.8.4.7. Con: EU membership hasn't prevented Sweden and Denmark's strong workers' rights, nor the Universal Basic Income in Finland.
1.8.5. Pro: EU laws [have supremacy](https://fullfact.org/europe/eu-law-and-uk/) over the national laws of the UK.
1.8.5.1. Con: All supranational laws override national laws. International laws have to override national laws in order to be effective, otherwise governments that break international law can just point to their own national laws and say they acted legally deeming international laws useless.
1.8.5.1.1. Con: Much of international law is consent-based governance. This means that a state member is not obliged to abide by this type of international law, [unless it has expressly consented](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_law) to a particular course of conduct, usually through [ratification](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ratification) \(making international law/agreements into national law\).
1.8.5.1.2. Pro: And Trade Agreements have supremacy over national laws of the UK. And we will have Trade Agreements anyway.
1.8.5.2. Con: Despite EU laws technically having supremacy over domestic law, the UK wields substantial influence over both.
1.8.5.2.1. Pro: True, but as a large and influential EU member state, UK has a big say in the laws that apply EU-wide.
1.8.5.2.2. Pro: Many of the EU laws and regulations supposedly imposed on the UK were heavily influenced by UK officials working in Whitehall and Brussels. The UK has [disproportionate influence](http://ukandeu.ac.uk/explainers/is-the-uk-marginalised-in-the-eu-2/) across a range of policy areas in the EU and has used this to establish common policies across the EU that benefit the UK.
1.8.5.2.2.1. Con: The problem is that once these laws and regulations are established, they are almost impossible to change as they require consensus across the EU. This means that a change in UK government cannot undo what was wanted by a previous government, undermining democracy.
1.8.5.2.3. Pro: On the votes in the European Parliament, the majority agreed with the UK in [95% of cases](https://fullfact.org/europe/eu-facts-behind-claims-uk-influence/). In 2%, the UK did not express an opinion.
1.8.5.2.3.1. Con: This still does not mean the UK has any power over the EU because it can't revoke the law when its citizens disagree, therefore it does not have sovereignty.
1.8.5.2.3.2. Con: Because the UK agreed with 95% of all cases does not necessarily mean that in those cases the policies were the most suitable for the UK, as the EU must provide policies that work to some extent for all members. The UK can agree with an EU policy and still believe an alternative option would work better for the UK.
1.8.5.2.4. Pro: Today, the British government remains solely responsible for the majority of policy actions that matter to voters, such as [health, welfare, pensions, border security, defense, and monetary policy](https://www.chathamhouse.org/sites/files/chathamhouse/publications/research/2016-05-09-britain-eu-sovereignty-myth-niblett.pdf).
1.8.5.2.5. Pro: The United Kingdom controls more than 98 per cent of its public expenditure. \(Niblett, 2016, [pg. 1](https://www.chathamhouse.org/sites/files/chathamhouse/publications/research/2016-05-09-britain-eu-sovereignty-myth-niblett.pdf)\).
1.8.5.3. Con: Much like in the US Federal laws override state laws. Or UK laws override local council laws.
1.8.6. Con: Brexit legislation has weakened parliamentary sovereignty. Government ministers can amend or repeal legislation without the need for parliamentary scrutiny, known as ["Henry VIII laws"](http://blogs.lse.ac.uk/brexit/2016/11/30/rights-for-the-chop-how-a-henry-viii-clause-in-the-great-repeal-bill-will-undermine-democracy/).
1.8.7. Con: It is unrealistic to expect that Britain will have perfect supremacy over its own laws, irrespective of its relationship with the EU.
1.8.7.1. Pro: With the EU being our [largest trading partner](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_the_largest_trading_partners_of_United_Kingdom), we are likely to align our laws to make trading easier if we were outside it.  We are going to end up with most EU legislation being enacted, we will just be losing the ability to influence those laws.
1.8.7.1.1. Con: Even if current EU legislation is incorporated into UK law, the UK parliament can amend or repeal those laws in the future whenever there is will to do so and any further EU legislation can \(or not\) be incorporated at the will of parliament.
1.8.7.1.2. Con: The UK will still be able to make its own laws in sectors apart from trade, such as the environment, taxes, education, and justice, to name a few.
1.8.7.2. Pro: Leaving the EU will likely lead to an increase in the influence of actors other than the British public on UK law.
1.8.7.2.1. Pro: The UK will have to strike deals with both the EU and third countries, which will require it to make concessions. It will probably have to accept, to some extent, the laws of the EU, especially if it wants to trade. It may even have to accept some regulatory alignment with third countries. The ability to choose not to be subjected to those rules is limited by the power the UK has vis-à-vis other countries. The UK may have less power on its own than as part of the EU.
1.8.7.2.2. Pro: The US food industry has already proposed bringing chlorinated, contaminated chicken into the UK. It’s going to be far more likely that the government [will accept lowered food standards from third countries](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2017/jul/26/uk-us-trade-deal-chlorinated-chicken-michael-gove-liam-fox?CMP=Share_iOSApp_Other) for the bump in GDP that results from the trade. Without the EU to trade with and the protections and standards they provide, it’s going to be tempting to sacrifice a bit of public health for the cash offered by a powerful US food lobby.
1.8.7.2.3. Pro: Multinational companies will be able to exert leverage on UK employment law by threatening to move jobs out of the UK, whereas they would be less able to use this negotiating tactic to influence EU-wide employment laws.
1.8.7.2.4. Con: Any marginal increase in external lobbying represents less of a problematic influence than that of the EU's direct sovereignty over UK institutions.
1.8.7.2.4.1. Pro: External lobbying only attempts to influence the UK, and does not have direct authority to mandate a change to UK law. Thus, the influence of lobbying is more easily resisted and less of a significant threat to sovereignty.
1.8.7.2.4.1.1. Con: Evidence from the [US](http://www.businesspundit.com/10-of-the-biggest-lobbies-in-washington/) indicates that lobbying groups can have a very strong influence on laws and government spending.
1.8.7.2.5. Pro: Some prospective trading partners prefer arbitration of agreements/treaties pertaining to trade to be conducted by [industry panels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Investor-state_dispute_settlement), not courts. This not only reduces transparency and therefore accountability, it also allows foreign private companies to sue the government for changing laws that they deem to be detrimental to their profits/competitiveness.
1.8.7.2.6. Pro: The UK government will still want to influence EU laws and regulations, but will do that by lobbying. This occurs mostly behind closed doors, and is therefore less transparent and accountable than having UK citizens in the European Parliament, and in various other positions in the EU.
1.8.7.3. Pro: It is politically naive to suggest that any country enjoys "full" sovereignty. All countries across the world sacrifice or pool sovereignty when they enter into trade treaties or international institutions, such as NATO, as those treaty obligations override domestic law. What is being asked for here is not achievable and not desirable.
1.8.8. Con: This aim is unlikely to be achieved as new trade deals bring in new regulations. Examples include American patent law and ability for coporates to sue governments.
1.8.9. Con: The extra layer of jurisdiction means that UK citizens can appeal to a higher court if they feel domestic law does not implement the rights of citizens that are described in EU law correctly, such as [equal pay](http://www.equalpayportal.co.uk/the-law/).
1.8.9.1. Con: The ECJ takes a purposive approach to interpreting EU regulation that expands its authority rather than respecting member states' sovereignty [\(Alter, p. 656\)](https://scholarship.law.duke.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=3083&context=faculty_scholarship).
1.8.10. Pro: The EU consists of member states that often have conflicting interests with clear winners and losers in any given piece of legislation, regulation, etc. It is better for nation states to control their own destinies.
1.8.10.1. Con: EU legislation undergoes fierce bargaining and is a result of compromise more often than not. In most cases member states ask for something in return in order to agree with some legislation.
1.8.10.2. Con: Decision making on a clearly national basis wouldn't avoid competition between national interests, and would facilitate zero sum situations as bigger nations would have no institutional checks on their power. This would only deprive EU nations of the platform and means to negotiate their differences in a peacefull, effective and mutually beneficial way.
1.8.10.3. Con: No nation state controls its own destiny in an[intensely globalized](https://www.theatlantic.com/international/archive/2018/03/trump-tariff-europe/555146/) world. [Giant players on the global stage](http://foreignpolicy.com/2016/03/15/these-25-companies-are-more-powerful-than-many-countries-multinational-corporate-wealth-power/) \(e.g. Exxon-Mobil, Microsoft, General Electric\), [manipulate national politics](http://www.transparency.org.uk/our-work/uk-corruption/lobbying/#.WrVYhpdpHtQ), preferring smaller, weaker states \(less likely to regulate and tax them\). The British electorate's interests are low priority for them, as opinions are easily manipulated via the popular press. Multilateral Organisations allow national governments to collaborate and retain power to act in the people's interests.
1.8.10.4. Con: Inside or outside of the EU, Britain is still beholden to the same tides of international politics and economics \(which are outside of the control of any one state\) that drive the rules and policies of individual states. As an EU member, Britain wields far more power and authority to deal with trans-national players \(e.g. corporations and their lobbyists, rebel groups, cross-border rogue states etc\) and navigate these tides than if it stands alone.
1.8.10.4.1. Pro: No trade agreement exists that does not have an arbitrator that forces changes of laws in the participating countries. Even falling back to World Trade Organisation would mean other countries have effects on our laws.
1.8.10.4.1.1. Con: MPs could hold the outcomes of the deal to account and have repealed it if it was unpopular. Within the EU, they cannot.
1.8.10.5. Pro: Even big corporations are bound by law, wether it be national or international, thus ways remain to limit their capacity for interference in national politics.
1.8.10.6. Pro: The EU itself lacks transparency and is very much affected by [lobbying](https://lobbyfacts.eu/charts-graphs) from big corporations. It is not an exaggeration to say that there is even less control from the people in this instance than there would be were the national state the only institution to be considered, in the absence of the EU.
1.9. Pro: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.
1.10. Con: The institutions of the EU are not representative of modern Britain.
1.10.1. Pro: The bureaucratic and elected institutions of the EU [demonstrate a distinct lack of ethnic diversity](https://www.dawn.com/news/1388420), particularly when UK-originating MEPs/employees are excluded. The UK should not be part of an institution which lacks diversity to this extent.
1.10.1.1. Pro: Currently only [5% of the MEPs are from ethnic minorities](https://www.europeaninterest.eu/article/meet-national-racial-minorities-new-european-parliament/) \(despite these people making up 10% of the total population\), and a fifth of these \(around 7 MEPs\) are from the UK.
1.10.1.1.1. Con: In the last European parliament only 1.6% of MEPs were from ethnic minorities. Now it's 5%. That's a 312% improvement in representation in one election cycle.
1.10.1.2. Pro: Six out of the seven different 'Presidents' of the EU are all [white and male](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_presidents_of_the_institutions_of_the_European_Union).
1.10.1.2.1. Con: This is not an EU issue, the EU is relatively young, compare the EU to other diplomatic organisations. Britain has had no prime ministers of colour and only two women in it's history.
1.10.1.2.1.1. Pro: [The UK has only had](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_the_United_Kingdom#Since_1721) 2 \(out of 75\) female prime ministers since 1721. \(Margaret Thatcher, Theresa May\).
1.10.1.2.1.1.1. Con: Over the last 40 years, the UK has had a female prime minister for [13 of those years](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Prime_Ministers_of_the_United_Kingdom), which is over 30% of the time. The EU has had female Presidents for zero of those years.
1.10.1.2.1.1.2. Con: The UK has had a series of incredibly well respected and powerful queens who ruled as head of state since [1558](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elizabeth_I_of_England).
1.10.1.2.1.2. Pro: The UK has only had white prime ministers.
1.10.1.2.1.2.1. Con: The UK had its first ethnic minority Prime Minister and first person from an ethnic minority background to hold one of the Great Offices of State in [1868](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benjamin_Disraeli).
1.10.1.2.1.3. Con: -> See 1.10.1.2.1.1.1.
1.10.1.3. Pro: The EU has been [accused of being blind to](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/jul/27/eu-criticised-leaving-out-ethnic-minorities-diversity-drive) [ethnic minorities](https://www.politico.eu/article/brussels-blind-to-diversity-whiteout-european-parliament/).
1.10.1.4. Con: This is a global problem that is not unique to these institutions, and therefore not a reason to leave the EU.
1.10.1.4.1. Con: The EU bureaucracy is a particularly bad example, which is paying lip service to the problem while continuing in the same worrying vein [favouring white men over woman and ethnic minorities for positions of real power](https://www.politico.eu/article/the-european-commission-gender-equality-6-men-no-women-promoted-to-top-eurocrat-grade/).  As the unelected bureaucratic body which in some respects wields more power than the UK parliament,  we should not accept its power until it has genuinely reformed.
1.10.1.4.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.10.1.1.
1.10.1.4.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.10.1.2.
1.10.1.4.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.10.1.3.
1.10.1.4.1.4. Con: The best way to influence change in the organisation is by being a member.
1.10.1.4.1.4.1. Con: David Cameron already tried proposing changes and [failed to extract meaningful concessions](https://www.conservativehome.com/parliament/2017/02/camerons-renegotiation-took-place-a-year-ago-heres-our-take-on-it-from-the-time.html).
1.10.1.4.1.4.1.1. Con: Mr Cameron secured the [“emergency brake/Red Card”](https://brexit853.wordpress.com/2016/09/27/powers-that-the-uk-has-failed-to-use-to-control-eu-freedom-of-movement-directive/) mechanism which allowed any EU country whose welfare system has come under strain, as the result of an influx of EU migrants of ‘an exceptional magnitude’, to restrict access to certain kinds of welfare benefits.
1.10.1.4.1.4.1.2. Con: It was recognised that the United Kingdom was [exempt](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-eu-referendum-35622105) to further political integration into the European Union.
1.10.1.4.1.4.2. Con: -> See 1.4.3.2.1.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3. Pro: Britain will have significantly less influence in Europe if it leaves the EU, and as a result will be a weaker nation in the international community.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.1. Con: Influence is complex and multifactorial, and therefore challenging to predict. The view that the UK's post-Brexit future influence will wane may therefore reflect cognitive bias.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.1.1. Pro: Given the size of the UK economy, the EU may be forced to respond to unilateral decisions taken by the UK.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.1.1.1. Pro: The EU has [threatened to sanction](https://www.investmentweek.co.uk/investment-week/news/3017883/uk-to-develop-distinct-regulatory-framework-post-brexit) the UK if it liberalises its economy post-Brexit, illustrating they fear the power and influence the UK will have post-Brexit.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.1.1.1.1. Con: The idea to treat a country that aims to become "an uncooperative tax haven" as "an uncooperative tax jurisdiction" seems a pragmatic, rather than fearful, response.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.1.1.2. Pro: Unshackled by the EU's protectionist rules, the UK could lead by example with economic and trade liberalisation, providing a [beacon to economic growth](https://www.economistsforfreetrade.com/News/brexit-could-boost-uk-economy-by-135-billion-say-top-economists/) that the EU could follow should they wish to replicate our success.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.1.1.2.1. Con: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.5.1.1.1.1.1.1.2.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.1.1.2.2. Con: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.1.2. Pro: Agreements reached between the UK and other countries may directly influence EU policy.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.1.3. Con: As a member of the EU the UK has a veto in many important areas of policy, and a vote in many more. It's nonsensical to suggest that losing veto power can add influence.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.1.4. Con: Many things are complex and multifactorial and challenging to predict. This doesn't mean you can't take a measured view on them. It seems highly likely that wielding less economic power post-Brexit will result in wielding less influence.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.2. Pro: The UK owns a market composed of 65 million people; the USA owns a market composed of 300 million people; the EU owns a market composed of 500 million people; China owns a market composed of 1 billion people: the larger your market is, the more powerful you are. Outside the EU, the UK will lose its bargaining power.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.2.1. Con: Economy size is only one metric of how much other countries want to trade with you. The other important metric is ease of doing business.  The UK together with the US are the [only G7 countries that make the top 10 list](http://www.doingbusiness.org/reports/global-reports/~/media/WBG/DoingBusiness/Documents/Annual-Reports/English/DB18-Chapters/DB18-Overview.pdf), indicating how desirable the UK economy is and how much other countries will want to do business with us. Germany only ranks 20th, France 31st and Italy 46th. The UK is both very big and a very easy place to do business.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.2.1.1. Con: Of course it is, but think about losing all the facilities that the EU offers to the UK \(for example, [two european agencies](http://www.consilium.europa.eu/en/policies/relocation-london-agencies-brexit/)\), and also think about all the cuts prepared by the English government. We can't be sure the UK will keep being a good place to do business.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.2.1.1.1. Con: It is the UK and not the EU that has made the UK a good place to do business, therefore fear mongering about what a future UK government may do outside of the EU is misguided.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.2.1.1.1.1. Con: Brexit is making the UK a less good place to do [business](https://www.verdict.co.uk/which-companies-could-leave-the-uk-because-of-brexit/).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.2.1.2. Con: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.16.8.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.2.1.3. Con: The UK already does business with plenty of other countries under the existing EU arrangements. It is not clear that the desirablity of the UK for external trade will increase as a result of Brexit.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.2.2. Con: Value and currency strength are also relevant to an assessment of a market. So one million UK citizens are worth far more than one million Chinese citizens in market terms.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.3. Con: Britain's soft-power and diplomatic relations with the Commonwealth, the Anglosphere and many other nations outside the EU make it if not the pre-eminent global soft-power, then a close second or third.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.3.1. Pro: A [report](https://softpower30.com) by Portland, a communications strategy firm working for governments and large corporations, currently ranks the UK as the nation with the most soft power in the world. This makes the UK well placed to weather the transition, and suggests a hard Brexit would not meaningfully affect its influence.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.3.2. Con: The Commonwealth has been shown to be an extremely weak institution, thus it does not grant the UK much 'softpower'.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.3.2.1. Pro: The [Commonwealth Charter](http://thecommonwealth.org/our-charter) aims to stop discrimination of all kinds, however Homosexuality remains a criminal offence in [35 of 53](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/LGBT_rights_in_the_Commonwealth_of_Nations#United_Kingdom) Commonwealth states.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.3.2.2. Pro: Several Commonwealth and former Commonwealth members have broken the [Harare Declaration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Harare_Declaration), despite the Commonwealth's objections. \[[1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations#Membership)\]
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.3.2.3. Pro: The commonwealth is comprised of 52 member states, none of which have any greater decision-making power than the other. This hinders Britain's ability to impose its political will on those nations.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.3.3. Con: The Anglosphere is currently pursuing a diverse range of political directions and agendas. It is unlikely that the UK can significantly direct individual countries, let alone the group as a whole.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.4. Pro: Leaving the EU would mean the UK probably could not go back if Brexit was a total disaster. Remaining would mean the UK could still leave if membership proved to get worse in the future. The best option is to stay and use veto to agitate for change and improve the EU as a full, key member.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.5. Con: The UK's negotiating position in the EU will likely be extremely poor. Cancellation of Article 50 will not make everyone forget about the whole affair.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.6. Pro: The UK will have less value to the US as it will no longer be its [window](https://www.usatoday.com/story/news/world/2016/06/20/brexit-european-union-united-states-europe/85976276/) to the European Union.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7. Con: Britain's foreign policy focus on Europe crowds out its ability to pursue partnerships with the [commonwealth](https://www.express.co.uk/news/politics/769151/Commonwealth-rebirth-Brexit-Britain-shun-EU-Queen-countries-Article-50-trade).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.1. Con: The commonwealth is a far less politically influential actor to be allied with than the EU.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.1.1. Pro: The combined GDP of the Commonwealth is only [$10,923 billion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Commonwealth_of_Nations_countries_by_GDP_\(nominal\)), compared to the EU's GDP of [$18,800 billion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Economy_of_the_European_Union). The latter is clearly the far stronger power.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.1.2. Con: The commonwealth as a group is not, but individual countries in it are — Australia, Canada, India, South Africa, etc.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.2. Con: The commonwealth is currently far less unified than the EU, making joint decisions on foreign policy far more difficult.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.3.2.3.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.2.2. Con: Because it’s not the same goal. Precisely what is criticized in the EU is the amount of control and influence it has on national politics.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.3. Con: The UK already holds significant political influence with commonwealth states.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.3.1. Con: The UK has come under criticism by Commonwealth nations in recent years due to its imperialist legacy, with prominent political parties in South Africa [boycotting](https://buzzsouthafrica.com/blacks-are-illiterates-unemployed-and-dying-because-of-whites/) Commonwealth conferences and traditions such as the Commonwealth games coming under [attack](http://allafrica.com/stories/201611240193.html). The political atmosphere makes future cooperation more difficult.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.3.2. Pro: The commonwealth's [headquarters](http://www.bbc.com/news/world-africa-16842428) are in London.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.3.3. Pro: Of the commonwealth member states, Britain [provides](https://www.newstatesman.com/politics/staggers/2017/02/commonwealth-global-britain-sounds-nostalgia-something-else) the most funds.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.4. Pro: The combined population of the commonwealth is [larger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations) than that of the EU.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.4.1. Con: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.1.1.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.5. Con: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.16.6.1.1.3.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.6. Con: In the status quo, Britain acts as a voice for the Commonwealth countries' interests within the EU. Commonwealth states in the African, Caribbean, and Pacific Group \(ACP\) benefit particularly from this arrangement, which has [led](https://www.thenational.ae/opinion/comment/the-commonwealth-a-dormant-giant-looks-towards-the-post-brexit-future-1.672397) to beneficial trade deals with the EU as a whole based on their historic association with Britain. Brexit robs these countries of their primary advocate in the bloc.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.7. Con: The Caribbean nations depend heavily on tariff-free trade with Britain under the Economic Partnership Agreement. A hard Brexit would leave [little time](http://www.ramphalinstitute.org/uploads/2/3/9/9/23993131/caribbean_hangover.pdf) to renegotiate this, harming British-Caribbean relations.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.7.1. Con: If it's an agreement already in place with the EU then they have the opportunity to agree it continues as it currently exists with the UK on a bilateral basis until something new is negotiated.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.8. Pro: -> See 1.7.1.3.1.2.1.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.9. Pro: A hard Brexit will adversely affect the interests of countries outside of Europe, leading to a fall in Britain's standing within the international community.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.9.1. Pro: The possibility of a hard Brexit has already thrown a trade deal with African states into question, adversely affecting [Kenya's flower exports](https://www.ft.com/content/01beeb54-5347-11e6-9664-e0bdc13c3bef) to the EU, a major part of its economy.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.9.2. Pro: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.7.7.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.10. Pro: Having a large say in the EU has allowed the UK to shape the policy interests of a larger and more powerful entity, giving Britain more influence on the international stage. Withdrawing from this position would likely reverse these effects, reducing British influence.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.10.1. Con: The UKs influence is derived as much from our global 'soft power' and alliance with the United States as anything else. The UK's ability to project hard power comes from its status as a nuclear power. The UK's status as a 'bridge' between the US and EU is much overhyped - witness the recent trade dispute of which the UK is merely a bystander. Freed from the EU, the UK can critique the EUs tendency to exploit its trade power with the global south.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.10.2. Pro: Baroness Ashton, a British Labour peer, was the first [High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High_Representative_of_the_Union_for_Foreign_Affairs_and_Security_Policy#Catherine_Ashton_\(2009%E2%80%9314\)), the most senior foreign affairs official in the EU following the Treaty of Lisbon.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.10.3. Pro: Not having a say within the EU could reduce the UK's leverage with Washington. The US [currently](http://www.cer.eu/insights/impact-brexit-eu) sees the UK as a bridge between themselves and continental Europe.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.10.4. Pro: While within the EU, Britain has sizable representation in the European parliament \([73 seats](http://www.europarl.europa.eu/unitedkingdom/en/your-meps/european_elections.html)\), as well as throughout all EU institutions.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.10.5. Pro: EU membership has enabled Britain to pursue critical foreign policy objectives as part of a more formidable bloc, such as confronting Russia over its actions in Ukraine or addressing the Iran nuclear program.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.10.5.1. Pro: Britain has been the most vocal supporter within the EU of sanctions against Russia, [according](http://foreignpolicy.com/2016/06/26/brexit-is-good-news-for-russia-but-a-headache-for-nato/) to Moscow's mayor Sergei Sobyanin.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.11. Con: The EU has shaped British foreign policy more than Britain has shaped European foreign policy. Speaking with an independent voice will give the UK the ability to lead by example and forge new partnerships rather than continually compromise with other EU countries.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.11.1. Pro: The [Common Foreign and Security Policy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_Foreign_and_Security_Policy) is the organised foreign policy of the EU. Decisions require unanimity in the European Council, however once agreed aspects of the policy can be decided by qualified majority voting which ultimately means that Britain does not exercise complete control over its foreign policy.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.11.2. Pro: -> See 1.4.3.13.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.11.3. Con: As a member of the EU, Britain has always tried to adopt a [foreign policy](http://carnegieeurope.eu/strategiceurope/72665) that would ensure a balance of power in continental Europe.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.12. Con: Britain is still in NATO, giving it many forums and connections for security policy coordination.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.12.1. Con: As a result of Brexit, the UK is at risk of losing its [most senior held position](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/home-news/brexit-latest-news-uk-lose-most-senior-nato-military-position-eu-european-union-deputy-supreme-a7519196.html) in NATO.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.12.2. Pro: Because Brexit means that the UK will no longer have to support EU military endeavors, they will have [more](http://foreignpolicy.com/2016/06/25/europes-loss-is-natos-gain/) martial resources to deploy in the service of NATO. This additional support ensures that Britain remains able to access NATO resources and maintain its standing outside the EU.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.12.2.1. Con: More martial resources for NATO comes at the expense of the EU's defence, making this a zero sum game. Other EU countries may have to redirect efforts away from NATO to shore up holes left by the British.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.12.3.
