Con: The Trump Administration has consistently [expressed doubts](http://www.slate.com/articles/news_and_politics/war_stories/2017/12/trump_went_back_to_nato_bashing_in_pensacola_speech.html) about the future of the alliance, making it an unreliable plan for British security moving forward.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.12.3.1. Con: Despite recent rhetorical grandstanding, the United States has taken [concrete steps](http://foreignpolicy.com/2018/01/29/trumps-shadow-hangs-over-nato-transatlantic-alliance-europe-defense-deterrence-europe-mattis-jens-stoltenberg/) to continue to support the alliance.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13. Con: Remaining in the EU would harm our democracy and humiliate the UK on the world stage.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.1. Con: Remaining could correspond to the democratic will \(expressed through a vote, through parliament, …\)
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.2. Con: Leaving in a way which is harmful to the UK may do even more damage to the UK's reputation.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.3. Con: Both remaining or leaving might harm democracy, and the UK is already humiliated by an incompetent negotiating team. Concerning 'humiliation on the world stage', What could be more humiliating for 'The Mother of Parliaments' and 'The home of Democracy', than the spectacle of an undemocratic government railroading through a decision that the population now probably regrets but will have been denied the democratic right to recant/confirm a possible 'No deal', bail out without a parachute. [Poll](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-poll-voters-eu-leave-wrong-theresa-may-negotiations-deal-yougov-a8383826.html)
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4. Pro: The British public voted to leave the EU. Democratic will requires that the outcome of the referendum must be respected, thus the UK must leave the EU regardless of the deal secured.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1. Pro: Remaining in the EU would undermine the UK citizens' faith in democracy.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.1. Con: If a decision to remain in the EU was framed as an acknowledgment that the promises made by the leave side in the referendum campaign were entirely unachievable, and that therefore to remain is "the best possible deal", this could reinforce or strengthen democracy.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.2. Pro: The Remain voters tended to come from the "[elite classes](https://lordashcroftpolls.com/2016/06/how-the-united-kingdom-voted-and-why/)" with higher levels of education or held professional/managerial roles. Choosing to remain could be seen as the popular will being overcome by the wishes of the elite.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.2.1. Con: The key figures behind the Leave campaign \(politicans, donors and media owners\) are all very wealthy and could well be argued are part of the "elite".
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.2.1.1. Con: Members of the elite establishment were present on both sides of the debate. That does not change that fact that Remain voters were more likely to have [gone to university](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2017/oct/30/facts-support-mps-claim-that-better-educated-voted-remain-pollster), and were [on average wealthier](https://www.spectator.co.uk/2016/07/brexit-voters-are-not-thick-not-racist-just-poor/).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.2.2. Con: Whilst yes the ABs \([The "Elite" classes](https://lordashcroftpolls.com/2016/06/how-the-united-kingdom-voted-and-why/)\) were the only social group to have a remain majority \(57%\), a much more decisive predictor of wishing to remain was age. 73% of 18-24s, 62% of 25-34s and 52% of 35-45s.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.2.3. Con: The pro-Brexit campaign was heavily support by wealthy individuals, largely being [funded](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-leave-eu-campaign-arron-banks-jeremy-hosking-five-uk-richest-businessmen-peter-hargreaves-a7699046.html) by five of the UK's wealthiest business men. Nigel Farage, perhaps the most prominent Leave voice, is [personally very wealthy](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2017/dec/16/nigel-farage-skint-ukip-4m-townhouse).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.2.3.1. Con: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.2.1.1.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.2.4. Con: So called ''elite classes'', by nature of their higher level of education, would be more likely to be properly informed on issues.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.2.5. Con: Most of those [working voted Remain](https://www.ipsos.com/ipsos-mori/en-uk/how-britain-voted-2016-eu-referendum).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.3. Pro: A majority of voters who did not vote in the 2015 election voted in the Brexit referendum. 60% of this group voted leave. A decision to not leave the European Union may disenfranchise these new voters \(Swales K., 2016, [pg. 19](https://whatukthinks.org/eu/wp-content/uploads/2016/12/NatCen_Brexplanations-report-FINAL-WEB2.pdf)\)
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.3.1. Con: These people can vote for a Brexit party at the next election if they want. They are not disenfranchised.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.3.2. Con: That these people have never voted for or against a representative suggests perhaps that they do not understand the principles of representative democracy, in which case they clearly did not understand the nature of the referendum which was consultative, irrespective of what David Cameron \(UK Prime Minister during the referendum campaign\) might [claim](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2016/jun/23/eu-referendum-legally-binding-brexit-lisbon-cameron-sovereign-parliament).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.4. Con: Data suggests there is popular [support](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2018/jan/26/britons-favour-second-referendum-brexit-icm-poll) for a second referendum once the Brexit terms are revealed.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.4.1. Pro: In 2015, Vote Leave director Dominic Cummings admitted before the referendum that defining Brexit would lose votes and that even Eurosceptic MPs could not agree on what Brexit should entail. He [proposed](https://dominiccummings.com/2015/06/23/on-the-referendum-6-exit-plans-and-a-second-referendum/) a referendum on the final deal himself to gain public consensus.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.4.2. Pro: A second referendum or a Brexit-focused vote, specifically on the deal the UK ends up with, possibly with an option to stay, would not necessarily undermine the will of the people. It may in fact strengthen it. It can be explained as a further exercise of democracy, and if so explained, not undermine citizens' belief in democracy at all.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.4.3. Pro: Even Nigel Farage has come out in [support](http://www.telegraph.co.uk/politics/2018/01/11/nigel-farage-suggests-uk-may-need-second-brexit-referendum-settle/) of a second referendum.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.5. Con: Part of the argument for leaving is that Parliament should be sovereign. Parliament is a democratically elected body. If Parliament decides that hard Brexit is not in the best interests of the UK, then this would be a democratic action.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.5.1. Con: This would be an undemocratic decision as parliament would not be representative of the people's vote in the referendum.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.5.1.1. Con: Our democracy is based on representation, and the duty of MPs is to [vote with their conscience](https://twitter.com/BBCPolitics/status/1108699600016297985?s=19).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.6. Con: Only a minority of those who were eligible to vote [voted](https://www.indy100.com/article/brexit-leave-remain-52-48-per-cent-voter-turnout-electoral-register-charts-7399226) to leave.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.6.1. Con: There is no reason to believe that those who did not vote would have voted in a different proportion to those who did vote.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.6.2. Con: A much smaller minority of those who were eligible to vote voted to stay.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.6.2.1. Con: A bit under two percent is not much smaller.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.6.2.1.1. Con: 1.3 million more people is much bigger.  This is about equivalent to the entire population of Estonia, and bigger than the entire populations of [3 other](http://www.worldometers.info/population/countries-in-the-eu-by-population/) EU states.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.6.3. Pro: The British 'public' did not vote Leave. The British 'public' far outnumber those in The electorate who voted Leave, but are still profoundly affected by the government's decision to do so. Leave only achieved 3.78% majority of the electorate, not the public.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.6.4. Con: This is juggling with numbers. The rules of the referendum were clear. With the amount of media attention the referendum got there was no way that anyone could not know about this event and its rules.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7. Con: Many people were denied a vote in the referendum; this was profoundly undemocratic.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.1. Con: Democracy does not mean that every man and his dog are eligible to vote. Rules and regulations on voting eligibility are necessary in a democracy to prevent abuse.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.1.1. Con: Rules disqualifying the people most affected by the decision from voting in it were not necessary to prevent abuse. They were, instead, effectively a form of voter suppression - forcing the people most hurt by the decision to have no say in it. Nor is there any evidence that restricting voting eligibility is 'necessary to prevent abuse' - on the contrary, the most common form of abuse in some democracies is abusive rules intended to prevent voting.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.2. Pro: Approximately 1.5 million young people \(16-17\), who polled overwhelmingly in favour of staying in the EU, were [not allowed](http://www.independent.co.uk/student/news/eu-referendum-uk-result-students-votes-at-16-remain-brexit-leave-a7101821.html) to vote.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.2.1. Con: Under 18’s can also not vote in UK General Elections, Local Elections, or European elections so it is disingenuous to say they were ‘not allowed’ implying that this is exceptional.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.2.2. Con: Even if those 16 or over could vote, an overwhelming majority would have to vote remain and not abstain in order to overrule leave votes. 16-17 year olds made up [Roughly 1,428,000](https://www.ons.gov.uk/employmentandlabourmarket/peopleinwork/employmentandemployeetypes/timeseries/jn5p/lms) of the voting population at the time. [89% of Scottish 16-17 year olds](https://www.electoral-reform.org.uk/wp-content/uploads/2017/06/Votes-at-16-briefing.pdf) registered to vote in the Scottish referendum, and [84.6% of those actually voted](https://www.electoralcommission.org.uk/__data/assets/pdf_file/0005/179807/Scottish-referendum-Public-Opinion-survey-ICM-Report-WEBSITE.pdf), which totals 75.3% of 16-17 year olds. Assuming similar proportions in the EU vote, only 1,075,198 votes would have been cast. This is not enough to change the outcome.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.3. Pro: More than 1 million Britons, who had been living in Europe for over 15 years, were [denied a vote](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2017/apr/21/britons-abroad-denied-uk-general-election-vote-tories) in the referendum.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.4. Pro: Approximately 1.5 million Europeans working in the UK were [denied a vote](https://www.indy100.com/article/heres-a-list-of-all-the-people-who-can-vote-in-the-eu-referendum-and-those-who-cant--x1ZGMHAFWW).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.5. Pro: Nationals of most EU states, who pay tax and can vote in some other elections, were [not allowed](https://theconversation.com/who-can-vote-in-the-eu-referendum-57694) to vote in the EU referendum. They should have been allowed to vote.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.5.1. Con: Paying taxes is not a prerequisite for voting eligibility. If it were then very poor people, for example, may be excluded. It is not unreasonable to limit voting on matters of state to people who have fealty to said state.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.5.2. Con: Everyone who is eligible to vote in UK general elections was eligible to vote in the referendum.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.5.3. Con: Britain is actually much more liberal on voting rights than many other countries, and so by international standards EU citizens should NOT have been allowed to vote. For example, only Irish citizens can vote in Irish referendums. Germany does not allow [any non-German EU citizens to vote](https://www.dw.com/en/german-election-ballot-box-closed-to-78-million-residents/a-40359141) in general elections, even if resident in Germany for decades.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.5.4. Con: Every visitor to the UK who buys something pays tax in the form of VAT. Paying UK taxes does not entitle nationals of 3rd countries to vote.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.8. Con: The referendum was [advisory only](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-vote-eu-referendum-not-legally-binding-supreme-court-legal-challenge-article-50-a7418706.html). It fundamentally undermines UK democracy to change the nature of the vote from advisory to binding after the results are in.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.8.1. Con: Parliament voted to implement the decision by triggering Art. 50 by an [overwhelming majority](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-38833883).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.9. Con: Implementing a Brexit that does not meet the promises made by Leave politicians or fulfill the expections of Brexiteers will undermine the UK citizens faith in democracy.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.10. Pro: The turnout for the Brexit Referendum was higher than that of any [general election](http://www.ukpolitical.info/Turnout45.htm) since 1997 \([at 72.2%](http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/politics/eu_referendum/results)\)
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.10.1. Con: Only 27% of UK residents [voted](https://www.indy100.com/article/brexit-leave-remain-52-48-per-cent-voter-turnout-electoral-register-charts-7399226) to leave while millions were [denied](https://www.indy100.com/article/heres-a-list-of-all-the-people-who-can-vote-in-the-eu-referendum-and-those-who-cant--x1ZGMHAFWW) a vote in the referendum.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.10.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.10.1.2. Con: Even though this is true, even fewer people voted to remain. In a binary vote, anyone who chooses to abstain from the vote submits themselves to the result. You absolutely cannot protest a vote of this nature by means of abstaining. There is no third option.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.10.1.3. Con: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.7.2.2.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.10.2. Con: General elections since 1997 are a poor basis for comparision. The circumstances and procedure were significantly different between the two, so it is inappropriate to compare the two turnout rates.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.10.2.1. Pro: Selecting a limited sample of voter turnout misrepresents turnout in general. Low voter turnout in general elections is a recent and potentially temporary phenomenon. [Voter turnout did not drop below 70% from 1922 to 1997 and averaged over 75% from 1945 to 1997.](https://www.tutor2u.net/politics/reference/turnout)
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.10.2.2. Pro: Recent general elections have suffered from voter apathy caused by a lack of clear choice, which encourages turnout to be low.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.10.2.3. Pro: The Scottish Independence Referendum with a comparable question and situtation had a considerably higher turnout of [84.6%](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2014_Scottish_independence_referendum).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.11. Pro: Whatever the eventual consequences of a hard Brexit would be, preserving the basic social contract that gives legitimacy to a modern democracy is almost certainly more important.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.11.1. Con: The idea of a 'social contract' is \(mostly\) that citizens have surrendered decision-making power to the government, in [exchange for a protection of their rights and security.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Social_contract) Accepting the results of non-binding referenda \(with a very narrow majority\) as binding, without nuance, may undermine the objective of the social contract by not protecting the rights and security of a large portion of those citizens.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.2. Con: There was not a clear majority, only 51.89% voted Leave. There should have been at least 10% threshold to ensure a clear majority. Such a momentous change in national circumstance should not rest on such an insignificant majority.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.2.1. Con: A majority is still a majority, and other elections and general elections don't have a 10% threshold, so the referendum should not either.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.2.1.1. Con: A referendum is not an election. In an election the stronger the majority the more powerful the government. The weaker the majority, the more a government needs to consider the wider appeal of its policies.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.2.1.1.1. Con: A government with a weak majority would have to be more careful to appeal to all factions within its ruling group.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.2.1.2. Con: Constitutional change often requires a [supermajority](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitutional_amendment).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.3. Pro: The fact that Brexit negotiations have been so difficult has further [convinced](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2017/jul/14/brexit-eu-referendum) many Leave voters that the EU is un-democratic and impinges on British sovereignty.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.3.1. Con: Negotiations have been so difficult because the UK government did not plan enough time before triggering Article 50 to begin those negotiations.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.3.2. Con: Negotiations have [mainly](https://www.economist.com/bagehots-notebook/2018/10/01/the-tory-party-is-split-down-the-middle) been [between](https://www.politico.eu/article/steve-baker-brexit-chequers-theresa-may-tory-party-faces-catastrophic-brexit-split-says-former-minister/) [competing](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/conservative-party-split-theresa-may-chequers-brexit-steve-baker-conference-a8530126.html) factions of the Tory party, rather than with the EU.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.3.3. Pro: -> See 1.4.3.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.3.4. Con: Increased conviction of Leavers to Leave can largely be attributed to [confirmation bias](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Confirmation_bias). Many Remainers view the failure of negiotations as increased evidence for remaining.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.4. Con: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.6.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5. Pro: The UK not leaving the EU would, to those who voted Leave, be a strong indication that their vote in the 2016 referendum did not carry any weight. This could result in reduced voter turnout as people think their votes don't matter.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.1. Con: The public has [lost faith](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/brexit-poll-uk-public-trust-government-political-parties-bored-hope-not-hate-a8751406.html) in the democratic process however the Brexit mess plays out.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.1.1. Pro: At least part of that is because the public conflate the referendum with British democracy. It has brought that into disrepute.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.1.1.1. Pro: The holding of referendums is not a conventional part of British Democracy: Parliament being sovereign and thus able to hold one does not contradict that [there is no pre-determined format or voting franchise for any such vote](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Referendums_in_the_United_Kingdom). British democracy is now, as it has been, elected officials using the superior knowledge available to them to make the hard choices. Parliament giving the suspect \(the EU\) to the lynch mob was irresponsible.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.1.2. Con: Brexit is no more nor less than a total breakdown in faith in the democratic process. Whatever reasons people had for voting to leave, they all revolve in a loss of that faith: whether it is faith in the democratic processes of the EU, in our government's ability to realise our proper place in it, or voting leave because they have simply lost faith in our political system and see this as a chance to express that opinion.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.2. Con: The 2016 referendum was advisory and as such did not carry any legal weight. That this point wasn't made clear and indeed promises were made that couldn't be kept that have since been exploited by populists, is unfortunate but we live in a democracy and citizens have responsibilities as well as rights, they should understand the political process and that any brexit outcome does have any bearing on the importance of their vote matters in legally constituted elections.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.2.1. Pro: If we have to follow through and do everything that is promised by people who don't have the authority to promise it, we'll end up in a right old mess.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.2.2. Con: Regardless of any moral obligation to understand, many won't.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.2.2.1. Pro: The lack of understanding is in some part due to [politicians](https://youtu.be/3AwKLOLB4Qg) and [newsmedia](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enemies_of_the_People_\(headline\)) failing to accurately explain.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.2.3. Pro: Referenda are [not legally binding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Referendums_in_the_United_Kingdom) in the United Kingdom, as it is recognised that Parliament has sovereign authority.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.2.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.8.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.2.3.2. Pro: The EU referendum was not a General Election. There is no legal precedent on implementing the "democratic will" of the referendum before the Government defines the details of its interpretation of Brexit. A Government interpretation of what Brexit means is not the "will of the people".
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.2.3.3. Con: British constitutional law is not codified and operates partially through [conventions or customs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constitutional_convention_\(political_custom\)#United_Kingdom). Respecting the outcome of a referendum is an important convention, even if not codified.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.2.3.3.1. Con: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.1.1.1.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.2.3.3.2. Pro: In 1890, one of the foremost constitutional theorists, A.V. Dicey [noted](http://www.keepcalmtalklaw.co.uk/constitution--convention-whats-stopping-a-second-brexit-referendum/) that a referendum ought to be an authoritative judgment on matters of constitutional importance, and therefore accepting its result should be a powerful convention.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.2.3.3.2.1. Con: [Constitutional changes conventionally](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supermajority) require a supermajority.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.2.3.4. Pro: The referendum was legally advisory, and no supermajority was therefore required. It has only become politically binding during the referendum campaign, but this is not something that Parliament could vote for or against. If it was legally binding, Parliament might have [insisted on a supermajority](https://publications.parliament.uk/pa/ld201012/ldselect/ldconst/177/17706.htm).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.5.3. Con: Their votes have been valued, parliament has spent the last 3 years talking about very little else but how to enact brexit. There's been a strangely divisive approach to turning a terribly vague, ill-defined position that people voted for into a terribly unpopular and damaging set of options. This is how democracy works.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.6. Pro: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.1.10.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.7. Pro: Because of how [divided](http://www.bbc.co.uk/news/av/uk-36638528/brexit-just-how-divided-is-britain) the country is, reversing the referendum would require a level of nationwide consensus building that is unprecedented in modern history.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.7.1. Con: Unprecedented things can and do happen. That's where precedents come from.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.7.2. Con: The referendum result itself was a large factor in dividing the country - Scotland and Northern Ireland both voted to Remain \(both by a significant margin - 62% and 55.8% respectively\), but the overall result was marginal towards leave due to a small lead in England \(53.4%\) and Wales \(52.5%\).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.7.2.1. Con: Reversing the referendum without an electoral mandate may well heighten, not soften, the bitterness of that divide.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.7.3. 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.11.1.1.2.1.1. Con: A person's age is not the determining factor in deciding voting preferences; the [generation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Generation#Western_world) one grew up is important as well.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.11.1.1.2.1.1.1. Pro: The current older generation may vote conservative due to lower education levels, which will not be the case when current young generation becomes old. Younger generations are more likely to have been to university \([35% of 30-year-olds have a degree, compared with 10% of 70-year-olds](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2015/nov/03/do-we-become-more-conservative-with-age-young-old-politics)\), and [higher education tends to make people more socially liberal](https://theconversation.com/hard-evidence-do-we-become-more-conservative-with-age-47910). As the current young generation ages, they will therefore not be educationally equivalent/follow the same voting patterns as the current old.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.11.1.1.2.1.1.2. Con: The effect of aging on party affiliation as distinct from generational factors has been [known for some time](https://academic.oup.com/poq/article-abstract/26/4/648/1868747?redirectedFrom=PDF).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.11.1.1.2.1.1.3. Pro: "It has not always been the case that younger generations are more Left-wing", according to a United States PEW Research [study](http://www.people-press.org/2015/04/30/a-different-look-at-generations-and-partisanship/) showing that whilst in 2014 age correlated with right-wing voting, the opposite was true in 1994.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.11.1.1.2.1.1.4. Pro: Studies suggest that the events that happen fairly early on in life set basic outlooks and orientations that affect how people vote for the rest of their lives. 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.11.1.1.2.1.2. Pro: A long term study tracked voting choices of several thousand people over their lifetime, and found a [0.32% - 0.38%](https://www.sciencedirect.com/science/article/pii/S0261379413000875) increase in Conservative voters per year.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.11.1.1.2.2. Pro: Older people [were more likely to vote for Brexit](https://lordashcroftpolls.com/2016/06/how-the-united-kingdom-voted-and-why/), and if it is true that people become more likely to support Brexit as they age then the academics' predictions will be inaccurate.

1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.11.1.1.2.4.1. Con: While life-experience increases with age, people's [IQ start decreasing by the age of 60](https://medium.com/psyc-406-2015/how-fast-does-iq-decline-can-you-do-anything-about-it-f5ca370d8b62), hampering good judgement and application of their knowledge.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.11.1.1.2.4.1.1. Con: The decrease in some measures of IQ happens after people tend to become fixed in their political opinions. Therefore the opinions of older generations are representative of the peak of their raw IQ-based capabilities.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.11.1.1.2.4.2. Pro: A university of Texas study found that [wisdom increases with age, especially for longer term strategic decisions](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/health/elder/8720182/Wisdom-comes-with-age-study-shows.html) which took future stages into account. They found that younger adults were better when only the immediate rewards needed to be considered. They suggested a biological cause of younger adults using their still strong ventral striatum, compared to older people who are forced to compensate by using their prefrontal cortices as their ventral striatum degrades.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.11.1.2. 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.11.1.2.2. Con: This poll seeks to analyse all 600+ constituencies on the basis of a survey of just 15,000 people. This means an average sample size of just 25 people per constituency. The mean constituency size is [~70,000](https://www.parliament.uk/about/how/elections-and-voting/constituencies/#jump-link-0), which means the margin of error for each constituency of [20%](http://www.comresglobal.com/our-work/margin-of-error-calculator/). Given the difference between Leave and Remain is far less than this in most constituencies, the headline result of this polling data of 100 constituencies switching is, statistically speaking, invalid.

1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.12.2.5.5. Con: There is a big difference between lies and predictions. Lies were told to intentionally mislead the electorate in able to win votes. It could be argued that predictions were based on us leaving the EU and the Single Market. Neither has occurred yet.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.12.2.5.6. Con: A new government study shows that the UK will be [worse off](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-46366162) under Brexit in any form than staying in the EU.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.12.2.5.7. Con: The UK has not left the EU yet \(as of 12th Feb 2018\).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.12.2.5.8. Con: The effects of leaving will only materialise when we leave. This has not happened yet therefore the assertion that lies were told on both sides is incorrect
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.12.2.6. Pro: -> See 1.6.9.4.7.8.4.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13. Con: Even though the UK voted to leave the EU, it is unclear what the democratic will is with regards to how this is done.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.1. Con: When the British people decided by referendum on 23 June 2016 to leave the EU, the decision was not conditional on "hard" or "easy" Brexit.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.1.1. Con: The British people did not *decide* to leave the EU, they merely suggested it, in the certain knowledge that Parliament has a clear duty to consider all the relevant facts and then make the decision themselves...
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.1.2. Con: This very statement accepts and proves that what is currently on offer / happening in the Brexit process could not have been foreseen by the general public, and as such should be put to another vote.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.1.3. Con: The option for what kind of Brexit people wanted was not given as an option on the ballot. Neither was the option for "Leave the EU, the single market, Euroatom and the Bill of human rights" but that all seems to have been inferred.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2. Pro: The "instructions" from the electorate were far from specific, that is the central problem the UK finds itself dealing with - there is and never was any clear understanding of what version of leave to pursue.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1. Pro: The WTO governed trade relationship with the EU in the event of no deal would not represent an outcome that the electorate voted for.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.1. Pro: Vote Leave - [the official Brexit campaign](https://www.huffingtonpost.co.uk/entry/vote-leave-eu-referendum_uk_570e5074e4b00ed33e06b5f4) - clearly stated that there would be a deal.  It is clear from the second paragraph of[the "About The Campaign" section of their website](http://www.voteleavetakecontrol.org/campaign.html), and from page 10 of the Vote Leave ["Why Vote Leave" document.](http://view.publitas.com/15010/164151/pdfs/9b3d7fac7290257c2e72c7784537315b9351806e.pdf).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.1.1. Con: It is [predicted](https://www.conservativehome.com/video/2017/11/watch-ici-londres-britain-has-nothing-to-fear-from-trading-with-the-eu-on-wto-terms.html) that trading on WTO terms will, as a worst case scenario,  result in a less than a 2% decrease in trade with the EU which is 0.25% of GDP.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.2. Pro: Though it is not as explicit as Vote Leave, the other prominent Brexit campaign group - Leave.EU - clearly states [their vision](https://leave.eu/about/) that includes some kind of continued relationship with the EU, and explicitly lists Norway-plus and Canada models as options.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.3. Pro: Vote Leave said "we will [negotiate the terms of a new deal before we start any legal process to leave](http://view.publitas.com/15010/164151/pdfs/9b3d7fac7290257c2e72c7784537315b9351806e.pdf)".
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.3.1. Pro: The full quote is "Taking back control is a careful change, not a sudden step – we will negotiate the terms of a new deal before we start any legal process to leave"
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4. Con: [Many](https://whatukthinks.org/eu/questions/do-you-support-or-oppose-the-conservative-policy-of-leaving-the-eu-without-a-deal-if-the-terms-are-unacceptable/) people voted for the Conservatives under the [assumption](https://euobserver.com/brexit/137962) that they would leave the EU even if it meant leaving without a deal.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.1. Con: [52 percent](https://www.bbc.com/news/election/2017/results) of voters [chose](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-39665835) parties who are [against](https://www.independent.co.uk/voices/brexit-hard-leave-voters-labour-tory-mp-lies-boris-johnson-david-davis-jacob-rees-mogg-a8393841.html) a no-deal Brexit.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.1.1. Pro: Labour, with [40%](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/election/2017/results) of the vote, was against a no-deal brexit.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.1.1.1. Pro: Keir Starmer, the shadow secretary of state for exiting the European Union, stated the Labour Party position on a no- deal Brexit, in a speech in April "[A Labour approach means rejecting no deal as a viable option](https://fullfact.org/europe/whats-labour-policy-no-deal-brexit/)"
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.1.1.2. Pro: Labours manifesto said "Labour recognises that leaving the EU with ‘no deal’ is the worst possible deal for Britain and that it would do damage to our economy and trade. [We will reject ‘no deal’ as a viable option](https://brexitcentral.com/labours-manifesto-says-brexit/) and if needs be negotiate transitional arrangements to avoid a ‘cliff-edge’ for the UK economy."
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.1.2. Pro: The Liberal Democrats \(7.4%\), SNP \(3%\), and the Greens \(1.6%\) are [all strongly against](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-39665835) Brexit, including a No Deal.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.1.3. Con: The 2017 election result cannot be taken as an effective endorsement of no deal, since it was a [general election](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_election) on a range of wider issues.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.1.3.1. Pro: Two major terrorist attacks took place during the election campaign, with parties [arguing about](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2017_United_Kingdom_general_election#Security) the best way to prevent such events. This discussion would inevitably have effected some people's votes.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.1.3.2. Pro: Social care became a major election issue after the Conservative Party's manifesto [included](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2017_United_Kingdom_general_election#Social_care) new proposals, which were subsequently altered after criticism.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.1.3.3. Pro: Polling shows that [only 21%](https://yougov.co.uk/topics/politics/articles-reports/2017/07/11/why-people-voted-labour-or-conservative-2017-gener) consider Brexit the main reason they voted for their Conservative MP.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.1.3.4. Pro: Polling shows that [65% of labour voters](https://yougov.co.uk/topics/politics/articles-reports/2017/07/11/why-people-voted-labour-or-conservative-2017-gener) main reason for voting was not Brexit related. This includes being Anti-Tory, liking Corbyn, and the NHS.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.2. Con: The Tories lost their majority in the election.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.3. Con: The [manifesto](https://www.conservatives.com/manifesto) says "Theresa May's Conservatives will deliver the best possible deal for Britain as we leave the European Union delivered by a smooth, orderly brexit". It goes on to say "the government believes that no deal is better than a bad deal but we will enter the negotiations in a spirit of sincere cooperation and committed to getting the best deal for Britain." It is clear and indeed [openly referred to](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-44556764) that no deal was purely for negotiation purposes.

1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.8. Pro: The Brexit Party [supports](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brexit_Party) a no deal Brexit, and is expected to gain over [30%](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/uk-politics-48227459) of votes in the 2019 European elections. Many of these are [former Conservative voters](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/may/18/opinium-poll-conservatives-trounced-by-brexit-party-remainers-abandon-labour), suggesting there has always been support amongst the Conservative base for a no-deal Brexit.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.8.1. Con: As there [was no support](https://youtu.be/0xGt3QmRSZY) for a No Deal Brexit before the referendum, it rather suggests that the Brexit party has exploited public dissatisfaction with the handling of the Brexit process to push for an extremist position that simply wasn't part of the discussion before the referendum.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.4.9. Pro: There has been a core group of Tory MPs that [supported](https://www.politico.eu/article/brexit-8-tory-tribes-conservative-party/) a no-deal Brexit during the 2017 general election. These roughly [60 MPs](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/2016/11/19/heavyweight-brexiteers-go-public-as-60-tory-mps-demand-clean-bre/) were calling upon Theresa May to pursue a Hard Brexit with the EU. The fact that they got elected clearly illustrates that there are a lot of Conservatives who support a no-deal Brexit.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.5. Con: WTO governed trade allows beneficial trade deals to be struck and therefore is what the electorate voted for.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.6. Con: WTO governed trade does not preclude a good trade deal with the EU, which definitely represents the desires of the majority.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.7. Pro: Only about [35% of Leave voters](https://www.markpack.org.uk/154126/leave-voters-expected-britain-stay-single-market/) thought leaving meant leaving the single market, as would happen with no deal.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.8. Pro: Even [Nigel Farage](https://twitter.com/Femi_Sorry/status/1118817963874881536?s=09) advocated a deal on the same basis as Norway.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.9. Pro: The electorate knew what they were voting for and what they were voting for was the [deal](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Ldvt0O4ekV0) that the Prime Minister has come back with. If parliament does not find that deal acceptable, then they must deem that the electorate did not know what they were voting for.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10. Con: The public has shown more support for a No-Deal than for remaining.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.1. Con: [600 constituencies support Remain](https://twitter.com/Channel4News/status/1114231615838916614), 2 support a deal, and 30 support no-deal, according to a December 2018 YouGov poll.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.2. Pro: 46% of adults are confident that the UK will [ultimately be OK, after brief uncertainty, if it leaves the EU without a deal](https://webcache.googleusercontent.com/search?q=cache:xVTypcjSb68J:https://www.telegraph.co.uk/politics/2019/03/19/no-deal-better-brexit-delay-say-public-poll-finds-just-one-10/+&cd=1&hl=en&ct=clnk&gl=de), according to a March 2019 poll by the Telegraph.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.2.1. Pro: If the UK leaves the EU, Brexit MEP Lucy Harris believes it "will" rebound economically in about [30 years](https://www.indy100.com/article/brexit-party-eu-elections-lucy-harris-economy-30-years-8919786).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.2.1.1. Con: Many of those who voted in the referendum will be dead in 30 years time.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.2.1.2. Con: There is [some uncertainty](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/shortcuts/2018/jul/24/two-50-or-100-years-when-do-leavers-think-brexit-will-pay-off) regarding just how long it will take for the UK to make it back. Some Brexiteers think it will take 50 years to take advantage of Brexit, while others think it may be even longer than that.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.2.2. Con: “Ultimately” is too vague a term. There’s no way to tell what time frame respondents had in mind: Would the average be 5 years, 25 years, 125 years? We have no way of knowing so the statistic is meaningless.

1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.2.4. Con: The Telegraph does not show it's methodology. As a newspaper with a [political bias for Leave](http://www.ox.ac.uk/news/2016-05-23-uk-newspapers-positions-brexit), there is a strong possibility of bias or intentional misleading.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.3. Pro: When asked "In the event of Parliament voting down the Brexit deal that the Government negotiates with the European Union, what do you think should happen next?" No Deal has [consistently been the top choice](https://whatukthinks.org/eu/questions/in-the-event-of-parliament-voting-down-the-brexit-deal-that-the-government-negotiates-with-the-european-union-what-do-you-think-should-happen-next/) in a series of 9 polls \(2018/2019\).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.3.1. Con: The cited article shows that no-deal has consistently been supported by a plurality of fewer than 30% of the responders.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.3.2. Con: This poll has several similar options which have split the vote of people who are against a no deal. If you combine the two "second vote" options, it easily overtakes the "no deal" option.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.3.2.1. Con: One of the "second vote" options is still between two variants on leave, without allowing for remaining.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.3.2.1.1. Con: That is consistently the less popularly supported of the two, enjoying approximately 10% support. Even summing it and no deal does not reach a simple majority.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.4. Pro: [45% of the public agreed](https://www.comresglobal.com/wp-content/uploads/2019/04/Leave-Means-Leave-April-Snap-Poll-Tables-Final-Published.pdf#page=11) when asked if we should leave and trade on “WTO rules” instead of delaying Brexit, with only 34% disagreeing, according to a Comres poll in April 2019.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.4.1. Con: The polls methodology [suffers from acquiescence bias](http://ukpollingreport.co.uk/blog/archives/10054).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.4.1.1. Pro: When asked if you agree or disagree with something, you’re more likely to agree with the statement. The 45% could have been boosted by people answering “agree” when they may not have, had the question been worded more neutrally.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5. Pro: When given the choice of leaving with no deal or remaining, no deal is the most popular choice.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.1. Pro: [76%](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2019/jan/04/most-tory-members-would-choose-no-deal-over-may-brexit-plan) of conservative members party members chose 'No Deal' over remain in a ESRC survey.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.1.1. Con: Conservative voters are a minority of the UK population.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.1.2. Con: Conservative party members are [not representative](https://www.psa.ac.uk/insight-plus/members-only-views-conservative-party%E2%80%99s-rank-and-file) of the wider population.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2. Pro: YouGov polling in April 2019 found that [44% to 42% of Britons would prefer to leave with no deal](https://yougov.co.uk/topics/politics/articles-reports/2019/04/04/what-do-public-think-about-no-deal-brexit) if the alternative is remaining.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2.1. Con: This poll did not include Northern Ireland, which is more pro Remain than Great Britain.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2.1.1. Pro: [55.8%](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/politics/eu_referendum/results) of Northern Ireland voted remain, compared to only 48% in Great Britain.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2.1.2. Pro: By excluding a section of the electorate, this poll contributes towards [disinformation](https://en.m.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disinformation).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2.1.2.1. Con: Excluding Northern Ireland is the case with a lot of political polling in the UK, largely because Northern Ireland’s party system is different from the rest of the nation. This is less a purposeful disinformation campaign, and more a limitation of pre-existing political polling structures.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2.1.2.2. Con: Disinformation is a deliberate act, but this poll openly stated where the results came from. Without attempting to hide or mislead, it is not disinformation.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2.2. Con: YouGov polls [consistently over-predict](https://d25d2506sfb94s.cloudfront.net/r/53/YouGov%20Record%20May%202016.pdf) the pro no-deal party UKIP's success \(7 out of 9 recent polls\). These errors may also manifest in other no-deal related predictions.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2.2.1. Con: UKIP were genuinely hard to predict, due to their volatility and over-representation in the media.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2.2.1.1. Pro: Potentially the largest election-time complaint to the BBC \(1190 complaints\) was that they [gave much coverage to UKIP](https://www.theguardian.com/media/2014/may/30/bbc-complaints-ukip-election-coverage-bias), or were biased in favour of the party.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2.2.1.2. Pro: UKIP have had a [turbulent rise and fall](https://qz.com/1002422/uk-election-the-rise-and-fall-of-ukip-the-party-that-birthed-brexit/), which makes them much harder to predict than mainstream parties. They became the first party in 100 years that was not the Tories or Labour to win a nationwide election in 2014, but are [virtually dead](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/ukip-local-elections-nigel-farage-gerard-batten-brexit-collapse-a8293406.html) in 2018 with 75% fewer candidates.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2.2.1.3. Pro: UKIP are over represented in the media because of over-optimistic polling.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2.2.2. Con: Other polling sources regularly predicted UKIP higher than YouGov did.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2.2.2.1. Pro: In the 2009 EU Elections, the 2014 EU Elections, and the 2015 General Elections [YouGov predicted UKIP lower \(or the lowest\) compared to other pollsters.](https://d25d2506sfb94s.cloudfront.net/r/53/YouGov%20Record%20May%202016.pdf).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.2.2.2.2. Con: In the London mayor elections of 2016, [YouGov predicted UKIP higher](https://d25d2506sfb94s.cloudfront.net/r/53/YouGov%20Record%20May%202016.pdf) than any other listed polls.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3. Con: If we want to know the answer to a different question to that asked in the referendum and be able to action it, then we need to formally ask that question in a referendum.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1. Con: Polls work well at finding public opinion, therefore the government can use them to make decisions they know the public support.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.1. Con: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.9.4.1.1.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.2. Pro: Looking at polls for the last week of campaigns for 220 national elections in 32 countries over the decades to 2017 – with data for a handful of countries stretching back to 1942 – [errors have held steady at about 2%](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2018/mar/12/polls-as-accurate-as-they-have-ever-been-study-says).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.3. Pro: A dataset of countries that had regular polling over several decades found that [polling errors have dropped over time](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2018/mar/12/polls-as-accurate-as-they-have-ever-been-study-says).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.4. Pro: Recent popular [opinion](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2018/apr/17/lords-tell-political-pollsters-to-get-house-in-order) that polls are getting worse are incorrect. There is [no evidence that](https://statfr.blogspot.com/2018/08/forecast-error-are-polls-getting-worse.html) poll errors have increased over time.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5. Con: The [bandwagon effect](https://www.google.com/amp/s/theconversation.com/amp/how-political-opinion-polls-affect-voter-behaviour-60554) influences the results of democratic elections.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.1. Pro: Selective publication of single data points from misleading polls is part of a right-wing [tactical gaming](https://www.indy100.com/article/nigel-farage-andrew-marr-video-interview-watch-brexit-party-bbc-8911301) of the [media](https://youtu.be/79txI6-RZks).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2. Con: The bandwagon effect would continue without opinion polls, but instead be based on inaccurate perceptions.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.1. Pro: Without opinion polls, voters will rely on their 'gut feeling' of how likely each party is to win. This is more likely to be incorrect as it is based on circumstantial or anecdotal evidence.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.1.1. Con: When politically motivated interest groups [commission polls](http://www.ncpp.org/?q=node/4), they are often not intended as a guide for voters to help them make correct political decisions, but are for [social and political commentators to sell news](https://documentcloud.adobe.com/link/track?uri=urn%3Aaaid%3Ascds%3AUS%3Af54b1e30-ebdb-4ea6-8ad4-09cf9112a1f1). They are [now being](https://fullfact.org/europe/telegraph-suspending-parliament/) [gamed by politicians](https://youtu.be/79txI6-RZks) to influence elections.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.1.2. Con: People's gut feeling about who they want to win cannot be incorrect. That, and knowledge of policies and track records, is precisely how people should make electoral choices, not on who is most likely to win.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.1.2.1. Con: Gut feelings about "who they want to win" and "who they think will win" are different. Only the latter affects the bandwagon effect.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.1.3. Con: People's gut feeling, being more directly related to their emotions, experience and political views, of who is likely to win is a better measure than a badly worded and/ or politically motivated and/or selective option opinion poll.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.2. Pro: Bandwagon/contagion voting is a part of the human thought process, and not caused by polling. Humans want to be on the winning side, and so the effect would continue even if polls were unavailable.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.2.1. Con: The deliberate staging of polls excluding certain options or demographics to demonstrate support for preferred options exploits this effect.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.2.1.1. Pro: Polls are easily abuseable, and are used to purposefully give false information in a package which many people will believe.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.2.1.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.1.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.2.1.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.1.1.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.3. Con: Constant repetitious polling amplifies the bandwagon effect by flooding media, notably [social media](https://twitter.com/LeaveEUOfficial/status/1128705404987498496?s=09), with bite-size [selective, unrepresentative data](https://europeelects.eu/2018/12/05/how-unpopular-is-french-president-macron-really/).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.3.1. Con: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.9.4.1.1.4.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.3.2. Con: This assumes the polls are inaccurate, which is generally untrue.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.3.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.2.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.3.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.3.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.3.3. Con: The problem is with fake news, not fake polls.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.3.3.1. Pro: Purposeful misinformation is an inherent part of modern society, be that [outright fake news spreading faster than real news](https://www.marketwatch.com/story/fake-news-spreads-more-quickly-on-twitter-than-real-news-2018-03-08) or the [mostly biased media](https://www.investors.com/politics/editorials/media-bias-left-study/) writing misleading or one-sided content.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.6. Con: Although polls give a good idea of public opinion, they also have an impact on public opinion due to the [bandwagon effect](https://www.google.com/amp/s/theconversation.com/amp/how-political-opinion-polls-affect-voter-behaviour-60554). Conducting an [increasing number of polls](https://www.brookings.edu/articles/can-we-trust-the-polls-it-all-depends/), sometimes of disproportionate selected groups of the electorate or on limited selective options, is therefore irresponsible.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.6.1. Con: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.1.5.2.

1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.2. Pro: -> See discussion #10250: Voters in the UK should have a final vote on the Brexit deal.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.3. Con: Referendums are generally a bad idea. They are the tools of dictators and demagogues, a point agreed by both Atlee and Thatcher.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.3.3.1. Con: -> See discussion #28455: All major political decisions should be made via public referendum.

1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.4. Con: In the [European elections](https://www.bbc.co.uk/news/topics/crjeqkdevwvt/the-uks-european-elections-2019) appoximately 40.4% of votes went to explicitly Pro-Remain parties \(LD, Green, SNP, Plaid, Change\) while only 34.9% went to explicit No-Deal parties \(Brexit, UKIP\). The remaining 23.2% went to parties currently trying to offer some form of compromise \(Labour, Conservative\).
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.4.1. Con: The turnout of 36.7% was fairly low, so the views expressed in these elections might not represent true support for the various options.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.4.2. Con: EU election results are an imperfect way to measure No-Deal/Remain sentiment, because it was a election on a wide range of issues.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.4.2.1. Pro: The Green Party may be seeing growth [due to](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/europe/european-election-result-greens-green-wave-spd-germany-a8931351.html) the current environmental catastrophe.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.5.4.2.2. Con: In the event of No-Deal, the MEPs elected would not even make it to Brussels. Thus for No-Deal supporting Brexiters there was no reason/incentive to vote for any party besides the Brexit Party.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.6. Con: The public does not support a no deal when all other options are kept on the table.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.6.1. Pro: In a February 2019 YouGov poll asking how good various outcome would be, the majority [preferred remaining or leaving with an alternative deal](https://yougov.co.uk/topics/politics/articles-reports/2019/03/01/where-we-stand-brexit), and also narrowly accepted leaving with Mays withdrawal bill over the No-deal brexit option.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.10.6.2. Pro: According to YouGov polling in April 2019 asking for peoples "Preferred Option" [only 26% of respondants chose No deal.](https://yougov.co.uk/topics/politics/articles-reports/2019/04/04/what-do-public-think-about-no-deal-brexit) 37% prefered remaining, and 23% prefered a deal.

1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.3. Pro: The referendum was badly drafted, because it did not distinguish between vastly different versions of Brexit. [Leave voters themselves do not agree which version they want.](https://d25d2506sfb94s.cloudfront.net/cumulus_uploads/document/glo3xaqerh/InternalResults_170327_AnthonyBrexitQs_W.pdf) If and when the Government eventually decides what Brexit it wants, the majority of the public may be against it.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.1.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.3.2. Con: This point confuses the role of the referendum - to decide whether to leave the EU - and the role of government, which is to negotiate arrangements with 3rd parties on our behalf.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.3.2.1. Con: The decision to leave the EU can only be made properly when the alternative to the EU is clear. While they are two different processes, one may choose to leave the EU in the hope of a particular alternative, but the actual alternative offered in the end may be much worse, at which point the initial decision to leave the EU is not legitimate.

1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.3.3.1. Pro: [Many people](https://yougov.co.uk/news/2017/03/29/attitudes-brexit-everything-we-know-so-far/) considered maintaining access to the single market an important priority in the negotiations.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.3.3.2. Pro: A roughly 50/50 split, as in this case, cannot be considered a consensus of the British people.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.3.3.3. If we leave the Single Market, the will of those particular Leave voters would be disrespected.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.3.3.3.1. Pro: One of those voters so disrespected would be Nigel [Farage](https://twitter.com/Femi_Sorry/status/1132305288357400578?s=09). Nigel Farage has, having told one story to win the referendum vote, changed his position to a far more extreme one and is pretending nothing has changed.
1.10.1.4.1.4.3.13.4.13.2.3.4. Pro: "Referendums should be held when people know exactly what they are getting... We should not ask people to vote on a blank sheet of paper and tell them to trust us to fill in the details afterwards" David Davis \(now Secretary of State for Leaving the EU\) [said](https://publications.parliament.uk/pa/cm200203/cmhansrd/vo021126/debtext/21126-17.htm) in 2002.
